Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An alley which is higher than twenty cubits should be reduced; Rebbi Jehudah says, this is not necessary<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 1:1:23" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.1.1.23"><i>Šabbat</i> 1, Note 142</a>. The status of an alley, a narrow public road bordered by walls of houses, essentially is that of a <i>karmelit.</i> Since such an alley is not a thoroughfare, it is customary that under certain circumstances the dwellers in houses opening on the alley can turn it into their common private domain. A prior condition is that the alley be a dead-end street; an alley open at both ends, while not a public domain in the technical sense, does not qualify directly. The second condition, mentioned only in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, is that the entrance to the alley must be well defined and immediately recognizable; this is done either by placing a horizontal beam over the entrance (קוֹרַה) or placing there a vertical lath (לֶחִי). The Mishnah presumes that a beam was used; the majority holds that a beam placed higher than 20 cubits is not noticed whereas R. Jehudah holds that it always is noticed.</i>. If it is wider than ten cubits it should be reduced<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The entrance to the alley should not be wider than 10 cubits.</i>; if there is a door-frame even though it is wider than ten cubits there is no need to reduce<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If there are two door-posts and a beam from one to the other, even if there are no doors, it is clear that the domain is visibly separated from the thoroughfare onto which it opens.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An alley which is higher than twenty,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The same text also is the introduction to Tractate <i>Sukkah</i>1:1 (<b>ס</b>).</i> Rebbi Yose said it without attribution, Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rav: The rabbis inferred it from the door of the Sanctuary, but Rebbi Jehudah inferred it from the door of the Hall<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbis hold that one does not realize that a beam is there to indicate a door if it is higher than 10 cubits since the entrance door to the Herodian Temple was 10 cubits. R. Jehudah disputes this since the inner door between the antechamber and the main room, visible only to the officiating priests, was larger. The biblical accounts of Solomon’s Temple (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 6:2-3" href="/I_Kings.6.2-3"><i>1K.</i> 6:2–3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 3:3-4" href="/II_Chronicles.3.3-4"><i>2Chr.</i> 3:3–4</a>) do not give measurements of the doors. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 2a" href="/Eruvin.2a">Babli 2a</a>.</i>. An argument from the door of the Hall is sufficient only up to forty cubits, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Middot 3:4" href="/Mishnah_Middot.3.4">Mishnah <i>Middot</i> 3:4</a>.</i>: “The door of the Hall was forty cubits high and twenty cubits wide.” Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, even forty or fifty cubits; Bar Qappara stated, even a hundred cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">He disputes any connection with Temple measurements since R. Jehudah rejects height limitations in general. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 2b" href="/Eruvin.2b">Babli 2b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abin said, Rebbi Jehudah follows his opinion and the rabbis follow their opinion, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:1:2-5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.1.2-5.3">Mishnah 9:5</a>.</i>, “and similarly one carries under panelled bridges on the Sabbath, the words of Rebbi Jehudah, but the Sages forbid it.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The bridge is supported by a structure of beams underneath with beams running lengthwise on both sides. R. Jehudah holds that the boundary of the space below the bridge is clearly indicated if there are virtual walls hanging down from the beams enclosing the space which therefore assumes the role of a house. The rabbis accept only real walls.</i>” As you are saying there, one considers the cross-beam<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μέλαθρον, τό. This is a substantial load-carrying beam used in construction.</i> as if it came down<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">As a virtual wall, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.3">Note 40</a>.</i> and closed, here also one considers the roof as if it came down and closed. This is the opinion of Rebbi Jehudah in the case of a <i>sukkah,</i> this is his opinion in the case of an alley. Also the opinion of the rabbis in the case of a <i>sukkah</i> is their opinion in the case of an alley. But they do not compare<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that the rabbis take 20 cubits as upper height limit for both the entrance to an alleyway and a <i>sukkah</i> is accidental since the rules in both cases diverge widely.</i>. There are items qualified for a <i>sukkah</i> which are disqualified for an alley and those qualified for an alley but disqualified for a <i>sukkah.</i> Double pointed stakes<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δίκρανον, τό.</i> are qualified for a <i>sukkah</i> but disqualified for an alley, and it was stated thus: If he brought four double pointed stakes and thatched over them it is qualified as a <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">One may make a <i>sukkah</i> by putting up four poles carrying a frame on which one thatches a roof on condition that the poles qualify as walls, i. e., each of them is at least 4 hand-breadths wide. Otherwise one would have to make makeshift walls between the poles as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.9.1">Mishnah 9</a>.</i> but disqualified for an alley<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The poles carrying the cross beam may not be much higher than the walls of the alley (or the cross beam cannot be fastened to the tops of the poles, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 9a" href="/Eruvin.9a">Babli 9a</a> top).</i>. That is, if they are higher by more than three [hand-breadths] than the walls of the alley. But if they are not higher by more than three [hand-breadths] than the walls of the alley it is qualified. If they are not, but if they are four [hand-breadths] wide they are qualified even if arbitrarily high<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">If a pole qualifies as a wall it is clear that it may be 20 cubits high for the rabbis and arbitrarily high for R. Jehudah.</i>. Walls may be qualified for a <i>sukkah</i> but disqualified for an alley, and it was stated thus: “Two regular and the third even one hand-breadth is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 1:13" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).1.13">Tosephta <i>Sukkah</i> 1:13</a>. A <i>sukkah</i> must be at least 10 hand-breadths high and the sides have to be at least 7 hand-breadths wide.</i>.” Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Two of four hand-breadths each and the third even one hand-breadth is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.9.1">Mishnah 9</a>, a space of slightly less that 3 hand-breadths is disregarded. Therefore a wall qualifies as being 7 hand-breadths wide if the actual material wall extends to slightly more than 4 hand-breadths.</i>. But for an alley only if it is closed in its four directions<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">It must have three actual walls and a symbolic door on the fourth side.</i>. Wider than ten cubits is qualified for a <i>sukkah</i> but disqualified for an alley<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> for the alley. There are no limitations on the surface area covered by a <i>sukkah.</i></i>. “If he drew a vine or squash.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This may refer either to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.4.1">Mishnah <i>Sukkah</i> 1:4</a> or to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 1:7" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).1.7">Tosephta <i>Eruvin</i> 1:7</a>; shortened to leave out all of the argument. The thatched roof of a <i>sukkah</i> must be vegetal material not connected to the ground. Therefore vine or squash are disqualified to be used for thatching. But a vine growing in an arch over the entrance of an alley is as good as a beam to mark the entrance.</i>” That means, up to an area of two <i>bet se’ah.</i> But more than an area of two <i>bet se’ah</i> it is a partition made for agriculture where one may transport only four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">A vine used to designate the entrance to an alley may be used only if the total area of the alley does not exceed 5’000 square cubits since this is the maximal size of farming or ranching corrals permitted for Sabbath use. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 23b" href="/Eruvin.23b">Babli 23b</a>.</i>. If its sunshine is more than its shadow it is disqualified as <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Sukkah</i> 1:1</a>. Since alleys usually have no roof, they are not expected to provide shade.</i> but qualified for an alley. A roofed <i>sukkah</i> is disqualified, a roofed alley is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The essence of a <i>sukkah</i> is its makeshift thatched roof; it is not a <i>sukkah</i> if it has a waterproof roof. But if the entrance to an alley passes under a permanent roof at least 4 hand-breadths wide it does not need any other sign at the entrance. If the alley is open at both ends and one end is roofed, it is considered a dead-end alley. Cf. S. Liebermann, <i>Tosephta ki-Fshutah</i> part 3, p. 309, on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 1:7" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).1.7">Tosephta 1:7</a>.</i>. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rav Oshaia:<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to decide between this reading, quoting Babylonians, and <b>ס</b> “R. Aḥa in the name of R. Hoshaia”, quoting Galileans. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 3a" href="/Eruvin.3a">Babli 3a</a> the rule is given in the name of other Babylonian Amoraim.</i> Not only roofed, but if he put there a cross beam four hand-breadths wide, it makes the alley permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">A cross-beam 4 hand-breadths wide is a roof and not subject to any restrictions imposed on beams or laths in order to permit carrying in an alley.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The text is copied by Nachmanides in his <i>Novellae</i> to <i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 4 (col. 596–597 in the edition Jerusalem 1973); a parallel for the entire paragraph is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 46a" href="/Eruvin.46a">Babli 46a</a>. A defective copy (by the corrector) also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.2.2">Chapter 6, Note 33</a>.</i> Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥinena in the name of Cahana: Practice does [not]<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe wrote “not” and then [erroneously] crossed it out. Therefore it is missing in the printed editions.</i> follow Rebbi Jehudah. If it were not so what would we say? Rebbi Jehudah versus the Sages, practice should follow Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">It should not be necessary to mention that practice does not follow the lone dissenter.</i>? But since Rebbi Jacob bar Idi said in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, practice follows the lenient opinion in <i>Eruvin,</i> and Rebbi Naḥman bar Isaac<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Read with the later text and the quote in Nachmanides: R. Isaac bar Naḥman.</i> said in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, practice follows Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.5.1">Mishnah 4:5</a>, about a person who fell asleep in an uninhabited area on Friday afternoon and was sleeping during twilight, the Sages hold that since he did not consciously determine his place of rest at sundown he only can move inside 4 cubits from the place where he slept, but R. Joḥanan ben Nuri lets him move 2’000 cubits in every direction.</i>. (We asked about it, did not Rebbi Jacob bar Idi say in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, practice follows the lenient opinion in <i>Eruvin,</i> and Rav Naḥman bar Isaac said in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, practice follows Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri.)<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is duplicated afterwards; it is not in Nachmanides.</i> We asked about it, we would think this<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">That one follows the lenient opinion.</i> refers to an individual versus an individual but not an individual versus the Sages. There came Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Jacob bar Idi said in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, practice follows Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri even though the Sages disagree with him. Why should practice not follow Rebbi Jehudah even though the Sages disagree with him<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">If practice follows R. Joḥanan ben Nuri as a matter of principle, it also should follow R. Jehudah in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.1">Mishnah 1:1</a>.</i>? Samuel said, practice follows the lenient opinion in <i>Eruvin.</i> Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi said to him, for example what we have stated there, “and similarly one carries under panelled bridges on the Sabbath, the words of Rebbi Jehudah, but the Sages forbid it<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The bridge is supported by a structure of beams underneath with beams running lengthwise on both sides. R. Jehudah holds that the boundary of the space below the bridge is clearly indicated if there are virtual walls hanging down from the beams enclosing the space which therefore assumes the role of a house. The rabbis accept only real walls.</i>”? He told him, did I not say <i>Eruvin</i> but partitions<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The case of a bridge is not a case of <i>turning</i> a common domain into private domain but whether the space under a bridge automatically has the status of a permanent dwelling which does not need any <i>eruv.</i></i>? Cahana shares Samuel’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">He agrees with Samuel not only that in matters of <i>eruv</i> one follows the lenient opinion but also that the principle does not apply to questions of partitions. In the matter of an alley he holds that turning it into a private domain needs two things, first the fixation of the entrance partition described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.1">Mishnah 1:1</a> to which the principle does not apply, and the collection of an <i>eruv</i> as described in Chapter 2, to which the principle does apply.</i>. Therefore it was necessary to say that practice does not follow Rebbi Jehudah.
They only said “higher than twenty”. Therefor at the end of twenty it is qualified. This follows Rav who said that the end of measurements is for leniency; Rebbi Joḥanan said that the end of measurements is for restriction<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">As a general principle, every rabbinic statement that a quantity <i>x</i> is qualified up to a limit <i>a</i> is read by Rav as “<i>x</i> less or equal to whereas R. Joḥanan reads it as “<i>x</i> strictly less than <i>a</i>”. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 3a" href="/Eruvin.3a">Babli, 3a</a> (last line), the position of Rav is represented by the Babylonian Rava, that of R. Joḥanan by the Galilean Ulla.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: For an alley whose beam is higher than twenty he puts another beam within twenty and this reduces to less than twenty<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 3b" href="/Eruvin.3b">Babli 3b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, on condition that the entire qualification beam be within twenty<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The upper rim of the beam must be less than 20 cubits from the ground.</i>. Rebbi Ḥilqiah said in the name of Rebbi Aḥa, even this comes following Rav, as Rav said that slaughter half by half is qualified, and why did they say that it is disqualified? Because of the visual impression<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Even though Rav said that the upper bound is qualified in theory, in practice he will agree with R. Joḥanan that to avoid errors one has to be restrictive. His example is the rule that ritual slaughter requires that as biblical requirement half of both windpipe and esophagus of the animal have to be cut in slaughter. Nevertheless as a practical rule, since 50–50 is not exactly determinable by visual inspection, one requires that clearly more than half be cut in one stroke; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 29a" href="/Chullin.29a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 29a</a>.</i>. For if you say that he has to put it within twenty, he would put it higher than twenty<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">It is possible to read מסטבה.</i>.
“It should be reduced.” How does one reduce? He makes a platform<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">It is possible to read מסטבה.</i> at the entrance of the alley and permits the alley. How much must it be? Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: Four hand-breadths for an alley<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is difficult. Rashba <i>(Novellae</i> to <i>Eruvin,</i> first paragraph) reads: כדי מקים “as a space”, an expression also used in the next paragraph. An area of four by four hand-breadths is a space for itself; cf. Mishnah Šabbat 11:3. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 10a" href="/Eruvin.10a">Babli 10a</a>, top.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, one hand-breadth corresponding to the beam<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah only requires that the beam not be higher than 20 cubits above ground, it is necessary to lift the ground only directly below the beam.</i>. The colleagues say, anything, since here is no longer a height of twenty cubits in the widths of a hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">This follows R. Joḥanan in the preceding paragraph and refers to the case that the beam is exactly 20 cubits above ground everywhere. Then lifting the ground by an arbitrarily small amount at an arbitrarily small area satisfies the requirement of the Mishnah.</i>.
If its height was less than ten hand-breadths one has to dig out. How much does he have to dig? Rebbi Aḥa said, four cubits as an alley<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Since 4 cubits is the maximum one is permitted to carry in the public domain, it is the minimum for an alley to require an <i>eruv.</i></i>. Rebbi Yose said, four hand-breadths as a space. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">For chronological reasons one has to read “Yasa” instead of “Yose”.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, he makes a ditch at the entrance of the alley, ten [hand-breadths] deep and four wide; this permits the alley from inside and out<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">From the outside the ditch hinders the access from the public domain; from the inside the ditch is as good as a fourth wall which would turn the alley into a courtyard.</i>, on condition that it be within three [hand-breadths] of the walls of the alley<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Since a distance of less than 3 hand-breadths always is disregarded.</i>. And even he who says there four, here he agrees with three. There it is outside of the alley, but here within the alley<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The reference to 3 and 4 hand-breadths is to a text close to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta 1:6</a>, a case where there is not one beam crossing from one wall to the one on the other side of the alley but short beams sticking out from either wall. If the distance between these beams is less than 3 hand-breadths for the rabbis and 4 for Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, the beams are considered adjacent and qualify as one beam. It is noted that Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel also will agree that a ditch, which is not dug as a marker but to qualify for the minimal height of the alley walls, cannot be farther than 3 hand-breadths from a wall.</i>.
If he made a stone fence at the entrance to the alley, ten [hand-breadths] high and four wide, it permits the alley from inside and out<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">There had been a stone fence but it collapsed and now the heap is not 10 hand-breadths high on a basis 3 cubits wide; it does not qualify as a wall but is part of the alley. Cf. Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 100a.</i>. If there had been a stone fence, if it reduces to ten hand-breadths within three<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">It is not known where to find a relevant statement of R. Meїr.</i> it needs a beam, otherwise it does not need a beam. There are Tannaim who state, within four [cubits]. He who says, within three [cubits], the rabbis. He who says, within four [cubits], Rebbi Meїr<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">If the incline covers a height of at least 10 hand-breadths the dwellers in the two parts of the alley may make separate <i>eruvim</i> without additional beam or lath. Ravia §271 (ed. A. Aptowitzer p. 401 <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.5.1">Note 13</a>.)</i>. If there was a slope in the middle of the alley, for those at the high end it acts as a ditch, for those at the low end as a stone fence<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">A short pillar which reduces the width of the entrance to 10 cubits allows an <i>eruv</i> for the alley.</i>.
“If it is wider than ten cubits it should be reduced.” How does one reduce? He puts a cross-beam<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μέλαθρον, τό. This is a substantial load-carrying beam used in construction.</i> on the entrance of the alley and permits the alley<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">A short pillar which reduces the width of the entrance to 10 cubits allows an <i>eruv</i> for the alley.</i>. This was said for width; the rabbis also apply it to height<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">A pillar at the entrance of the alley is a distinctive sign which separates the alley from the public domain and permits an <i>eruv.</i></i>. Is it not as if opened in its length<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">If the entrance is too wide, what is the use of a vertical beam? The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 11a" href="/Eruvin.11a">Babli 11a</a> requires not only a vertical beam but a full door frame. It is impossible to read this into the Yerushalmi text.</i>? It is turned into a porch<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἐξέδρα, ἡ, a covered walkway. While this is a well-defined space, it has no walls. In order to treat it by the rules of an alley one has to hold that the beams which form the frame for the roofing (which may be either a full roof or simply lattice work) define virtual walls around the walkway.</i>. Rebbi permitted a porch in Bet Shearim. How many pillars did it have? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Rebbi Ḥiyya and Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">For chronological reasons one has to read “Yasa” instead of “Yose”.</i>, one said, six, but the other one said, eight. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, they do not disagree. He who said six does not count the two outer ones<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The two door posts with the beam connecting them which forms the entrance door frame and is necessary to define the space of the alley.</i>, he who said eight does count the two outer ones. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Six is not important, eight is not important. But if it was used by the public even with two it will be forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Since an alley can be permitted as private space only if it is used exclusively by the inhabitants of the houses opening into it and their guests, the same must hold for a virtual alley.</i>, if it is not used by the public even with many it will be permitted. The case which happened was as it happened.
If a porch was totally open to the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 94b" href="/Eruvin.94b">Babli 94b</a> calls this “a porch in an agricultural domain”. Since the porch has a roof it is a well-defined domain and should have the status of a house.</i>, Rebbi La: [Rav]<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The word was written by the scribe but erroneously deleted by the corrector, as shown by the parallel in <i>Sukkah</i> 1:1, 51d l. 13; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 94b" href="/Eruvin.94b">Babli <i>Eruvin</i> 94b</a>.</i> and Rebbi Joḥanan both are saying, one may carry in its entirety; Samuel said, one may carry in it only four cubits. Rebbi Yose said, does not Samuel agree that an alley roofed higher than twenty cubits is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Since in a house one does not require that the ceiling be lower than 20 cubits.</i>? But Samuel must hold, only if the qualifying beam be within twenty cubits. There came Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥinena in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia, only if the qualifying beam be within twenty cubits. Rebbi Yose said, even if Samuel would think that an alley roofed higher than twenty cubits is qualified, an alley is not comparable to a porch. An alley is made for the use of the adjacent houses, a porch is made for the use (of a thing)<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is difficult to understand, S. Liebermann proposes to read אבר “air” instead of דבר “thing”. The <i>exedra</i> is made so one may sit outside in the shade.</i>. If an alley is not roofed but there is a beam within twenty cubits, would it not be qualified? But if a porch is not roofed although there is a beam within twenty cubits, would that mean anything<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">A space delineated by four poles connected by horizontal beams is a private space only under very restricted circumstances, e. g., around a cistern or a well (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.1.1">Mishnah 2:1</a>).</i>?
If it was wide within fifteen cubits, Rebbi Abba and Rav Huna in the name of Rav: He makes a plank of slightly more than three hand-breadths and removes it two cubits from the wall<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">If the entrance to the alley is rather more than 10 cubits wide, up to 15 cubits one puts up a plank which divides the entrance into two parts. The main part is ten wide, the plank is chosen to be slightly wider than three cubits and the second opening is 2 cubits wide. Then since the width of the opening is less the width of the plank, the opening is disregarded. The small amount by which the plank is wider than 3 cubits is used as lath to qualify the larger opening as entrance to the alley. (One uses 3 and 2 cubits to be on the safe side. As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.4">Note 71</a>, as a precaution one does not say that the plank has to be 2+ε and the opening 2-ε for some arbitrarily small ε &gt; 0.) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 10b" href="/Eruvin.10b">Babli 10b</a>.</i>. The slight addition is treated as lath, and it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Reading with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 10a" href="/Eruvin.10a">Rashba (<i>Novellae</i> to <i>Eruvin, ad</i> 10a, col. 71</a>) ושרי instead of והשאר “and the remainder.”</i> on condition that what is standing be more than the empty space<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">A general principle in constructing separations for the rules of the Sabbath, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.8.1">Mishnah 1:8</a>.</i>. Should he not make a plank four hand-breadths wide? Did we not state, he makes a plank four hand-breadths wide? Rebbi Abba bar Pappai said, in order to permit both parts without beam<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">If the plank is 4 cubits wide it can be put anywhere in the opening as long as no empty stretch is wider than 10 cubits. Then the rims of the plank on both sides act as laths; it no longer is necessary that the second opening be less than the width of the plank, and neither entrance needs a beam.</i>. Some Tannaim state, he puts up a stick and a double-pointed stake<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δίκρανον, τό.</i>; some Tannaim state, he makes a plank four hand-breadths wide. Rebbi Yose said, practice follows him who said, he makes a plank four hand-breadths wide.
It was stated: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, if an alley has two entrances<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Not that the alley had exits at opposite ends, since then it would be a thoroughfare which cannot be turned into a private domain, but at one end it is open to the public road and nearby it has a second entry point where a piece of side wall is missing.</i>, he puts a beam over one of them and permits it. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Levi: Practice does not follow Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. Rebbi Abba said, only if he put up a stick and a doubly pointed stake; but if he made a plank four hand-breadths wide,<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The second entrance is partially blocked by a vertical plank 4 hand-breadths wide, so that it appears as a hole in the wall but not an entry point.</i> practice follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, as it was stated thus: If an alley has four entrances<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Rashba (<i>ad</i> 10a, col. 93) reads: “4 hand-breadths”.</i>, he puts a beam over one of them and permits it. Rebbi Yose said, only if he does it for a middle one<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The plank must be for the secondary opening, not for the outer opening to the road. The argument proves Rashba’s reading since if there are 4 openings there is no middle one.</i>. But if he put it on an outer one could it be that what is standing here would permit what is open there? What would permit that which is open there? Rebbi Shammai said, explain it that it follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel and you cannot infer anything<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Practice still requires a beam or a lath for each of the entrances.</i>.
A curved and open alley<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">As S. Liebermann has pointed out, the correct interpretation is in Raviah §379, p. 401 (Notes 16 ff.). The alley is L-shaped but open at both ends. It cannot get an <i>eruv</i> in its entirety since it is open at both sides. But it can be turned into two separate private domains, each for one straight leg.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he puts a beam or a lath on one side and makes a door frame on the other side<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">A beam or lath at the entrance from the public road and a door frame at the bend, to indicate that the common area in the alley ends here.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he puts a beam or a lath there which permits<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The sharp corner in itself is enough of a sign that the common area stops here.</i>. In Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion, if they wanted to fix it so that each side may use it up to the closing wall, would not two domains use a common domain? But one may use it up to the bend and the other may use it up to the bend<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The square area at the bend which is common to both legs as straight rows is forbidden to the dwellers in both legs whereas in R. Joḥanan’s way of doing it the door-frame is the border between the two domains and all inhabitants may use their respective domains up to the door frame.</i>. Would not two domains use a domain forbidden to them? Rav and Samuel, Rav like Rebbi Joḥanan and Samuel like Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 6b" href="/Eruvin.6b">Babli 6b</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish were staying in Rebbi Isaac’s street<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Which was an L-shaped alley, one leg of which was turned into a private domain for the purposes of the Sabbath following R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish carried, according to his opinion, Rebbi Joḥanan did not carry, according to his opinion. Rebbi Joḥanan neither forbade nor carried; he said, let the dwellers in the alley be in error, lest they be intentional. What, did he carry or did he not carry? If you say that he carried, then Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion did change. If you say that he did not carry, it should be forbidden to the dwellers in the alley<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">As a matter of principle, a common courtyard or an alley may be turned into a private domain only if all residents there agree to the operation. A single dissent makes the <i>eruv</i> illegal.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan annulled his rights<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">If the dissenting person agrees not to be counted as a dweller there, and therefore, not to use the common space, the <i>eruv</i> may be established without him.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa said, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish forced Rebbi Joḥanan and he carried. Rebbi Mattaniah said, this is correct. What is Rebbi Meïr’s reason<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.1.1">Mishnah 6:1</a> where R. Meïr states that a single dissenting person makes an <i>eruv</i> impossible even if that person either is a Gentile for whom the Sabbath rules do not exist or a Sadducee who objects to the notion of <i>eruv</i> as not biblical. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 62a" href="/Eruvin.62a">Babli 62a</a>.</i>? They considered him as unintentional compared to intentional<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">While a Sadducee willfully disregards rabbinical rules, it is not because he is intent of breaking them but because he does not accept their validity. Since for him breaking the rules excludes criminal intent, it cannot be considered as equivalent of intentionally breaking the rules.</i>. What is the rabbis’ opinion? Since they are suspected of carrying they are as if intentional. They told him, if this be so then the haters of Rebbi Joḥanan should be considered like a Sadducee for the dwellers in the alley and it should become forbidden for the dwellers in the alley<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">If he refuses to recognize the <i>eruv</i> he will be unable to contribute to it and thereby make the entire <i>eruv</i> impossible.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, a Sadducee is suspected of carrying<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Sadducee does recognize only the biblical text as authoritative and strictly refrains from carrying anything in the public domain but objects to the notion of <i>eruv</i> as a rabbinic invention (CD xi), it is quite possible that he might consider a dead-end alley as not a public domain and permitted area for carrying without an <i>eruv</i>.</i>; here what can you say? What is the difference between them? If it was built like a χ. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he puts a beam or a lath on one side and makes a door frame on the other side<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">He considers an X-shaped configuration of alleys as a combination of two V-shaped ones, each one of which is treated according to the rules of L-shaped alleys where each leg can be turned into an private domain by putting a door frame at the bend.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, even many beams do not make it permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The explanation follows Rashi to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 6a" href="/Eruvin.6a">Babli 6a</a>, top. The entrance to the alley is ten cubits wide; the alley itself is wider. If the wall reducing the entrance to 10 cubits is broken in the length of 4 hand-breadths the alley becomes one with two entrances and needs a beam over one and a door frame for the other. The same is true if one of the side walls of the alley is broken for more than 10 cubits (but not if there are framed entrances to courtyards opening into the alley.)</i>.
Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: An alley which was broken at its end into four [hand-breadths], at a side more than ten [cubits]<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The explanation follows Rashi to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 6a" href="/Eruvin.6a">Babli 6a</a>, top. The entrance to the alley is ten cubits wide; the alley itself is wider. If the wall reducing the entrance to 10 cubits is broken in the length of 4 hand-breadths the alley becomes one with two entrances and needs a beam over one and a door frame for the other. The same is true if one of the side walls of the alley is broken for more than 10 cubits (but not if there are framed entrances to courtyards opening into the alley.)</i>. The colleagues in the name of Rav, there is no difference, whether at its end or at a side four [hand-breadths]. They wanted to say, he who says that “at a side more than ten [cubits],” therefore ten are permitted if it is four [hand-breadths] wide<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If the additional wall at the entrance is at least 4 hand-breadths wide, any breach at a side wall up to 10 cubits can be left untreated..</i>. He who says, “whether at its end or at a side four [hand-breadths],” if it is not four hand-breadths wide<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">If the standing wall at the entrance is less than 4 hand-breadths wide, the breach at the side follows the rules of a breach in the front.</i>. Were we not of the opinion that Rav is like Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Joḥanan always requires a beam or a lath at the main entrance and a door frame at the other entrance, how can Rav make it dependent on the size of the breach?</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, to unify the entire domain<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the L-shaped alley he requires a door frame to equally permit <i>eruvim</i> for both legs of the L. But here we have a breach, not a door, and if it is larger than 10 cubits there is no way of turning the alley into a private domain.</i>.
An alley which was broken at its side towards the entrance<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The entrance is ten cubits wide; near the entrance there is a hole in one of the walls bordering the alley.</i>, the rabbis of Caesarea said, Rebbi Ḥiyya and Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">For chronological reasons one has to read “Yasa” instead of “Yose”.</i>. If four [hand-breadths] wide was standing it needs a beam<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Because it is a separate entrance it does not follow the rules of a breach in the wall further away from the entrance.</i>, otherwise it does not need one. What is the difference between this and an alley which has two openings<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The rules for an L-shaped alley do not depend on width or length.</i>? If an alley has two openings, does he not put a beam over one of them and permits? Rav Naḥman bar Jacob said, if an alley has two openings, is it not usual that people enter by one and leave by the other? But is it usual that people enter by one and leave by the breach?
May one beam permit two alleys? Rebbi Jereniah said, Rebbi Zeˋira<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Babylonian spelling. Since in the next paragraph his name is spelled in the original Galilean form זעורה, the spelling here probably is a copyist’s mistake.</i> and Rebbi Abbahu, one said, it is forbidden, the other said, it is permitted. He who said, it is forbidden, if it was put higher than three [hand-breadths] than the walls of the alley; he who said, it is permitted, if it was put lower than three [hand-breadths] than the walls of the alley<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Two diverging alleys whose entrances are close to one another but whose walls have different heights. If the walls have equal heights there is no problem since then one very long beam simply can be considered as two beams, one for each alley. The only problem arises if the beam is appropriate for one alley but not quite so for the other. Then one follows the principle stated earlier (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.9.1">Note 22</a>) that distances of less than three hand-breadths are disregarded.</i>.
Rebbi Zeˋira said, by what can a courtyard become permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">A courtyard wall separating it from the public road was breached. The length of the breach must be less than 10 cubits; otherwise one may not carry in the courtyard on the Sabbath until the wall is repaired.</i>? It was found stated, with one plank<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The practical difference between a lath לחי and a plank פס is that a lath has a minimal length but no minimal width or thickness whereas the plank has minimal length and width (4 hand-breadths) but no minimal thickness (cf. Tosephot 12a, <i>s. v</i>. בפסי). On both ends of the breach one has to put up planks as a minimal indication of a door.</i>, the words of Rebbi, but the Sages say, with two planks. Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Read: “Rebbi”. The R. Ḥiyya quoted here is R. Ḥiyya bar Abba.</i> Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya, practice follows him who said, by two planks on condition that there be a plank of four hand-breadths on each side. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, practice follows him who said, by two planks, but I am saying, by one plank. On condition that there be a plank of four hand-breadths on each side? But there are only planks of three hand-breadths on each side<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">In keeping with the requirement of R. Yose in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.6.1">Mishnah 6</a>.</i>, and what he said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, a plank of any thickness on each side<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">As for the laths used to permit an alley. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 12a" href="/Eruvin.12a">Babli 12a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Zeˋira, Rav Huna in the name of Rav: An alley whose length and width are equal is not permitted by lath or beam but by planks like a courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 12b" href="/Eruvin.12b">Babli 12b</a>.</i>. Samuel heard this from him and exerted himself after him 40 times<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">He repeated in order to memorize.</i>. The rabbis of Caesarea do not say so but rather that Samuel asked Rav, by how much must their length exceed their width? He responded and told him, anything<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli Rav attributes the ruling to his uncle R. Ḥiyya.</i>. For this he exerted himself after him 40 times, for one might need Rav and not find him.
There we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.8.1">Mishnah 6:8</a>. The rules which make a multi-party courtyard accessible to all parties are completely parallel to those which make a multi-courtyard alley accessible to all courtyard dwellers.</i>: “Because the relation of the alley to courtyards is like that of a courtyard to houses.” How many courtyards must there be for an alley<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">That the alley can be turned into private domain for the rules of the Sabbath.</i>? Rav and Samuel both are saying, there cannot be less than two. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, even one courtyard on each side. Rebbi Aha, Rebbi Ḥinena in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan said, even a courtyard on one side and a house on the other side, a house on one side and a store on the other side. Rav Naḥman bar Jacob (asked) [in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan]<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses was written by the scribe; it was changed into the text in brackets by the corrector. The first text is quoted by the Sephardic Rashba (<i>ad</i> 12b, col. 97) but the second by the Ashkenazic <i>Sefer Miṣwot Gadol</i> (<i>Hilkhot Eruvin</i>, fol. 245a in the Venice edition). R. Naḥman bar Jacob is not known to have had contact with R. Joḥanan.</i>: An alley cannot have less than two courtyards, a courtyard cannot have less than two houses<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference in that an alley with only one courtyard cannot be turned into a private domain but a courtyard belonging to only one one-family dwelling automatically is private domain.</i>. An alley whose length and width are equal is not permitted by lath or beam but by planks like a courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 12b" href="/Eruvin.12b">Babli 12b</a>.</i>. Samuel asked Rav, by how much must its length exceed its width? He told him, anything<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli Rav attributes the ruling to his uncle R. Ḥiyya.</i>. The dwellers of the Land of Israel make theirs square. If five alleys open into one alley. Whether what is standing is more than what is breached or what is breached is more than what is standing, it does not need a beam<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">A dead-end alley with five side arms becomes permitted by beam or lath at the entrance from the public road and does not need anything for the side arms (assuming that all dwellers there participate in the <i>eruv</i>) even if the total length of the openings into the side alleys is larger than the total length of the walls bordering the main alley.</i>, if they are distant four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of this clause is unknown.</i>.
Rebbi Hoshaia stated: Doors which open on the public road, if they are open their place becomes public domain, if they are locked their place becomes private domain<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">This is another way to describe a platform in front of a door opening to the public road; cf. <i>Šabbat</i> 1, Notes 172–173.</i>. Alleys that open on the sea are like alleys open to an agricultural area<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The alley is not a dead-end street since the sea does not act as a wall. On the other hand it is not a thoroughfare since it ends in <i>karmelit</i>, not the public domain. Therefore it can become permitted on the Sabbath if it receives a beam or lath at the opening to the public road and a door frame towards the sea like any other alley open on both sides.</i>. The street of Rebbi Ḥanin does not need a door frame since it descends ten hand-breadths within three [cubits]<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">His alley did open to the sea and it did descend 10 hand-breadths within 3 cubits. The dwellers in the upper part can use the declivity as a wall (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.8">Note 85</a>) but then the lower part of the alley is not private domain for the Sabbath. But a door frame at the end pointing to the sea together with a beam at the entrance from the street turns the entire length of the alley into potential private domain.</i>. They made an arrangement to allow the lower-lying houses.
“If there is a door-frame even though it is wider than ten cubits there is no need to reduce<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If there are two door-posts and a beam from one to the other, even if there are no doors, it is clear that the domain is visibly separated from the thoroughfare onto which it opens.</i>.” Ḥananiah bar Shelemia was sitting stating this to Ḥiyya, Rav’s son. Rav put out his head from the window and said, it is not so. He told him, but was it not stated so? He said to him, if you state this to him you have to inform him that it is not so<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 2b-3a" href="/Eruvin.2b-3a">Babli 2b/3a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 11a" href="/Eruvin.11a">11a</a>.</i>.
Abba bar Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">A Babylonian Amora of the second generation, student of his father Rav Huna. He exemplifies the Babylonian tradition, in contrast to the restrictive Galilean tradition which requires a door frame which is ready to have a door placed in it.</i> said, the door frame about which they spoke is a stick on each side and bast over them. Rebbi Yannai the son of Rebbi Ismael in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: A hinge and its fastener; nothing should be missing but the door.
Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: How does one permit public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to an alley between walls which is open at both ends. Turning parts of a public road into private domain, for the owner of houses opposite one another on a street, is mentioned in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 6" href="/Eruvin.6">Babli (6a/b</a>, <i>Šabbat</i> 6a, 117a) but rejected.</i>? Rebbi Jehudah says, a lath on each side or a beam on each side, but the Sages say, a lath and a beam on one side and he makes a door frame on the other side. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: A hinge and its fastener; nothing should be missing but the door. Rebbi Jeremiah said this before Rebbi Zeˋira, who told him, what do you have in your hand<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">This is nothing new since it already was stated by R. Simeon ben Laqish and is rejected by the Babylonian R. Zeˋira who quotes R. Joḥanan as adopting the Babylonian position.</i>? Rebbi Ila in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, the door frame about which they spoke is a stick on each side and bast over them. Rebbi Yose said, the rabbis here follow their opinion and the rabbis there follow their opinion. Since the rabbis there are saying, the door frame about which they spoke is a stick on each side and bast over them; they are those who say, it is not so<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">They follow Rav in rejecting the Mishnah since their “door frame” is flimsy. The Mishnah is accepted as practice in Galilee where a solid frame is required.</i>. But the rabbis here who are saying, a hinge and its fastener; nothing should be missing but the door, they are those who say, it is so.
Ḥanania the son of Rebbi Joshua’s brother<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">His name was Ḥanania ben Ḥanania, the unlucky form indicating a posthumous child.</i> says, the House of Shammai say, a door on each side. When he enters he closes and locks. But the House of Hillel say, a door on one side and a door frame on the other side. Samuel said, practice follows Ḥanania the son of Rebbi Joshua’s brother<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 6b" href="/Eruvin.6b">Babli 6b</a>.</i>. Is that not the same as the earlier statement? Rebbi Abba said, if there is a door it can be locked<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between the rule of Ḥanania following the House of Hillel and the rule stated earlier in the name of the Sages is that Ḥanania requires a door on one side which could be locked even if actually it never will be locked.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa instructed one who made a door frame that he had to make it inside of four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">The door frame has to be made between walls, not at their end.</i>. Rebbi Aybun instructed in this sense.
Rebbi Zeˋira<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">The great variability of the spelling of this name, זעירה, זעירא, זירא raises questions about the homogeneity of the following portion.</i> in the name of Rav Ḥisda: If pegs stick out from the walls of an alley, a person places a beam on them and allows the alley<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">The pegs stick out in front of the alley into the public road. A beam placed there indicates that the alley should be considered a private road even though under the beam there is public road. Only the beam must be within 3 hand-breadths of the alley walls so it may be considered having no distance from them. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 8b" href="/Eruvin.8b">Babli 8b</a>.</i>, on condition that they be within three [hand-breadths] of the alley. Two Amoraim, one said, only if they are strong enough to carry the beam and its tiles, but the other said, even if they are not strong enough to carry the beam and its tiles<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">This is a question which can be raised for all beams, whether they only have to be wide enough to support a tile or also strong enough. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 14a" href="/Eruvin.14a">Babli 14a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Zeˋira asked: under the beam it is forbidden but it permits the alley<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Since the beam is clearly outside the alley, lying over public domain, how can it serve to turn the alley into a quasi-private domain?</i>? Rebbi Aḥa ben Rebbi Ulla said, why did one not say to him,<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">This is the corrector’s text. The scribe’s was ולמה לא. ולא אשכחנן “why not? Did we not find …”. This is a better text.</i> did we not find this for Samuel? As Samuel said, under the beam<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">This now refers to a normal beam lying between the walls of the alley. Since the beam represents the lintel of an imaginary door at the inside of the beam, the space under the beam belongs to the outside. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 8b" href="/Eruvin.8b">Babli 8b</a>.</i> it is forbidden but it permits the alley. Rebbi Zeˋira in the name of Samuel: Under the beam and between the laths it is judged like a threshold<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Šabbat</i> 1, Note 66.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira asked, what kind of threshold<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">If the threshold is ten hand-breadths above street level and is large enough to be a domain in its own right, it would be forbidden to carry there both from inside the house of from the outside.</i>? Like a permitted threshold or a forbidden threshold? Rebbi Zeˋira said, when I was still there it was questionable for me, but when I came here I heard Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If a threshold is ten [hand-breadths] high but less than four wide it is permitted for both domains; only one should not exchange<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">One may not carry anything from the inside to the threshold, put it down, and take it up again moving to the public domain.</i>. Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yose, look what he is saying! He only said, if it is less than four wide. But if it is four wide it is forbidden. He said to him, is that not the case of a cross-beam<sup class="footnote-marker">41,</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μέλαθρον, τό. This is a substantial load-carrying beam used in construction.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">It is implied here that a μέλαθρον is at least four hand-breadths wide (quoted by R. Hananel <i>ad</i> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 3a" href="/Eruvin.3a">Babli 3a</a>). Then the space under it is roofed, clearly not in the public domain and, therefore, permitted to the dwellers in the alley.</i>? Is it not permitted under a cross-beam? Rebbi Zeˋira said, Samuel and Rebbi Joḥanan. Samuel said, under the beam it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">This now refers to a normal beam lying between the walls of the alley. Since the beam represents the lintel of an imaginary door at the inside of the beam, the space under the beam belongs to the outside. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 8b" href="/Eruvin.8b">Babli 8b</a>.</i>, but Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is permitted. Everybody agrees that between laths it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is a flimsy sign and is neither roof nor door, it belongs to the outside. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 9a" href="/Eruvin.9a">Babli 9a</a>.</i>, and it was stated thus: If an alley is made of laths, if between one and the next are four hand-breadths one carries up to the outer one; otherwise one carries up to the inner one<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">There is a path but not between walls. If the walls are represented by laths and between one lath and the next are less than 4 hand-breadths, then according to what had been said before the laths are virtually touching, they all form one lath and by the rule just stated the entire length belongs to the outside. But if the distances are larger, only the lath designating the exit to the public road falls under this rule. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 9a" href="/Eruvin.9a">Babli 9a</a>.</i>. The Rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Uqba, unless the outer one is wider. But if the outer one was wider one carries up to the outer one<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">If the width of the alley at the outermost lath is larger than at the nearby inner laths, it is separate and cannot be counted with the others.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The legitimization of an alley: the House of Shammai say by lath and beam<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">A beam must have a minimal width as indicated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>, there is no requirement of minimal thickness or strength for the lath, which is only needed to indicate the place up to where the dwellers in the alley may carry on the Sabbath.</i>; but the House of Hillel say, either by lath or by beam; Rebbi Eliezer says, two laths<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">On both sides of the alley.</i>. A student<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Babli, this was R. Meїr, the only Sage of whom it is known that he studied first under R. Ismael and then under R. Aqiba.</i> said before Rebbi Aqiba in the name of Rebbi Ismael that the Houses of Shammai and Hillel did not disagree about an alley which is less that four [cubits wide] that it is [permitted] either by lath or by beam; about what did they disagree? About one wider than four [cubits] up to ten<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Since for alleys wider than 10 cubits both Houses agree that one needs “a shape of a door,” i. e., two laths and a beam over them.</i> where the House of Shammai say by lath and beam; but the House of Hillel say, either by lath or by beam. Rebbi Aqiba said, they disagree in both cases.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The legitimization of an alley,” etc. What laths<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to R. Eliezer who in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> requires two laths to permit an alley. The first question is whether he also requires a beam following the House of Shammai or no beam following the House of Hillel. Even though in the Babli R. Eliezer is called a Shammaite, it seems that in general he was extremely conservative and objected to all innovations. Therefore his opinion might show what practice was before schools developed in Pharisaic Judaism. The second question refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.6.1">Mishnah 6</a>, where R. Yose sets minimal standards for the width of laths but is opposed by the majority.</i>? Two laths and beam following the House of Shammai? Laths but no beam following the House of Hillel? Three [hand-breadths wide] following Rebbi Yose? Arbitrarily small following the rabbis? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).1.2">Tosephta 1:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 11b-12a" href="/Eruvin.11b-12a">Babli 1 11b/12a</a>. In the Babli and one Tosephta ms. the name is ben Perida; the other sources have פריה, פירה, פריד.</i>: “It happened that Rebbi Eliezer went to his student Rebbi Yose ben Perora at Oblin. He showed him an alley where there was only one lath. He told him, make it two laths. He said to him, what? Are you telling me to close it off? He told him, let it be closed; there is an argument for the domains of the Sabbath in this respect<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">The objection was that since the alley, while not a thoroughfare, is public property, it is not possible to close it off as private property. R. Eliezer’s answer is that property rights are no argument for the rules of the Sabbath which are only based on use.</i>?” This implies three following Rebbi Yose. If you would say, arbitrarily small following the rabbis, does arbitrarily small close off<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Eliezer agrees that two laths put at right angles to the roadway would in effect close it off it is clear that these cannot be of negligible width.</i>?
It was stated, practice follows the student, for if it were not so, would we say following the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Since the student in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> affirms the tradition of the House of Hillel and only gives a new interpretation of the position of the House of Shammai, there should be no question of practice which is not supposed to follow the House of Shammai.</i>? Could practice follow the House of Shammai rather than the House of Hillel? But since it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 12a" href="/Eruvin.12a">Babli 12a</a> (middle). In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta 1:3</a> (and the first version in the Babli) the reading is “four cubits”. The reference is to the width of the alley.</i>, “Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, an alley which is not four hand-breadths deep does not need a beam.” The words of the Sages? Rav Ḥuna in the name of Rav: An alley which is less than four cubits does not need anything<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">In general the majority of the Sages has to be followed against Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel (in the theory of the Babli, except for Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel quoted in the Mishnah); but in this case it was stated that practice follows the student who required either lath or beam for any alley irrespective of width.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The beam about which they spoke must be wide enough to carry a tile which is half a brick of three hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore the tile is 1.5׳3 hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>.</i>; therefore it is sufficient for a beam to be one hand-breadth wide to accept the tile lengthwise<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since a hand-breadth is counted as 4 thumb-breadths, the tile will extend over the beam by one thumb-breadth on each side.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The beam about which they spoke,” etc. Rebbi Abba said, to accommodate a row<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δόμος, ὁ.</i> of tiles lengthwise. It was stated: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, to accommodate a row of tiles crosswise. What is the difference between them? Rebbi Ashian said, strength is between them. In the opinion of the rabbis, forty tiles. In the opinion of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, twenty tiles<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation seems self-contradictory. The length of the beam is assumed to be the standard 10 cubits which is 60 hand-breadths. A tile is 3 hand-breadths long and 1.5 hand-breadths wide. If the tiles are put on the beam lengthwise, the beam has to carry 20 tiles, if crosswise, there are 40 tiles. Of the medieval commentators, Rashba (col. 105, Note 446) quotes the text as given here but adds “it seems that the formulation is slightly corrupt.” His student Ritba (<i>ad</i> 13b, ed. Warsaw 22a) switches the places of 40 and 20. The same is true for Meïri (ed. Herschler, col. 68a) but since he gives a paraphrase rather than a copy of the text it is not clear how he read it. In any case, the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 14a" href="/Eruvin.14a">Babli disagrees, 14a</a>.</i>.
Two beams, on each one half of a hand-breadth; how much can be between them? Rebbi Zeˋira said, half a hand-breadth. What is the difference between half a hand-breadth on either side and half a hand-breadth in the middle<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the rabbis in the preceding paragraph. If two beams protrude from the walls on each side, they can be counted as one only if 20 tiles can be put on them. Therefore the gap between them must be bridged by one tile which according to R. Zeˋira must lie safely on both sides but according to R. Hoshaia only minimally.</i>? Rebbi Hoshaia stated, a hand-breadth. Two beams, on each a little more than a third of a hand-breadth and between them less than a third of a hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">This is a more restrictive interpretation of the preceding. As explained in the following sentence, we have a general rule that gaps can be disregarded if they are minor (for walls less that 3 or 4 hand-breadths) and in addition the solid material is wider than the gap. When applied to beams, the author of the present statement holds that on each side the solid part must be larger than the gap. To accommodate one hand-breadth, on each side one needs 1/3+ε of solid material to justify a gap of 1/3 -ε for arbitrary ε&gt;0.</i>. This parallels what Rebbi Joḥanan said, what is standing and empty space combine for four [hand-breadths], on condition that what is standing be more than the empty space<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to walls; cf. Chapter 7(24b l.55), Chapter 8(25a l.32), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 16a" href="/Eruvin.16a">Babli 16a</a>.</i>.
If a beam protrudes from one wall but does not touch the second wall, or there were two opposite ones, if the gap between one and the other is three hand-breadths it needs another beam, otherwise it does not need another beam. There are Tannaim who state, four. He who says three, that a human may pass through. He who says four, that it be a place<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">This explains the general disagreement whether one may disregard a gap only up to 3 or up to 4 hand-breadths, cf. Notes 80,81. 3 hand-breadths is the minimum width of an adult; 4 hand-breadth everywhere is the minimal size of a domain in the rules of the Sabbath, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.5">Note 74</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 14a" href="/Eruvin.14a">Babli 14a</a>.</i>.
If one of the heads of a beam is higher that twenty cubits and the other lower than twenty cubits, one looks that if one would cut within three hand-breadths it would be seen lower than twenty cubits it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">If the beam except the last 3 hand-breadths near the second wall is below 20 cubits the beam is acceptable since the higher part could be cut and it still would be qualified under the rule of the preceding paragraph. Since the rules of <i>Eruvin</i> are rabbinic, the possibility alone is sufficient for qualification.</i>, otherwise it is forbidden. If one of the heads of a beam is lower than ten hand-breadths and the other higher than ten hand-breadths, one looks that if one would cut within three hand-breadths it would be seen lower<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously one has to read: higher. Then the argument is parallel to that of the preceding sentence.</i> than ten hand-breadths it is permitted, otherwise it is forbidden.
If two beams are one on top of the other. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, one looks at the lower one as if it were higher or the higher one as if it were lower, on condition that the upper one not be higher than twenty cubits and the lower not be lower than ten hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 14a" href="/Eruvin.14a">Babli 14a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta 1:5</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> requires that the beam be wide enough for a tile to be placed there. The case considered here is of two beams, each one too narrow, but which would be acceptable if they were parallel on the same level. R. Yose ben R. Jehudah asserts that if the beams could be imagined to be moved vertically to become parallel they are acceptable even if on different height levels.</i>. It was found stated that Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah follows his father’s argument and disagrees with him. He follows his fathers argument since he says, one looks at the lower one as if it were higher or the higher one as if it were lower<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Jehudah in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.4.1">Mishnah 4</a> is explicit that the beams do not have to be strong he is the author of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.5.1">Mishnah 5</a> which introduces the principle of “one looks as if” used by his son.</i>. He disagrees with him since he says, on condition that the upper one not be higher than twenty cubits and the lower not be lower than ten hand-breadths. If you would say that he follows his father’s opinion it could be arbitrarily high<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose, Ḥinena bar Shelemiah, Rebbi Yudan; Ḥinena bar Shelemiah in the name of Rav: Only if the upper one be within three hand-breadths of the lower one<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">He permits the argument of R. Yose ben R. Jehudah if the difference in level of the two beams is negligible, i. e., smaller than 3 hand-breadths.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Wide enough to carry a tile and strong enough to carry a tile. Rebbi Jehudah says, wide enough even if not strong enough<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since the only function of the beam is to indicate the boundary of the private domain in the alley, only size but not structural stability is required.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Wide enough to carry a tile,<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>” etc. It was stated: Rebbi Simeon says, strong even if it is not wide. Rav Huna in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">The presumed author of the anonymous Mishnah. Since the editor of the Mishnah formulated it without mentioning a name, he indicated that his opinion was practice to be followed.</i>. Samuel said, practice follows Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, practice follows Rebbi Simeon. Rebbi Simeon bar Carsana said, since you are saying that practice follows this one and that one, one may follow this one or that one without worry. Rebbi Mana said, since it was said that practice follows the rabbis, one disregards individuals and acts following the rabbis.
Rav went to a certain place; he saw a certain alley, he understood that [the beam] was [rotten]<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">With Rashba (<i>ad</i> 15a, col. 112) read בלייה. The action must have been on a Sabbath; Rav prohibited carrying in the alley.</i>, he hit it with a stick and felled it. Rav Ḥuna said to him, the date palm stands<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">A date palm was standing at the entrance of the alley; it could be used as lath following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.7.1">Mishnah 7</a>.</i>. Rav told him, the eyes of Rav Ḥuna are high, the eyes of Rav are not high; but I am forbidding it for them because of a beam<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Since he stated that practice follows R. Meïr there was no valid <i>eruv</i> to start with.</i> and the next Sabbath I am permitting it to them because of a lath<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">The date palm can be used as lath but it must be so in the preparation for the Sabbath. No <i>eruv</i> can be made on the day of Sabbath.</i>, that they should know that if the palm tree were removed they would be forbidden to carry. Some want to say, that they should not think following Rebbi Jehudah, as Rebbi Jehudah said, “wide enough even if not strong enough.<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>”
Rebbi Ḥinena did not say so but: <sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">This is the version alluded to in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 15a" href="/Eruvin.15a">Babli, 15a</a>.</i> Rav went to a certain place; he saw a certain alley whose beam had been removed, and forbade it. Rav Ḥuna said to him, the date palm stands. Rav told him, the eyes of Rav Ḥuna are high, the eyes of Rav are not high; but I am forbidding it for them because of a beam, and the next Sabbath I am permitting it to them because of a lath, that they should know that if the palm tree were removed they would be forbidden to carry. Some want to say, that they should not think following Rebbi Jehudah, <sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">The following should be replaced by the text of the preceding paragraph. The text here is taken from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.6.2">Chapter 2 (Note 93</a>) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 4:3:2-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.4.3.2-12"><i>Beṣah</i> 4:2</a> (62c l. 17) and has no connection with the present topic. The problem is that in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.6.1">Mishnah 2:6</a> R. Jehudah allows carrying in a corral only if it contains at least a cistern, or a water ditch, or a cave, but in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 4:1:2-2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.4.1.2-2.4">Mishnah <i>Beṣah</i> 4:2</a> he permits taking anything from any corral which is defined as fenced-in area close to a village.</i> as we have stated there, “what is a corral? Anything close to a village, the words of Rebbi Jehudah.” And here he says so? Rebbi Mana said, Rebbi Jehudah treated it<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">The cistern or cave is treated as a house and the corral as the house’s yard where carrying is permitted.</i> as a house.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If it was made of straw or reeds, one considers it as if of metal<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This Mishnah follows R. Jehudah in the previous Mishnah which thereby is declared practice to be followed.
