The beam does not need to be able to carry any load.</i>, curved as if it were straight<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">If the beam is not a straight log but is curved.</i>; round one considers it as if square<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If the beam is a round log then while it might be strong enough to carry a tile it is impossible to fasten one on it permanently.</i>. If its circumference is three hand-breadths its width is one hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This defines π as 3, from an interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 7:23" href="/I_Kings.7.23"><i>1K.</i> 7:23</a> (presuming that עָגוֹל means “circular” not “oval”).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If it was made of straw or reeds,<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.5.1">Mishnah 5</a>.</i>” etc. For whom was this needed? For Rebbi Jehudah, as Rebbi Jehudah said, “wide enough even if not strong enough.<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">Since for the majority a bundle of straw never can carry the load of tiles.</i>”
“Curved one considers it as if it were straight.<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.5.1">Mishnah 5</a>.</i>” Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Zeˋira: This is Rebbi Jehudah’s. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Zeˋira: It is everybody’s opinion, but only if the curvature be at the side, a curvature which does not obstruct the alley. But a curvature which impedes the alley makes it forbidden. What is a curvature which obstructs<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously one has to read: “does not obstruct”. Noted by Rashba, col. 109.</i> the alley? Any which if you excise it there is less than three [hand-breadths] between the pieces<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">If one eliminates the curved parts the gap created in the straight segment is less than 3 hand-breadths. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 14a" href="/Eruvin.14a">Babli 14a</a>.</i>.
“Round, one considers it as if square.<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.5.1">Mishnah 5</a>.</i>” Again this is Rebbi Jehudah’s<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">Since tiles cannot be laid on a round beam, the round beam is unacceptable for the majority.</i>. They inferred it from the sea<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">The water container in Solomon’s temple. Since the verse there states that its diameter was 10 cubits and its circumference 30, it is inferred that for halakhic computations the ratio of circumference of a circle to diameter is 3 (even though it is obvious that for other computations this cannot be correct since it is the ratio of the circumference of the regular hexagon to the diameter).</i>. <i>He made the sea cast</i>, <i>ten cubits</i><sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 7:23" href="/I_Kings.7.23"><i>1K</i>. 7:23</a>.</i>. It is impossible to say “circular” since already it was said “square”<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">The verse notes that the “sea” at the top was circular, diameter 10 and circumference 30. But in v. 24 it says that below it was adorned by flower buds, 10 cubits at each side. This implies that the bottom was square.</i>. It is impossible to say “square” since already it was said “circular”. If you said circular, it would turn out to contain 160 times the measure of purity. If you said square, it would turn out to contain 122 times the measure of purity. Therefore you have to say that the upper two cubits were circular, the lower three cubits were square and it turns out that it contained 150 times the measure of purity<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">The numbers are impossible; to make them approximately intelligible one has to switch the positions of “square” and “circular”. The height of the “sea” was 5 cubits. The “measure of purity” is the minimum size of a <i>miqweh</i> which is 3(cubits)<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup>. If the “sea” were cubical, the outer volume would be 500(cubits)<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup> = 166⅔ <i>miqwaot</i>. Since the verse asserts that the walls were 1 hand-breadth wide (1/6 cubit) the actual inner volume was 4 5/6 (9⅔)<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup> = 451.6481 (cubits)<sup>3</sup> = 150.54 <i>miqwaot</i>. The fractional part should be disregarded. If the “sea” were a right circular cylinder, with the prescribed value of π=3 the outer volume would be 375(cubits)<sup>3</sup> = 125 <i>miqwaot</i>; the inner volume 338.7361 (cubits)<sup>3</sup> = 122.91 <i>miqwaot</i>. In v. 26 it is stated that the volume contained in the “sea” was 2’000 <i>bat</i> which is 6’000 <i>se</i>ˋ<i>ah</i> or 150 <i>miqwaot</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 14a" href="/Eruvin.14a">Babli 14a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 109b" href="/Pesachim.109b"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 109b</a>). This volume also is the sum of the volumes of a rectangular solid of edge legths 3,10,10 and a right circular cylinder of radius 5 and height 3, as stated in the text. The problem is that these are outer measures; the inner measures are consistently disregarded.</i>. One verse says, <i>it contained 2’000 bat</i><sup>204</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 7:26" href="/I_Kings.7.26"><i>1K</i>. 7:26</a>.</i>, but another verse says, <i>it contained a volume of 3’000 bat</i><sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 4:5" href="/II_Chronicles.4.5"><i>2Chr</i>. 4:5</a>.</i>. It is impossible to say 2’000 since already it says 3’000 and it is impossible to say 3’000 since already it says 2’000. Therefore you have 2’000 in fluid measure which are 3’000 in dry measure. From here the Sages learned that 40 <i>seah</i> in fluid measure are two <i>kor</i> in dry measure<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 4:5" href="/II_Chronicles.4.5"><i>2Chr</i>. 4:5</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The laths<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The plural indicates that the statement is valid also in R. Eliezer’s opinion.</i> about which they spoke, their height ten hand-breadths, their width and thickness arbitrarily small; Rebbi Yose said, their width three hand-breadths.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The laths<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The plural indicates that the statement is valid also in R. Eliezer’s opinion.</i> about which they spoke,” etc. The Mishnah if they were distant from the wall. But if they were leaning against the wall even Rebbi Yose will agree<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">A lath plastered on the wall may be narrower than 3 hand-breadths.</i>. And it was stated thus: If one made laths for an alley, if they are less than three [hand-breadths] from the ground or they were within three [hand-breadths] from the wall, it is permitted. Three only if what is standing be more than the empty space<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">In the tradition of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 14b" href="/Eruvin.14b">Babli 14b</a> a space of full 3 hand-breadths never is negligible.</i>.
Building stones which protrude from the building, if between one and the other is less than three hand-breadths they are considered as laths<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 15a" href="/Eruvin.15a">Babli 15a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 1:9" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).1.9">Tosephta 1:9</a>.</i>. Corners sticking out at each side are considered as laths<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">The entrance to the alley is slightly narrower than the alley itself because on both sides the walls of the alley end in convex corners. This is enough of a hint of a door frame.</i>. A wall which is indented and looks like a plank from inside but even on the outside or looks like a plank from outside but even on the inside is considered as laths<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 15a" href="/Eruvin.15a">Babli 15a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 1:10" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).1.10">Tosephta 1:10</a>; the thin wall is considered as door frame.</i>. If it was prolonged, Abba bar Rav Huna said, one looks at it as if indented<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">A small continuation of a wall of the alley is built into the street into which the alley opens. The extension represents a door frame.</i>. Rav Ḥisdai objected, did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.2.1">Mishnah 9:2</a>. The breach happened on the Sabbath. The large courtyard may continue to be used to move things since the breach acts like a door and is not open into public domain. The small yard cannot be used since the breach turns it into an appendix of the large one and its dwellers did not contribute to the <i>eruv</i> of the large one.</i>, “if a large courtyard collapsed into a small one, the large one is permitted but the small one forbidden.” Should not the walls of the large one be like laths for the walls of the small one and the small one be permitted? Abba bar Rav Huna told him, explain it if they were extended more than ten<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that the breach can be at most 10 cubits wide since it is considered a door. If only one cubit of the wall is left standing, 3 hand-breadths on each side, they should be considered as laths for the small one and its <i>eruv</i> should continue to be valid. If more of the wall is standing the total sum of the standing wall has to be more than 10 cubits.</i>. Rebbi Ashian stated before Rebbi Aḥa, if the large one is eleven but the small one ten. The <i>baraita</i> was searched for but not found.
<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts a Genizah fragment edited by Ginzberg, p. 93 (G). The title in brackets is added from G.</i>[Rebbi] Aḥa told him, so says Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: One does not use as support any Mishnah which is not accepted by a group<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">The explanation of R. Ashian is without foundation.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, it follows him who said, a courtyard becomes permitted by two planks on condition that there be a plank of four hand-breadths on each side<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The practical difference between a lath לחי and a plank פס is that a lath has a minimal length but no minimal width or thickness whereas the plank has minimal length and width (4 hand-breadths) but no minimal thickness (cf. Tosephot 12a, <i>s. v</i>. בפסי). On both ends of the breach one has to put up planks as a minimal indication of a door.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, even you have to say that it follows him who said, a courtyard becomes permitted by two planks on condition that there be a plank of four hand-breadths on either side. (Rav) [Rebbi Mar]<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is the Leiden ms.’s, the one in brackets from G.</i> Uqba in the name of the rabbis there: They are considered only for the large one but are not considered for the small one<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian rabbis essentially accept R. Ashian’s objection that the smaller courtyard also be permitted but explain that the Mishnah refers to a special configuration where the walls of the large courtyard could not be used for the smaller one. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 9b" href="/Eruvin.9b">Babli 9b</a>.</i>.
If a wall on one side is extended and on the other side is shortened. Cahana said, he puts the beam obliquely and uses it obliquely<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">If the walls bordering on the alley do not end at equal distances from the road, one may use a beam going from the end of the wall at one side to the end of the wall at the other. Greek λοξός,-ή, -όν “oblique”.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Cahana: he puts the beam in the diagonal and uses in the diagonal, but only on condition that the beam not be extended more than ten? (From) Rebbi Yose [said]<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the Leiden ms. in parentheses, of G in brackets.</i>, Rav Eudaimon the emigrant confirmed it, on condition that the beam not be extended more than ten<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">Since the beam represents the entrance door and no door may be more than 10 cubits wide, the restriction also applies to the oblique beam. It needed the inquiry of an emigrant to Babylonia to ascertain the tradition of the Babylonian Cahana.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: On condition that the wall not be extended more than four [cubits]<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">May the longer wall extend more than 4 cubits over the shorter one, or do more than 4 cubits at one side define a domain different from the alley?</i>? [Rebbi Yose, Rebbi Ila, Rebbi Aḥa<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">If one does not read the additions from G, the preceding question was declarative, not a question, and in the end only the first question of R. Aḥa was obvious for R. Yose. If one reads the additions, the name tradition here is thoroughly garbled since R. Aḥa, a contemporary of R. Yose, cannot have been the source of the earlier R. Ila. In addition, at the end it is asserted that what was problematic for R. Aḥa was obvious for R. Yose and vice-versa. Therefore in the previous sentence one has to replace “Aḥa” by “Yose”, and read here “R. Aḥa, R. Ila” as recognized by S. Liebermann.</i> in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: On condition that the wall not be extended more than four [cubits].] You conclude to say, what was problematic for the one was obvious for the other [and what was problematic for the other was obvious for the one].
<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of this is totally unknown. Similar language is used in Chapter 8, on Mishnah 3 (25a l.36).</i> Rebbi Ulla said, the Mishnah is from three to four [hand-breadths]. Less than three even more than ten [cubits] is permitted, four even less than ten [cubits] is prohibited. Where do we hold? From three to four.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may use anything for laths, even living things<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">One may tie an animal which is at least ten hand-breadths high to the entrance of an alley as a symbolic lath. R. Meїr requires the lath to be permanently fixed.</i>; Rebbi Meїr forbids this. And it makes impure because of a cover stone<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">If an animal is used to cover a grave, it acts as a grave stone and touching it transmits the severe impurity of the dead. For R. Meїr a living animal cannot play the role of a grave stone, therefore its body is a roof over the corpse and acts as a shield against transmission of impurity.</i>, but Rebbi Meїr declares it pure. One writes on it divorce documents<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The husband may write the text of the divorce document and have the witnesses sign on the horns of a cow and hand over the cow to the wife, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.3.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 2:3</a>, where R. Yose the Galilean also rejects foodstuffs as writing material for divorce documents.</i> but Rebbi Yose the Galilean disqualifies.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One may use anything for laths,” etc. It was stated: Anything alive one makes into a sidewall<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">The wall of a booth for Tabernacles.</i> but one does not make it into a lath, the words of Rebbi Meïr, but the Sages say, one does not make it into a sidewall but makes it into a lath. Rebbi Aḥa said, there is one Tanna; for him who says that one makes it into a sidewall one makes it into a lath, and for him who says that one does not make it into a sidewall one does not make it into a lath<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">He asserts that both for R. Meïr and for the Sages there is no difference between the rules for <i>Sukkah</i> and <i>Eruvin</i> as far as use of animals is concerned; their only difference is whether such use is permitted or not. One statement covers both cases.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, there are two Tannaim. Why could one make it into a sidewall? For if it would slip off, would he not notice? Why does one not make it into a lath? For if it would slip off, would he notice<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">There is a reason to differentiate between the rules of <i>Sukkah</i> and <i>Eruvin</i>. A person using the booth will immediately notice if a wall is missing; if the animal runs away he will not use the booth. But a person using the alley may not see the place of the lath; if the animal runs away he might use the alley to carry when it is forbidden to do so.</i>? Rebbi Joshua the Southerner was standing with students of Rebbi Aḥa. (They said to him) [He said to them]<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of [G] is preferable over that of (the Leiden ms.)</i>, it is said at the end what disagrees with your teacher: But the Sages say, one does not make it into a sidewall but makes it into a lath. Since in a situation where he would not notice in case it slipped off you are saying that it is permitted, here where he would notice in case it slipped off not so much more? Therefore there must be two Tannaim according to Rebbi Aḥa<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Yose has shown that it is unreasonable to allow animals to be used for <i>eruvin</i>, the only question is whether the use is permitted for a <i>sukkah</i> or not.</i>. Since you are saying that there are two Tannaim according to Rebbi Yose, so you also have to say here, there are two Tannaim according to Rebbi Aḥa.
<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 2:4:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.2.4.2-5"><i>Sukkah</i> 2:4</a> (52d l.60, <b>ס</b>).</i> Rebbi Simeon bar Carsana in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: Rebbi Meïr, and Rebbi Yose, and Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah, all three said the same. Rebbi Meïr of the laths, “it makes impure because of a cover stone<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">If an animal is used to cover a grave, it acts as a grave stone and touching it transmits the severe impurity of the dead. For R. Meїr a living animal cannot play the role of a grave stone, therefore its body is a roof over the corpse and acts as a shield against transmission of impurity.</i>, but Rebbi Meïr declares it pure.<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the previous paragraph, an animal may run away and therefore is not under the rules of a stone in any way.</i>” Rebbi Yose of <i>Ahilut</i>, “Rebbi Yose said, the house on a ship does not transmit impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">Since the house is built to be moved all the time it does not qualify as “tent” and the rules of “tent impurity” do not apply to it.</i>.” Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah, as it was stated: It happened that Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah and Rebbi Aqiba came in a ship, and Rebbi Aqiba made himself a <i>sukkah</i> on the ship’s deck. There came a wind which blew it away. Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah said to him, Aqiba, where is your <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eleazar ben Azariah did not make a <i>sukkah</i> on the ship since he holds that since such a <i>sukkah</i> is not guaranteed to stay for the 8 days of Tabernacles it is not qualified even if the wind does not blow it away.</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph, dealing with divorce documents, is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:3:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.3.2-8"><i>Gittin</i> 2:3</a>, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:7-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.7-10">Notes 83–97</a>.</i> What is Rebbi Yose the Galilean’s reason? <i>Scroll</i>. Since a scroll is special in not being a living being, so no living being. Since a scroll is special in not being food, so no food. What is the rabbis’ reason? <i>Scroll</i> Since a scroll is detached, so everything being detached. Are stalks of food in Rebbi Yose the Galilean’s opinion like food? Let us hear from the following: If he wrote it on a deer’s antlers, shaved it off, had it signed, and gave it to her, it is valid. Because he shaved it off before he had it signed. Therefore, not if he had it signed and afterwards shaved it off. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Miasha: Only if he wrote on the male horn. But if he wrote on the sheath<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">Greek νάρθηξ, -ηκος, ὁ.</i> it is as if separated and is valid. Rebbi Jonah asked: Is that the same for preparation of produce? If he desired that it should rain on an animal and [the rain] dripped from the animal onto food. There, Rebbi Yose said <i>scroll</i>, since a scroll is special in not being a living being, so no living being; and here, does he say so? There is a difference since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:34" href="/Leviticus.11.34"><i>Lev</i>. 11:34</a>.</i>, <i>any drinkable drink in any vessel shall be impure</i>. Then also if it rained into cisterns, ditches and caves? There is a difference here, for it is written: <i>a vessel</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a caravan rested in an area of difficult access<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Which therefore is <i>karmelit,</i> where carrying is forbidden only rabbinically and which therefore can be turned into a kind of private domain by an improvised wall.</i> which they surrounded by the implements of animals<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The bags on the beasts of burden may be used as temporary walls for the Sabbath.</i> one may move things inside on condition that the fence be ten hand-breadths high and the openings not be more than the built-up part<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The bags used for the wall need not touch one another but the openings must be smaller than the lengths of the bags, and no opening may be more than 10 cubits.</i>. Any opening up to ten cubits is permitted since it is like a door; more than that is forbidden.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a caravan rested in an area of difficult access<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Which therefore is <i>karmelit,</i> where carrying is forbidden only rabbinically and which therefore can be turned into a kind of private domain by an improvised wall.</i>,” etc. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of (Rebbi Ḥasdai) [Rav Ḥisda]<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">Since no R. Ḥasdai is otherwise known in the Talmudim, the reading of [G} is preferable to that of the (Leiden ms.).</i>: For whom was this needed? For Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah, since Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:3-10:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.3-10.6">Mishnah 10</a>.</i>, any partition which is not woof and warp is no partition, (the words of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehuda.)<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">This quote from the Mishnah is inappropriate here; it was deleted by the corrector in the ms. But G shows that it belongs to the original text.</i> But he agrees here, and it was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 15b-16a" href="/Eruvin.15b-16a">Babli 15b/16a</a>.</i>: One makes partition with everything, even camel saddles, even tubs, even camels, on condition that between two saddles there not be space for a saddle, between two tubs there not be space for a tub, between two camels there not be space for a camel, and on condition that no standing wall be opposite standing wall and breach opposite breach<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">Not only is it required that the length of the wall be strictly greater than the opening (a matter of dispute in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 15b" href="/Eruvin.15b">Babli 15b</a>) but also, and this is accepted in the Babli, that there can be no straight path from an opening on one side to that on the other side. This implies that if the caravan is camping on a road, the roadway has to be blocked at least on one side.</i>, but a standing wall opposite a breach and a breach opposite a standing wall.
<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:2:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.2.9"><i>Kilaim</i> 4:4 (Notes 82–87</a>,<b>כ</b>), it also is found in <i>Sukkah</i> 1:1 (52a l. 74,<b>ס</b>) The baraita is similar to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 4:6" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).4.6">Tosephta Kilaim 4:6</a> quoted also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 16a" href="/Eruvin.16a">Babli <i>Eruvin</i> 16a</a>. The main difference between Yerushalmi and Babli-Tosephta is that in the latter source, each breach has to be smaller than the surrounding wall whereas in the Yerushalmi only the total length of standing wall has to be larger than the total length of the missing parts.<br>The problem of <i>kilaim</i> addressed here is that grain must be separated from vines by at least 4 cubits. If the farmer wants to sow grain close to his vineyard, he has to fence off the vineyard by a wall. The question is, what is called a wall?</i> It turns out that concerning <i>kilaim</i> everything less than three [hand-breadths] is considered closed<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">As noted before, an opening of less than three hand-breadths in a wall is disregarded; in computing the total length of the torn-down parts, breaches smaller than three hand-breadths in width are disregarded.</i>. From three to four, if what is standing is more than what is torn down it is permitted, but if what is torn down is more than what is standing, it is forbidden. From four [hand-breadths] to ten [cubits], if what is standing is more than what is torn down, it is permitted; but if what is torn down is more than what is standing it is permitted where it is standing<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli notes that not only are breaches up to three hand-breadths long disregarded as holes, but also standing pieces of wall up to three hand-breadths wide are disregarded as walls. This is the background of the discussion later in this paragraph.</i>, forbidden where it is torn down.
Concerning the Sabbath, everything less than three [handbreadths] is considered closed. From three to four, and from four [hand-breadths] to ten [cubits], it is permitted if what is standing is more than what is torn down, but forbidden if what is torn down is more than what is standing. More than ten, it is forbidden even though what is standing is more than what is torn down. Rebbi Ḥananiah, Rebbi Judah bar Pazi, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: There is no “from three to four.<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan wants to eliminate the middle part of the baraita, the one dealing with breaches of between three and four hand-breadths, in case that the standing walls also are in pieces less than four hand-breadths wide. While a hole wider than three hand-breadths is considered a breach, a wall narrower than four hand-breadths is not a wall. In that case, there are only holes and no wall at all; the entire wall should be considered as non-existent! He requires in this case that any sown field should be at least four cubits distant from the vineyard.</i>” There is a hole of three and no place of four! Rebbi Mana objected: Did we not state: “One surrounds with sticks,” does a stick form a place<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:3-10:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.3-10.6">Mishnah 1:10</a>. Since the sticks are counted and no stick is even one hand-breadth wide, any piece of wall should be considered a wall. The answer is that as long as no two sticks are three hand-breadths distant from each other, the entire dividing fence is considered a solid wall.</i>? He said to him, do not object to me from a breach less than three hand-breadths, since anything less than three is considered closed! Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav: In any case, since what is standing is more than what is torn down, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">This explicitly rejects the text of Babli/Tosephta, that no single hole may be wider than both adjacent pieces of standing wall; the wall is valid as long as the combined length of standing wall (of at least 4 hand-breadths length) is larger than the combined length of the breaches.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may surround by three ropes, one higher than the other, on condition that the distance between one rope and the next be less than three hand-breadths and the measure and their thickness be more than a hand-breadth so that together it be more than ten hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of making a wall of camel or donkey bags the caravan may delineate its resting place by putting up some poles and stretching ropes from one to the other. This then is considered a wall if the distances from the ground to the first rope and between the ropes each are less than 3 hand-breadths. If such a distance is denoted by 3-ε, the entire open space is 9–3ε. Therefore the ropes have to cover a width of at least l+3ε to insure that the top of the third rope be 10 hand-breadths above ground.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One may surround by three ropes,” etc. Rebbi Zeˋira said, they said this only about ten or more than ten<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah requires a wall for the Sabbath to be at least 10 hand-breadths high; similarly <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.10.1">Mishnah <i>Sukkah</i> 1:10</a> requires walls of a least 10 hand-breadths. R. Zeˋira states that in contrast to the rules of the Sabbath where only slightly more than 50% actually must be wall, the rules for a <i>sukkah</i> for which walls are hanging down actually require 10 hand-breadths. The consensus of all commentators is to read here “10” in the masculine even though it is written in the feminine since “10 cubits” would make no sense at all.</i>; therefore no less than ten. But was it stated thus? If one brought a wall of seven hand-breadths and raised it less than three from the ground, is it permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">Since דוֹפָן always is used for walls of a <i>sukkah</i>, the <i>baraita</i> refers to a <i>sukkah</i>. It is stated that if the wall almost reaches to the roof but is almost 3 hand-breadths from the ground, it is considered a wall standing on the ground since distances of less than 3 hand-breadths are disregarded. The text is repeated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.1.6">Chapter 7, Note 44</a>.</i>? There you are saying, everything less than three [handbreadths] is considered closed, and here you should be saying, everything less than three [handbreadths] is considered closed. If you want to object, object to the following: If one brought a wall of slightly more than (four) [seven]<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">The text in (parentheses) was written by the scribe, the one in [brackets] is the corrector’s. The correction is erroneous.</i> hand-breadths and raised it less than two hand-breadths from the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">If the rules of the Sabbath were applicable to <i>Sukkah</i>, this should qualify as a wall since its upper rim is 7 hand-breadths from the ground, and be acceptable for a roof 10 hand-breadths high. R. Zeˋira disqualifies it since the rules were meant only for the ground, not the roof.</i>, should it come following what Rebbi Joḥanan said, what is standing and empty space combine for four hand-breadths, on condition that what is standing be more than the empty space<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">A general principle in constructing separations for the rules of the Sabbath, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.8.1">Mishnah 1:8</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">The following text is both in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.4"><i>Kilaim</i> 4:4 (Notes 51–81</a>,<b>כ</b>) and in <i>Sukkah</i> 1:1 (52a l. 50,<b>ס</b>). Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 11a" href="/Eruvin.11a">Babli 11a</a>.</i> Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Rebbi Judah ben Ḥaninah<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote">This is the correct name as given in the parallels.</i>: If one put in four poles at the four corners of a vineyard and connected them with a bast string on top, this saves as a symbolic fence<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">In Tractates <i>Kilaim</i> and <i>Eruvin</i> the word פֵּיאָה does not mean “corner” as in biblical Hebrew and in Tractate <i>Peah</i> but a wire or string strung as a symbolic fence around a field or a vineyard to separate it from the neighboring one, or around a town to make it a “walled” town for the rules of the Sabbath. The imaginary fence represented by the string “saves” from the prohibition of <i>kilaim</i>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the rules of separation walls for the Sabbath are the rules for <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 11" href="/Eruvin.11">Babli 11a/b</a>, the statement of R. Simeon ben Laqish in the name of R. Judah ben Ḥaninah explicitly excludes the expedient of the bast rope for the Sabbath, but R. Joḥanan excludes it also for <i>kilaim</i>. R. Simeon ben Laqish is reported there to accept for himself the statement attributed here to R Joḥanan, that the rules for Sabbath and <i>kilaim</i> are identical, whereas R. Joḥanan rejects this in general. The position of the Babli is that R. Simeon ben Laqish only reports the opinion of R. Judah ben Ḥaninah without accepting it himself, and that R. Joḥanan prohibits the use of a rope for openings wider than 10 cubits, see <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.9.3">Note 262</a>. The Yerushalmi accepts R. Simeon ben Laqish’s statement as describing his own position.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it happened that Rebbi Joshua ben Qorḥah went to Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri at Nagnigar<sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote">A place in lower Galilee of unknown location.</i>; he showed him a field called <i>bet ḥaverata</i> whose wall had parts torn down more than ten [cubits] wide. He took wood to insert, forked poles<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δίκρανον, τό.</i> to insert, until he reduced to less than ten. He said, just like that is a dividing wall for the Sabbath. Rebbi Zeˋira said, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish agrees that on the Sabbath, a symbolic fence does not save more than ten<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote">The string represents a gate, not a wall.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai said, the Mishnah says so: “One may surround by three ropes one higher than the other.” If you say that the string saves, one rope would be sufficient<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote">The caravan clearly wants more space for animals and people than 10 by 10 cubits; if they park some camel loads at the four corners of their camp, they need a surrounding wall, not a surrounding gate. If it is possible to symbolize a wall with one rope, why does one need three?</i>.
Rebbi Jonah said: Rebbi Hoshaia asked, what do you say<sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows the parallel sources. איתאמר is Babli spelling.</i> about that symbolic fence, on top or at the sides<sup class="footnote-marker">265</sup><i class="footnote">If four poles are put up at the four sides of the vineyard, does the string have to go from top to top, to create the impression of a gate, or is it strung at half height between the poles, to give the impression of a wall?</i>? If you say on top, so much more on the sides. If you say on the sides, then not on the top. If you say on the top, Rebbi Ḥaggai said it correctly<sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote">Since Mishnah 9 does not require the lowest rope to be within three hand-breadths of the ground, it is clear that even if the rows are strung high one needs three ropes rather than just one.</i>. If you say from the sides, Rebbi Ḥaggai did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote">If the rope must be low, the case of פֵּיאָה is different from that of the Mishnah.</i>. What do you want to say<sup class="footnote-marker">268</sup><i class="footnote">A technical term, indicating that the desired result can be inferred from both hypotheses. The inference here is that R. Ḥaggai is correct in both cases, since the Mishnah is formulated to apply to both of them.</i>, if on top, it must be on top. Or from the sides, it is on the sides. The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: Explain it if they were strung on forked poles<sup class="footnote-marker">269,</sup><i class="footnote">If the poles have separate hooks so that each side has a complete arrangement of poles and rope, then even R. Zeˋira will admit that the rope forms a valid enclosure for the Sabbath. In this case, by necessity the rope is on top only. In this, the Yerushalmi parallels the final explanation of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 11b" href="/Eruvin.11b">Babli 11b</a>, rejecting symbolic walls but accepting symbolic gates.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δίκρανον, τό.</i>.
Rebbi Zeˋira, Rebbi Eudaimon of Haifa, in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: In height even one hundred cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">If a rope is drawn from the top of one pole to the next, there are no height limitations (except, naturally, that it cannot be lower than 10 hand-breadths.)</i>. Rebbi Judan said, that is, for <i>kilaim</i>, but as regards the Sabbath, a symbolic fence should not be better than a beam. Rebbi Yose said (in the name of Rebbi)<sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote">An addition of the text here, missing in the parallels, to be deleted.</i>, it is the same for <i>kilaim</i> and Sabbath. According to Rebbi [Yose]<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote">Necessary addition from the parallels, missing here.</i>, what is the difference between a beam and a symbolic fence? A beam saves in one direction<sup class="footnote-marker">273</sup><i class="footnote">Beam or lath are needed only at the entrance to the alley.</i>; a symbolic fence does not save from one side unless it is used to close all four sides;. This follows what Rebbi Zeˋira said in the name of Rav Hamnuna: A symbolic fence does not save unless it seals all four sides<sup class="footnote-marker">274</sup><i class="footnote">The problem is not discussed in the Babli.</i>.
Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, it is forbidden to carry under the four-gated bowers<sup class="footnote-marker">276</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τετράπυλον, τό, “archway entered from four sides.” Bowers erected at the crossing of two paths, with only the posts and the connecting beams built, and the roof formed by plants climbing on the poles. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal considers these beams as קוֹרָה under which one may not carry in a dead-end street (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.26">Note 159</a>).</i> in vineyards<sup class="footnote-marker">277</sup><i class="footnote">The vineyard in question is not enclosed by a wall, or it is enclosed but too large (larger than 5000 square cubits) to be considered a courtyard for the rules of Sabbath; hence, one may carry under the roof of the bower but not outside.</i> since they form the end of the roof and the end of a roof cannot save as a symbolic fence. Rebbi Phineas said, a case came before Rebbi Jeremiah of four pillars and on them four beams<sup class="footnote-marker">278</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἐπιστύλιον, τό, “architrave.”</i>; he permitted to carry under them because of a symbolic fence. Rebbi Abun and Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">279</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Kilaim</i> “R. Bun and the rabbis.” S. Liebermann points out that in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 11:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.11.4.5"><i>Terumot</i> 11:4 (Note 88</a>) for the Leiden text “R. Abun asked (verb in plural)” the Rome ms. reads: “R. Abun and R. Abin asked”. This probably should be read here also.</i> asked before Rebbi Zeˋira, does a symbolic fence save in a <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">280</sup><i class="footnote">The hut one builds to live in on the festival of Tabernacles must have roofing of plant material. The walls may be made of any material. The question is whether a virtual wall is permitted.</i>? He said to them, a symbolic fence saves in a <i>sukkah</i>. Does the end of the roofing save in a <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">281</sup><i class="footnote">Since the roofing must have some support, the four corner posts of the <i>sukkah</i> must have some connection on which the roofing may rest. Can these connections be considered as <i>peah</i>, following R. Jeremiah in the preceding case?</i>? He said to them, the end of the roofing does not save in a <i>sukkah</i>. What is the difference between them? One is made for the purpose, the other one is [not]<sup class="footnote-marker">281</sup><i class="footnote">Since the roofing must have some support, the four corner posts of the <i>sukkah</i> must have some connection on which the roofing may rest. Can these connections be considered as <i>peah</i>, following R. Jeremiah in the preceding case?</i> made for the purpose. Rebbi Abbahu said, all these things<sup class="footnote-marker">283</sup><i class="footnote">The rulings of R. Zeˋira. Building a <i>sukkah</i> is a positive commandment but neglecting it is not punishable in court. But desecrating the Sabbath is a capital crime.</i> are for discussion’s sake, but as far as teaching, it is forbidden to teach it. If this is forbidden for the minor commandment of <i>sukkah</i>, so much more for the strict commandment of Sabbath! Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi Zeˋira: Is not the Tanna, according to whom a symbolic fence saves, Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri? He said to him, that is the Tanna<sup class="footnote-marker">284</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, in regard to the Sabbath this is the opinion of a minority of one, which cannot be operational practice.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One surrounds with sticks, on condition that between one stick and the next there be less than three hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of using horizontal ropes one may use vertical sticks following the same rules.</i>. They spoke about a caravan, the words of Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">He accepts the imitation of a fence by either horizontal or vertical components only for a caravan camping in an uninhabitated area; whereas the majority accepts the rules as universally valid.</i>, but the Sages say that the Sages mentioned a caravan only as an example. Any partition which is not woof and warp is no partition, the words of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah; but the Sages say, one of these two<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose ben R. Jehudah disagrees with his father and rejects any fence not showing material in two directions perpendicular to one another.</i>. Of four matters they freed a military camp<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In wartime.</i>: One may bring wood from anywhere<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">They can collect fire wood from anywhere and it is not considered robbery.</i>, and they are not liable for washing their hands<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Before eating profane food (with bread).</i>, and from <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Provisions delivered to the army by not strictly observant persons may be eaten by strictly observant soldiers without setting heave of the Tithe apart, since it is only a suspicion, not a fact, that tithes were not taken from the provisions; cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Demay.</i></i>, and from making an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">In a camp in a <i>karmelit</i> situation one may carry freely once the boundary fence was established without going through an <i>eruv</i> ceremony as explained in Chapter 3.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One surrounds with sticks, on condition,” etc. One surrounds with sticks for a caravan, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. This implies that an individual needs woof and warp<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose ben R. Jehudah disagrees with his father and rejects any fence not showing material in two directions perpendicular to one another.</i>. So Rebbi Jehudah differentiates between an individual and a caravan; do the rabbis not differentiate between an individual and a caravan<sup class="footnote-marker">285</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah 10 the majority permits walls of sticks also for individuals; do they never differentiate between rules for individuals and for groups?</i>? Let us hear from the following:<sup class="footnote-marker">286</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).2.3">Tosephta 2:3</a>.</i> “If a caravan rested on a hill ten [hand-breadths] high, or in a depression ten [hand-breadths] deep<sup class="footnote-marker">287</sup><i class="footnote">Since these places are naturally separated from their surroundings, they do not need to be fenced in to become separate domains for the Sabbath.</i>, or in a garden fenced in by a stone wall, one carries in it even if it is a <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">288</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kor</i> is a measure of dry volume, of 30 <i>se’ah</i>. What it really should have said is “<i>bet</i> <i>kor</i>”, the area which can be sown with a <i>kor</i> of seed grain. Based on the standard definition of a <i>bet se’ah</i> as 2500 square cubits, a <i>bet</i> <i>kor</i> is 75’000 square cubits.</i> or two <i>kor</i>, (one carries in it up to two <i>bet se’ah</i>)<sup class="footnote-marker">289</sup><i class="footnote">The clause in parentheses is not in the Tosephta. Since it contradicts the statements preceding and following it should be deleted. One may conjecture that the sentence originally was a marginal gloss by a reader who did not understand the thrust of the argument, which then was copied into the text.<br>Two <i>bet</i> <i>seah</i> is the maximal area allowed for carrying on the Sabbath in a corral which is not used for human dwelling. But the camping caravan is temporary home to the travelers, therefore its domain is used for human dwelling.</i>, on condition that they do not leave an area of two <i>bet se’ah</i> unoccupied.” Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥinena: even with camel saddles, even with tubs. The word of Rav Huna implies, only by humans. For Rebbi Abba said in the name of Rav Ḥuna, to one or two one gives two <i>bet se’ah</i>, to three one gives six <i>bet se’ah</i>, from there on by proportion<sup class="footnote-marker">290</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of the Tosephta is qualified (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 16b" href="/Eruvin.16b">Babli 16b</a>). As noted in the next paragraph, groups of 3 or more people are defined as caravans. A caravan of <i>n</i> travelers can spend the Sabbath in a camp measuring <i>2n bet</i> <i>se’ah</i> (in fact, it can be 2(<i>n+</i>1)-ε since an additional (2-ε) <i>bet</i> <i>se’ah</i> is authorized in the Tosephta.)</i>.
A caravan is no less than three people<sup class="footnote-marker">291</sup><i class="footnote">In the language of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 16b" href="/Eruvin.16b">Babli 16b</a> three people travelling together automatically form a caravan.</i>. The Gentile does not complement a caravan<sup class="footnote-marker">292</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is not obligated to keep the Sabbath he cannot be a mitigating influence.</i>. Does an underage person complement a caravan<sup class="footnote-marker">293</sup><i class="footnote">He is not obligated now but his parents are obligated to teach him the laws of the Sabbath. The question is not resolved.</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">294</sup><i class="footnote">This is copied word by word in Chapter 6, discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>. The first case refers to the situation here. If two people were in an uninhabited area at the start of the Sabbath and enclosed an area of two <i>bet</i> <i>se’ah</i>, if on the Sabbath a Gentile came and enlarged that area they could not carry in the enlarged area since it already was forbidden to them at the start of the Sabbath (a matter of controversy in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 17a" href="/Eruvin.17a">Babli, 17a</a>). The next case belongs to Chapter 6. People in adjacent courtyards made separate <i>eruvim</i>. If on the Sabbath a Gentile opened a passage between the two domains, they continue using their courtyards since the legal status cannot be changed on the Sabbath even though if the passage had existed before the Sabbath they would not have been able to have separate <i>eruvim</i>. In these situations the Gentile is mentioned not because he is not obligated but because he is able to do work forbidden to Jews on the Sabbath.</i> How is this? If there were two and they established one <i>eruv</i>, and a Gentile came and added; the Sabbath already had started in prohibition. If there were three but they established two <i>eruvim</i> and a Gentile came and opened; the Sabbath already had started in permission. <sup class="footnote-marker">295</sup><i class="footnote">“Ox head” or “top of the line” means “vertex of a triangle” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:9:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.9.5"><i>Kilaim</i> 1:9 Note 202</a>, 2:7 <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.11">Note 101</a>). The configuration envisaged seems to be that of three triangular domains sharing a common vertex. By the rules of <i>Kilaim</i>, fields sown with different crops but sharing only a vertex do not need any real or symbolic separation. The question is raised whether the same holds true for the rules of the Sabbath. Since the question is not answered, the details of the configuration are not discussed.</i> Rebbi Redifa said that Rebbi Nisa asked, may they have three ox heads so that each of them may enter the two <i>se’ah</i> of another?
Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rav Ḥasdai: Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah follows the argument of his father and disagrees with his father. The argument of his father, about an individual more than two <i>bet se’ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">296</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:3-10:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.3-10.6">Mishnah 10</a> they agree that an individual only may fence in an area appropriate for a caravan only by a fence with both vertical and horizontal material.</i>. And disagrees with his father, about a caravan less than two <i>bet se’ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">297</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah only says that the one-directional fence is authorized only for a caravan, which gets several <i>bet se’ah</i>. It is asserted here that for smaller domains R. Jehudah authorizes one-directional fences for individuals while it is clear from the Mishnah that his son never admits them. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 17a" href="/Eruvin.17a">Babli 17a</a></i> Did we not hear that Rebbi Jehudah said, an individual for more than two <i>bet se’ah</i> needs woof and warp<sup class="footnote-marker">298</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:3-10:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.3-10.6">Mishnah 10</a>.</i>? Let us hear from the following, that Rebbi Aḥa the Tanna, Rebbi Ḥanin, Rebbi Yose in the name of Rav Sheshet: Just as they differ here so they are differing about <i>kilaim</i>. And in <i>Kilaim</i> not even if it is a <i>bet rova</i><sup class="footnote-marker">299</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>rova</i>ˋ is a quarter <i>qab</i>, a <i>qab</i> being a sixth of a <i>se’ah</i>. If they disagree for <i>kilaim</i> it follows that R. Jehudah must permit one-directional fences for small domains.</i>?
How many form a camp? Rebbi Ḥananiah said, one hundred. <i>Gideon came with the hundred men who were with him to the border of the camp</i><sup class="footnote-marker">300</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 7:19" href="/Judges.7.19"><i>Jud</i>. 7:19</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abuna asked, if it had said, “the camp of a hundred men who were with him” it would be acceptable<sup class="footnote-marker">301</sup><i class="footnote">Since the camp was the enemy’s the quote does not prove anything.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, ten: <i>David’s servants came and spoke to Nabal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">302</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 25:9" href="/I_Samuel.25.9"><i>1S</i>. 25:9</a>. It had been stated in v. 5 that they were ten.</i>, etc. What means <i>and they rested</i><sup class="footnote-marker">302</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 25:9" href="/I_Samuel.25.9"><i>1S</i>. 25:9</a>. It had been stated in v. 5 that they were ten.</i>? Rebbi Justus bar Shune said, they made a camp. Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi said, ten: <i>Until the camp was large like God’s camp</i><sup class="footnote-marker">303</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 12:22" href="/I_Chronicles.12.22"><i>1Chr</i>. 12:22</a>.</i>. How many are “God’s camp”? Ten<sup class="footnote-marker">304</sup><i class="footnote">The source of this statement is unknown. Rashba col. 124, who quotes this statement, does not comment on it.</i>. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah: 12’000 who are marching off as camp of Israel in a war of choice are permitted to rob moist wood but are forbidden to rob dry wood. Those who march out in a war of obligation are permitted to rob both dry and moist wood<sup class="footnote-marker">305</sup><i class="footnote">Details of the statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:3-10:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.3-10.6">Mishnah 10</a>. The Babli disagrees about the kind of wood, 17a, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 2:6" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).2.6">Tosephta 2:6</a>.<br>The source of the number 12’000 is unknown. In <i>Midrash</i> <i>Shemuel</i> (ed. Buber 23(9)) the quote is “12’000 like the camp of Midyan.” This cannot be correct since the escapees from Gideon’s attack were about 15’000 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 8:10" href="/Judges.8.10"><i>Jud</i>. 8:10</a>). Our text is confirmed by R. Ḥananel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 17a" href="/Eruvin.17a"><i>ad</i> 17a</a>).</i>. Rebbi Daniel the son of Rav Qatina in the name of Rav Ḥuna: If they were made into bundles it is forbidden. So far if it was not close to a forest; even if it was close to a forest. So far if it was not close to a spring; even if it was close to a spring. <sup class="footnote-marker">306</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:3-10:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.3-10.6">Mishnah 10</a> that in a camp one is not liable for <i>demay</i>. It is specified that food delivered to the camp is not liable even in a town but food acquired in town remains liable even if taken out to camp.</i> If they brought it with them to a town, they already are freed from liability. What they took out with them from a town already is liable for <i>demay</i>. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Abbahu, Rebbi Ḥizqiah said in the name of Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi: <i>Demay</i> was put in order or not in order<sup class="footnote-marker">307</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 2:7:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.2.7.6"><i>Šabbat</i> 2, Note 238</a>.</i>.
“And from making an <i>eruv</i>”. Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi said, this refers to <i>eruv</i> of courtyards<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">In a camp in a <i>karmelit</i> situation one may carry freely once the boundary fence was established without going through an <i>eruv</i> ceremony as explained in Chapter 3.</i>. But <i>eruv</i> of ranges is a matter of biblical law<sup class="footnote-marker">308</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 17b" href="/Eruvin.17b">Babli 17b</a>. It is agreed that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:29" href="/Exodus.16.29"><i>Ex</i>. 16:29</a> implies that leaving one’s place on the Sabbath, even if one does not carry anything, is prohibited. It is only the definition of “one’s place” which is a matter of rabbinic interpretation.</i>. Are there courtyards in a camp? To disassemble<sup class="footnote-marker">309</sup><i class="footnote">It is unclear what this means. Meïri (79b), Rashba (col. 125) and Ritba (28a) read “to permit”.</i> tents in a camp, as it was stated: “<sup class="footnote-marker">310</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 2:5-6" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).2.5-6">Tosephta 2:5–6</a>, partially quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 17a" href="/Eruvin.17a">Babli 17a</a>. In the Tosephta (ed. Liebermann p. 93) 3 out of 4 sources read: Tents in a camp <i>do not need</i> an <i>eruv</i>, tents of a caravan <i>need</i> an <i>eruv</i>. While the Medieval authors agree in substance with the majority of the Tosephta mss., the explicit quotes of Meïri and Rashba (loc. cit. Note 309) confirm the Yerushalmi text; only Ritba reads the Tosephta text in the Yerushalmi.<br>Maimonides in <i>Hilkhot</i> <i>Eruvin</i> 1:3 follows the Yerushalmi text but in <i>Hilkhot Melakhim</i> 6:13 he only requires that the camp be enclosed by a fence 10 hand-breadths high without <i>eruv</i> following the Babli. There is no reason to emend the text.</i> Tents in a camp need an <i>eruv</i>, tents of a caravan do not need an <i>eruv</i>. “Jehudah ben Tema says, also they may camp anywhere and at the place where they are killed they are buried, lest you say that they should be treated like war<sup class="footnote-marker">311</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πόλεμος, ὁ. This must refer to Gentile fallen soldiers.</i> dead.” There are Tannaim who state that it is forbidden to remove them; there are Tannaim who state that it is permitted to remove them. He who said that it is permitted to remove them, if they are together<sup class="footnote-marker">312</sup><i class="footnote">A mass grave. In <i>Masekhet</i> <i>Semaḥot</i> 14:4: “In times of war there is no permanence to graves”; it is implied that they always may be re-buried in a Jewish cemetary.</i>; he who said that it is forbidden to remove them, if they are dispersed. Just as they are exempt from four matters when they are marching out, so they are exempt from four matters when they are returning. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun understood this from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">313</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 7:3" href="/Judges.7.3"><i>Jud</i>. 7:3</a>.</i>: <i>Anybody who is fearful and anxious should return and sneak away from Mount Gilead</i>. Why did they have to return sneaking? Because of the enemies<sup class="footnote-marker">314</sup><i class="footnote">Since there always is danger, there is no reason to abrogate the exemption.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav: Just as they are exempt from four matters when they are marching out, so they are exempt from four matters when they are returning.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One makes planks for wells, four double columns<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The word seems to be a Greek-Hebrew hybrid, δύο-עֲמוּדִים “double column”. The circular well is in the middle of a square; at each corner one erects planks 10 hand-breadths high and on each side 6 wide. Since from each side one sees two planks, these are “4 looking like 8”. Since the opening between two planks, space in excess of the width of 6 cows for R. Meïr or eight for R. Jehudah, is more than what is permitted as opening of a wall of a private domain, special rules are needed to permit grazing animals to drink from a well. Even though the meadows on which they are grazing is public domain or <i>karmelit</i>, the well or cistern is private domain if it is at least 10 hand-breadths deep. Therefore one has to see to it that the animals drink in a private domain. R. Meïr requires a straight plank (of indeterminate width) after a 5 cubits opening.</i> which look like eight, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Meïr says eight which look like twelve, eight double columns and four simple ones, their height ten [hand-breadths], and their width six, and their thickness arbitrary. And between them the width of two teams of three cattle each, the words of Rebbi Meïr; Rebbi Jehudah says of four each, tied together rather than loose, one entering and one leaving<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As explained at the end of the discussion of this Mishnah, the space between two planks can be 10 cubits for R. Meïr and almost 14 for R. Jehudah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One makes planks for wells,” etc. Since what is breached is more than what is standing he makes double columns but not simple ones, which proves by itself that it is because of access to the well. Rebbi Zeˋira in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: You are reaching here what are the limits of domains of the Sabbath, for if he did so at (one)<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">With the later prints of the Yerushalmi one has to read here אַחֵר “another”. If one puts up four double-planks anywhere else so that the opening is wider than the standing walls it does not mean anything. If within this space one throws something more than 4 cubits it is throwing in the public domain and a Sabbath violation. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 20a" href="/Eruvin.20a">Babli 20a</a> reads differently.</i> place and then threw there four cubits in the public domain he would be liable. Rebbi Yose said, since you were lenient with him about what is breached may be more than what is standing, you are strict with him in another matter since everywhere else one makes a plank of four hand- breadths but here he makes a plank of six hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Both aspects, the corner planks and their width, are required to make it clear that these rules are valid exclusively for water sources but nothing else.</i>.
Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: They permitted planks for wells only for pilgrims<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The three yearly pilgrimages to the Temple. This implies that after the destruction of the Temple all the rules of the present Chapter became obsolete. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 20b" href="/Eruvin.20b">Babli 20b</a> as accepted doctrine.</i>. Rav Abin said, at the time of pilgrimage<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The suspension of the universal requirement that “what is standing must be more than what is breached” is authorized only temporarily for the needs of actual pilgrims.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: Because of the pilgrims<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">But along the pilgrim’s route from Babylonia to Jerusalem the installations are permanent.</i>. Rebbi Ezra said before Rebbi Mana, the Mishnah implies that one makes planks for wells in the present time<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah is formulated in the present it must be valid in the present, after the destruction of the Temple and the end of pilgrimages.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 10:13:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.10.13.1">Mishnah 10:12</a>.</i>, “one fills from the cistern of the diaspora with a wheel on the Sabbath, from the large cistern, etc.” Why “may one fill from the cold well<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">While the cisterns mentioned in the Mishnah were on the Temple Mount, the well was somewhere on the pilgrims’ route. Using a mechanical contraption is rabinically forbidden on the holiday since one might be tempted to fix it in case it broke on the holiday.</i> with a wheel on the holiday”? But at the time when Israel came from the diaspora and camped at this well, the prophets among them stipulated that that one could fill from the cold well with a wheel on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 104b" href="/Eruvin.104b">Babli 104b</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 8:22" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).8.22">Tosephta 8:22</a>.</i>. They did not permit all cold wells, only this well at which they camped. As you are saying there, what was permitted was permitted, and here what was permitted was permitted. Rebbi Eudaimon from Haifa instructed in Haifa, Rebbi Jeremiah instructed in Ḥelef about planks for wells in the present time<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian point of view is rejected.</i>.
They wanted to say that Rebbi Meïr would admit to Rebbi Jehudah, Rebbi Jehudah will not admit to Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Jehudah permits a wider opening, he cannot agree to R. Meïr’s lower limit. But R. Meïr could agree that R. Jehudah is correct in principle; only for practical reasons he prescribes a narrower opening.</i>. Rebbi Meïr will admit to Rebbi Jehudah? Does not Rebbi Meïr have simple poles, and you are saying so? If you are making double columns but no straight poles, he<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">An uninformed person who sees the surroundings of a well turned into a private domain by 4 double poles would think that any domain might be turned into one where carrying is permitted by the same device. But since he sees that at regular intervals simple planks are required he will inquire about the rules and be told that in all other cases the length of the walls has to exceed the lengths of the openings.</i> would think, perhaps a partition for the Sabbath is made in this way; he would make it in other places and become liable. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥinena: Neither of them yields to the other. Rebbi Mana said, even though Rebbi Yose (about)<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">For דכי read רבי “my teacher Rebbi Yose said”.</i> did not say this, he said something similar. Rebbi Bun and Rebbi Bun<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Read “R. Abun and R. Abin”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:9:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.9.6">Chapter 1, Note 279</a>.</i> asked before Rebbi Zeˋira: How far away does a double column have to be so it would not need a straight [plank]<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">When would R. Meïr dispense with straight planks? <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 19b" href="/Eruvin.19b">Babli 19b</a>.</i>? He told him, less than three [hand-breadths] is considered closed, more than six is far away<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">As long as one can disregard a distance of 3-ε on each side, nothing additional is required.</i>. Therefore we have to consider from three to four. If you would say that Rebbi Meïr yields to Rebbi Jehudah, should he not make a double column far away and not need a simple [plank]<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This quote of R. Zeˋira’s explanation is R. Mana’s proof that R. Zeˋira also holds that R. Meïr cannot agree with R. Jehudah in principle since he would have to indicate how it would be possible to make the enclosure without straight planks.</i>? Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, Rebbi Meïr requires simple [planks] only according to Rebbi Jehudah’s method<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">He wants to split the Mishnah into two parts and indicate that straight planks are needed only for a large width following R. Jehudah.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, even according to his own method, since in any case what is standing is not more than what is breached. Since what is standing should be more than what is breached it needs a simple [plank]<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah about enclosing a well in any case contradicts our principle that “what is standing must be more than what is breached”, R. Meïr’s insistence on straight planks is one of principle, not of practical consideration as suggested at the start of this discussion.</i>.
“If one big stone was there one sees that if it is split and there be six [hand-breadths] in both directions it is considered a double column.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 1:12" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).1.12">Tosephta 1:12</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 19b" href="/Eruvin.19b">Babli 19b</a>. If the stone has a square base and each side is six hand-breadths long it is counted as double column.</i>” Some Tannaim state, if it is hollowed out. They wanted to say that he who says, if it is hollowed out, measures from the inside while he who says, if it is split, measures from the outside. Rebbi Yose said, would you not let it stand if it was like garlic skin<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah does not prescribe depth of columns or planks there is no practical difference whether one measures from the outside or from the inside of an arbitrarily thin membrane.</i>? This implies that both he who says that it is split as also he who says it is hollowed out measures from the inside. He who says, if it is hollowed out, if it is round<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If the base of the stone is circular the radius, not the diameter, must be 6 hand-breadth (1 cubit). In Babli and Tosephta this is an explicit statement by R. Ismael ben R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa.</i>; he who says, if it is split, if it is square. You should know that it is so since one Tanna said to the other, the only difference between my formulation and yours is that you are speaking of a round one but I about a square one.
If there was a ditch ten [hand-breadths] deep and four wide, and it extends six in each direction<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">At a corner two ditches, 1 cubit long, <sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>/<sub>3</sub> cubits wide and 1<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>/<sub>3</sub> cubits deep are the replacement of a double column erected there.</i>, it is judged to be a double column. Not only a ditch but also a heap<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">A rudimentary wall also counts if it has the necessary dimensions.</i>. Partially a ditch and partially a heap? If there were five sticks with less than three [hand-breadths] between them which together cover six [hand-breadths] in each direction, they are judged to be a double column<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since any opening of less than 3 hand-breadths is disregarded, the five sticks (two at each side and one at the right angle) stand for solid walls of 1 cubit length in each direction.</i>. If there were three<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Three sticks at ABC form an isosceles right angled triangle with vertex B and edge length 6 hand-breadths.</i> and he tied bast on top, would this save as symbolic fence? Does not a symbolic fence save only if it is proven from each side<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:9:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.9.5">Chapter 1 Note 274</a>.</i>? Then what was tied around is as if blocked. Similar to what you are saying there, one sees that if it is split and there would be six [hand-breadths] in both directions it is considered a double column, could you say the same here? There would you not let it stand if it was like garlic skin; here what can you say? If you would say so you would abrogate the rules of symbolic fences<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">There are no exceptions to the rule indicated in the preceding Note.</i>.
A wall, and a tree, and a copse of reeds are counted as double columns<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 15a" href="/Eruvin.15a">Babli 15a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 19b" href="/Eruvin.19b">19b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 24b" href="/Shabbat.24b"><i>Šabbat</i> 24b</a>.</i>. If they grew by themselves one may carry there up to an area of two <i>bet se’ah</i>. If he planted them with his hands one may carry there even one or two <i>bet kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.10.2">Chapter 1, Note 288</a>.</i>. If there was one large well and he made [the double columns] for one half but not for the other half. For the half for which he made it it is permitted, for the half for which he did not make it it is forbidden. If there were two, could the space in-between be considered for both sides? What is his problem? If both were used to fill; but if only one was used to fill it is usable for both sides<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">If separate symbolic enclosures were made for two adjacent wells and one set of double columns serves for both enclosures, it is clear that one may not simultaneously draw water from both wells since this would use the middle double pillars in two contradictory ways. (This implies that they were double, not triple, i. e., shaped like a Γ but not a Τ.) But if at one time only one well is used, the next time the other well may be used, even on the same Sabbath.</i>.
From a courtyard open to planks one carries from the courtyard to the planks but not from the planks to the courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 1:15" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).1.15">Tosephta 1:15</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 20a" href="/Eruvin.20a">Babli 20a</a> disagree with this Yerushalmi in principle. The courtyard is walled in according to the rules that what is standing must be more than what is open; there one carries according to the general rules. It is open to a place where carrying is permitted only for the needs of animals; this place is an appendix of the courtyard but the courtyard is not an appendix to the place.</i>. Therefore two are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">If two different courtyards open on the well, the well cannot belong to two different domains; therefore it belongs to neither of them.</i>. Rebbi Abba said, not only two courtyards but even a single courtyard and in it two houses<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">And each of the houses has a backdoor opening on the well.</i>. They thought to say if they did not make an <i>eruv</i>; therefore if they made an <i>eruv</i> it would be permitted. Rebbi Daniel the son of Rav Qatina in the name of Rav Ḥuna: even if they made an <i>eruv</i> it is forbidden, for the <i>eruv</i> does not make them one<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The permission to use the well is given only if there is a single opening from the courtyard to the well.</i>. Rav Ḥuna follows his opinion, as they disagreed: An alley in which on one side dwelt a Gentile and on the other a Jew, Rav Ḥuna in the name of Rav: even if they made an <i>eruv</i> through doors the Gentile prohibits it for them<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Gentile is not subject to the laws of the Sabbath he cannot agree to a change of status of the alley for the Sabbath even if otherwise the <i>eruv</i> was totally conforming to the rules.</i>. Abba bar bar Ḥana in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, even if they made the <i>eruv</i> through windows the Gentile does not prohibit for them<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion the Gentile is irrelevant for the rules of the Sabbath even if the houses which are united by the <i>eruv</i> share only windows, not full door openings.</i>. Even if Rav Ḥuna would hold with Rebbi Joḥanan that there it is permitted, here he has to agree that it is forbidden.
