There, maybe tomorrow the well will run dry and then he would think that perhaps an <i>eruv</i> is efficient for the planks of wells when an <i>eruv</i> is not efficient for the planks of wells<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition of a well which is accessible from two different courtyards is based on a practical problem rather than a legal principle; one has to avoid giving the impression that courtyards joined in an <i>eruv</i> have any impact on the rules of wells surrounded by double columns. If the well runs dry before the Sabbath the double columns lose all their meaning.</i>.
Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, they permitted the planks of wells only to draw water<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 21a" href="/Eruvin.21a">Babli 21a</a>.</i>. If water came on the Sabbath, the Sabbath already came with prohibition. If the well dried out, the Sabbath already came with permission<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 20a" href="/Eruvin.20a">Babli 20a</a> disagrees in both cases.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said that Rebbi Nasa asked, may one carry between the planks<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">He questions R, Yose ben R. Abun’s statement.</i>? We should say that if there are four hand-breadths between one and the other it is like a prohibited threshold, otherwise like a permitted threshold, permitted for both domains; only one should not exchange<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.26">Chapter 1, Note 162</a>.</i>.
It was stated, there are about ten cubits, the words of Rebbi Meïr; Rebbi Jehudah says, like thirteen or fourteen minus<sup class="footnote-marker">2,</sup><i class="footnote">As explained at the end of the discussion of this Mishnah, the space between two planks can be 10 cubits for R. Meïr and almost 14 for R. Jehudah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Both R. Meïr and R. Jehudah presume that an average cow is 1.5 cubits wide and between the two teams one leaves 1 cubit of space. R. Meïr permits space for 6 cows, 9 cubits, + 1 cubit. R. Jehudah for 8 cows, 12 cubits, + 1 cubit, for a width of 13 cubits. He admits a wider room for moving of the cows and accepts any distance less than 14 cubits.<br>פרא is Latin <i>parum</i> “too little, not enough”. In <i>Demay</i> 5:2 the expression is פָּרָא צִיבְחַר “less a little bit.”</i>: a cubit, a half, and one cubit interspace<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> It is permitted to make them close to the well on condition that the head and most of the cow be inside when she drinks. It is permitted to put them arbitrarily far away on condition to add planks<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The distances between two planks cannot be greater than those described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>, 5 cubits for R. Meïr and 14 for R. Jehudah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “It is permitted to make them close to the well,”<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i> etc. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac: This is its measure<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah prescribes that the poles must be far enough away that a cow’s head and most of its body be inside. This is part of the rabbinic institutions, it is permitted even for camels which are much longer and must be observed even for a herd of goats which are much shorter.</i>. With this measure even though the camel is entirely outside it is permitted. Less than this measure even if the kid goat is entirely inside it is forbidden. It was stated: Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said, enough for a camel and its camel driver<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">A minimal domain around a well may be used for camels only if it is wide enough to allow the camel driver to be completely inside with his camel.</i>. Does he disagree? In every case where the cow stretches its neck, the camel bends its neck. Why do you want this<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The explanation is not satisfactory since neither cow nor camel are required to observe the distinction between public and private domain; this is only for humans. Why is space for the camel driver needed?</i>? Could not the cow stand in the public domain and eat in a private domain? Rebbi Yose said, there it is about one who [provides]<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Reading דמספיק for דמפסיק “who interrupts”.</i> for it, but here if it eats by itself<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The camel needs its camel driver while the cow does not need its master. Therefore humans are not mentioned by RR. Meïr and Jehudah who define everything referring to cattle. Babli 20b.</i>.
In Rebbi Meïr’s opinion, double columns and single ones; in Rebbi Jehudah’s opinion double columns but no single ones<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">If the area around the well is extended, then the additional planks required by the Mishnah are simple planks of arbitrary size for R. Meïr but double planks (2 times 6 hand-breadths straight) for R. Jehudah.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah says, up to two <i>bet se’ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion the maximal size of domain for which double planks are authorized are the standard 5’000 square cubits.</i>. They said to him, two <i>bet se’ah</i> were said only for a garden patch<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">An irrigated vegetable garden.</i> and a corral<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">For cattle.</i>. But if there was a sheep-pen, or a sheep corral, or a backyard, or a front yard, even five <i>bet kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>kor</i> is 30 <i>seˋah</i>; a <i>bet kor</i> 75’000 square cubits.</i>, even ten <i>bet kor</i> are permitted and it is permitted to put them arbitrarily far away on condition to add planks<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The distances between two planks cannot be greater than those described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>, 5 cubits for R. Meïr and 14 for R. Jehudah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> According to Rebbi Jehudah, is the well included in the measure of two [<i>bet</i>] <i>se’ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Who in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> restricts the area around the well to this size.</i>? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 1:13" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).1.13">Tosephta 1:13</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 22a" href="/Eruvin.22a">Babli 22a</a>.</i>: If a well is two <i>bet se’ah</i> one may not go farther away than for the head and most of the body of a cow. This implies that the well is included in the measure of two [<i>bet</i>] <i>se’ah</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a public road interrupts them he should divert it around it, but the Sages say that this is unnecessary<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The first clause in this sentence is R. Jehudah’s (and is stated as such in the independent Mishnah mss., the Mishnah in the Babli, and the quote in the Halakhah). He holds that enclosures which trespass on a public road are unauthorized.</i>. One makes planks both for a public cistern, a public well, and a private well, but for a private cistern one makes a partition of ten hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since the double planks are authorized only for the benefit of animals, once a cistern runs dry the double planks lose their utility and it would be forbidden to carry there. Therefore for a non-public cistern he requires a regular enclosure where one may carry regardless of the amount of water available.</i>, the words of Rebbi Aqiba. Rebbi Jehudah ben Bava said, one makes planks only for a public well; for everything else one makes a belt<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Possibly by the standards of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.9.1">Mishnah 1:9</a>.</i> ten hand-breadths high.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “Rebbi Jehudah says, up to two <i>bet se’ah</i>,”<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the quote is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>, the discussion is about <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.4.1">Mishnah 4</a> where R. Jehudah prohibits using part of a thoroughfare as enclosure of a well.</i> etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the argument of Rebbi Jehudah is inverted, as we have stated there: “And similarly one carries under panelled bridges on the Sabbath, the words of Rebbi Jehudah, but the Sages forbid it<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:1:2-5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.1.2-5.3">Mishnah 9:5</a>, explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.3">Chapter 1, Note 40</a>.</i>”. Rebbi Eleazar said, it is not inverted. Rebbi Jeremiah said, for he is of Rav Jehudah’s opinion, as Rav Jehudah said, that is if it is open to a domain of difficult access. But if it was open to the public domain it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">A quote from the end of Chapter 9. Since R. Jehudah in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:1:2-5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.1.2-5.3">Mishnah 9:5</a> accepts virtual walls only for <i>karmelit</i>, that Mishnah is consistent with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.4.1">Mishnah 2:4</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, everywhere Rebbi Eleazar seeks support from the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Yebamot</i>, end of Halakhah 2:3.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, how does one permit public domain? Rebbi Jehudah says, a lath on each side or a beam at each side but the Sages say a lath and a beam on one side and he shall make a door frame on the other side. And here he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">There he permits turning parts of a public road, i. e., an alley open at both ends, into a private domain but here he forbids. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 22a" href="/Eruvin.22a">Babli 22a</a>.</i>? Here about ten [cubits], there about thirteen<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">A roadway cannot have the status of an alley if it is wider than 10 cubits (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.1">Mishnah 1:1</a>) but the enclosure of a well can be 13.</i>. The words of the rabbis disagree. Rebbi Aḥa said, Rav Hoshaia asked my father, how large can the breach of an alley be in the opinion of Rebbi Jehudah? And he told him, thirteen [cubits]. And here he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was stated just before that only a breach up to 10 cubits is acceptable.</i>? The Rabbis of Caesarea say, there what is standing is more than what is breached but here what is breached is more than what is standing<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The two situations cannot be compared. 13 cubits are permitted only in the situation of the enclosure of a well which is made for the benefit of animals, not humans.</i>.
Paragraph. “Rebbi Jehudah says<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The first clause in this sentence is R. Jehudah’s (and is stated as such in the independent Mishnah mss., the Mishnah in the Babli, and the quote in the Halakhah). He holds that enclosures which trespass on a public road are unauthorized.</i>, if a public road interrupts them,” etc. What is the difference between a public cistern and a private cistern<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since the double planks are authorized only for the benefit of animals, once a cistern runs dry the double planks lose their utility and it would be forbidden to carry there. Therefore for a non-public cistern he requires a regular enclosure where one may carry regardless of the amount of water available.</i>? A public cistern has publicity<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">It is common knowledge when a public cistern runs dry and the enclosure may not be used, whereas for a private cistern he might start using the enclosure and discover only belatedly that he was carrying there in violation of the Sabbath laws.</i>, a private cistern has no publicity. Then even for his well<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Why are enclosures permitted for private wells whose status is not monitored?</i>! But a public well can be expected to run dry, a private well is not expected to run dry<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Only public wells are subject to permanent overuse.</i>.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Amram, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: Practice follows Rebbi Jehudah ben Bava<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 23a" href="/Eruvin.23a">Babli 23a</a>.<br>Since R. Aqiba permits flimsy enclosures only for public cisterns and he otherwise treats cisterns and wells according to the same rules, he must refer to public wells in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah the father of Rebbi Mattaniah said, the Mishnah says so, “one makes planks for wells,” not for cisterns. But did we not state, “but for a private cistern one makes a partition of ten hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since the double planks are authorized only for the benefit of animals, once a cistern runs dry the double planks lose their utility and it would be forbidden to carry there. Therefore for a non-public cistern he requires a regular enclosure where one may carry regardless of the amount of water available.</i>, the words of Rebbi Aqiba”? But therefore we must deal with public cisterns, and similarly “one makes planks for wells,” for public wells.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> In addition, Rebbi Jehudah ben Bava said, if a garden patch or a corral are seventy cubits and a remainder by seventy cubits and a remainder surrounded by a wall ten hand-breadths high<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Since 70.71…<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup> = 5’000, the area enclosed is 2 <i>bet se’ah</i>. In an enclosed area of up to 2 <i>bet se’ah</i> one may carry. One of more than 2 <i>bet se’ah</i> may be turned into a domain for carrying only if it is used for human dwelling.</i> one carries inside on condition that it contain a watchman’s hut or a dwelling or be close to a village<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Within 70.71 cubits of the village.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “In addition, Rebbi Jehudah ben Bava said,” etc. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan, they inferred it from the courtyard of the Tabernacle: <i>the length of the courtyard 100 cubits wide 50 by 50</i><sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 27:18" href="/Exodus.27.18"><i>Ex</i>. 27:18</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 23b" href="/Eruvin.23b">Babli 23b</a>. The expression “50 by 50” is read as meaning that the standard measure of area shall be a square of 5’000 square cubits.</i>. 50 times 100 are 5’000. 70 by 70 are 5’000 minus 100, and we have stated “seventy cubits and a remainder.” And Samuel stated, they stated cubits and two thirds of a cubit. Seventy times two thirds and seventy times two thirds make 140 thirds each; 140 thirds and 140 thirds are 93⅓. There is missing from there 4/9 for the corner, there remain nineteen thirds minus one ninth. As we have stated, there is a slight difference which the Sages could not compute<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Since 70<sup>2</sup> = 4900 and 71<sup>2</sup>= 5041 the square root of 5’000 is between 70 and 71. In order to compute 70⅔<sup>2</sup> one uses the binomial formula in its geometric form: A square of sides 70⅔ is composed of a square of sides 70 + two rectangles 70 by ⅔ + a square of area 4/9 for a total of 49937/9. The difference to 5000 is 62/9 = 19/3 -1/9. The additional correction was a problem. Not only is the square root of 5000 irrational, not expressible as a fraction, but of the two square root algorithms used in Antiquity, the Babylonian, using a kind of Newton approximation, is an approximation from above and the Hellenistic, using an equivalent of modern continued fractions, starts with an approximation from above, when the legal situation here demands an approximation from below. 70⅔, is a reasonable rational approximation from below to √5000 since 707/9<sup>2</sup> already is &gt;5009. The second term of the continued fraction development is an approximation from below, 70140/197, not a practical expression. The systematic approximation of square roots from below which used to be taught in our schools is essentially dependent on the notion of place values underlying Indian- Arabic numerals. The first approximation of √5000 both in the Babylonian and the Hellenistic methods, 705/7, is an exceedingly good low-denominator approximation from above, (705/7)<sup>2</sup> = 500025/49, and therefore not usable in this context. The approximation of 70⅔ cubits allows a measuring error of 1 digit without exceeding √5000. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 4:9" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).4.9">Tosephta 4:9</a>.</i>.
In a corral which covers two <i>bet se’ah</i> one is permitted only to carry a distance of four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to areas enclosed by a makeshift fence such as is authorized for a caravan. The moment it is a full two <i>bet se’ah</i> one requires a wall or a fence with woof and warp (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.5.1">Mishnah 5</a>).</i>. If there were two, each one a <i>bet se’ah</i> minus four cubits; Rebbi Zeˋira in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: one carries in the second as complement of the first<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 93a" href="/Eruvin.93a">Babli 93a</a>. Two people enclosed adjacent areas with makeshift fences. R. La notes that they cannot combine their areas to one authorized for caravans, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:10:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.10.4">Chapter 1 Note 296</a>.</i>. Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, one may not carry in the second as complement of the first. Rebbi Zeˋira said, Rebbi La agrees that if there were three<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Three people camping together form a caravan and follow the rules explained in Chapter 1.</i>. Each of the three a <i>bet se’ah</i>, one agrees that one carries in the second as complement of the first<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since as member of a caravan he has two <i>bet se’ah</i> at his disposal.</i>.
A corral which covers up to two <i>bet se’ah</i>. Rebbi Abbahu said, since it can be permitted by a caravan, if somebody threw from the public domain into it he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the enclosure made by a caravan does not qualify as wall of a private domain under normal circumstances, the area becomes a private domain for the rules of the Sabbath.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac asked, (not after it was permitted) [if it was chosen by]<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses was originally written by the scribe, the text in brackets is the corrector’s. The original text is preferable.</i> the caravan it becomes forbidden in a prohibition; therefore here is no liability for a purification sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abbahu’s statement is refuted. Since the entire arrangement is rabbinical, no biblical liability is possible.</i>. By the same argument, an alley whose beam is higher than twenty cubits since it is qualified in the opinion of Rebbi Jehudah (more than thirteen)<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses should be deleted; it is appropriate only in the next sentence.<br>Since practice does not follow R. Jehudah, the rules of private domain cannot be applied to areas which may become private domains only according to him.</i>, if somebody threw from the public domain into it would he be liable? An alley which was breached more than ten [cubits], since a breach of an alley in the opinion of Rebbi Jehudah is more than thirteen, if somebody threw from the public domain into it would he be liable?
If a courtyard is open into a corral one may carry from the courtyard to the corral but not from the corral to the courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Courtyard and corral must have a common owner. Since the corral (enclosed by a makeshift fence) is an appendix to the courtyard but not vice-versa, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.1.8">Note 48</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.2.1">Mishnah 9:2</a>.</i>. Rebbi Judah from Anthodriya asked, may one carry from one corral to another corral through the courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since from courtyard to corral is permitted, may one move from one corral to the other without stopping in the courtyard? The question is not answered since if half of the way is forbidden the entire way cannot be permitted.</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.4.4">Chapter 1, Note 194</a>.</i> The opinion of Rebbi Jehudah seems inverted, as we have stated there, “what is a corral? Anything close to a village, the words of Rebbi Jehudah.” And here he says so? Rebbi Mana said, Rebbi Jehudah treated it as a house.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah says, even if it only contains a cistern, or an irrigation ditch, or a cave, one carries in it. Rebbi Aqiba says, even if none of these is there one carries in it on condition that it be seventy cubits and a remainder by seventy cubits and a remainder<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Since 70.71…<sup>2</sup> = 5’000, the area enclosed is 2 <i>bet se’ah</i>. In an enclosed area of up to 2 <i>bet se’ah</i> one may carry. One of more than 2 <i>bet se’ah</i> may be turned into a domain for carrying only if it is used for human dwelling.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.4.4">Chapter 1, Note 194</a>.</i> The opinion of Rebbi Jehudah seems inverted, as we have stated there, “what is a corral? Anything close to a village, the words of Rebbi Jehudah.” And here he says so? Rebbi Mana said, Rebbi Jehudah treated it as a house.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Eliezer says, if its length exceeds its width even by one cubit one may not carry there<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">His reason is unknown. The statement is rejected in the Babli.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, even if its length is twice its width one carries in there.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “Rebbi Jehudah says, even if it only contains a cistern,” etc. It was stated, “Rebbi Eliezer says, if its length exceeds its width even by one cubit one may not carry there<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">His reason is unknown. The statement is rejected in the Babli.</i>.” This disagrees with R. Jonathan<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Who said earlier (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.5.2">Note 81</a>) that one inferred from the courtyard of the Tabernacle whose sides were in ratio 1:2.</i>. “Rebbi Yose says, even if its length is twice its width one carries in there,” this agrees with Rebbi Jonathan. We also have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The reference is to a statement close to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 2:6" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).2.6">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 2:6</a>: “How much is a <i>bet rova`</i>? 10.5 cubits square, Rebbi Yose says even if its length is twice its width.” The quarter is a quarter <i>qav</i>, 1/24 of a <i>se’ah</i>. Therefore a <i>bet qav</i> = 2500/24 = 104.1667 square cubits or 10.206<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>, somewhat less than the 10.5<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup> given in the Tosephta. The quote shows that R. Yose consistently considers “squares” mentioned in legal contexts as measures of area, rather than geometric squares.</i>, “<i>Kilaim</i> in a quarter<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Read רובע for רובה.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, even if its length is twice its width.”

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Illai said, I heard from Rebbi Eliezer: Even if it is a <i>bet kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliezer never permitted carrying in a corral which does not contain a human dwelling and which is larger than two <i>bet se’ah</i>. If the entire area was fenced in and there was an area smaller than two <i>bet se’ah</i> somehow separated from the entire area, such as a hill, or a depression, or a cave, one may carry in that separate area because of the fence around the entire area.</i>, and I also heard from him that if one of the dwellers in a courtyard had forgotten to make an <i>eruv</i> he is forbidden to bring into or bring out of his house but to others it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Whereas for the majority nobody may carry in and out of the courtyard unless the non-participant annulled his rights (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.13">Chapter 1, Note 112</a>).</i>. I also heard from him that one may satisfy one’s obligation on Passover with white stone-crop<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A medicinal plant which he authorizes as “bitter herb” for the Seder night.</i>. I asked around among all his students to find support but did not find any<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore these statements are not authoritative.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “Rebbi Illai said, I heard from Rebbi Eliezer:” Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, this Mishnah is about two <i>bet seah</i> collected from the <i>bet kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliezer never permitted carrying in a corral which does not contain a human dwelling and which is larger than two <i>bet se’ah</i>. If the entire area was fenced in and there was an area smaller than two <i>bet se’ah</i> somehow separated from the entire area, such as a hill, or a depression, or a cave, one may carry in that separate area because of the fence around the entire area.</i>.
“And also I heard from him that if one of the dwellers in a courtyard had forgotten to make an <i>eruv</i> he is forbidden [to bring into] his house.” There it follows the rabbis, here Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbis in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.3.1">Mishnah 6:3</a> forbid his house to him and everybody else and permit other houses opening to the same courtyard to him and everybody else.</i>. The rabbis say, a person may annul his right to his house but not his right to his courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">This has to read: “The rabbis say, a person may annul his right to his courtyard but not his right to his house.” In the matter of <i>eruvin</i>, “annulling one’s right” means asking not to be counted. This permits the other parties to establish an <i>eruv</i> without his participation; he will have no right to use the <i>eruv</i>.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer says, just as a person may annul his right to his house so he may annul his right to his courtyard. According to Rebbi Eliezer, could he not be treated like a guest<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">An Aramaic word derived from Greek ξένος, ὁ “stranger”. A guest who has no property rights in the courtyard is irrelevant for the <i>eruv</i>.</i> and be permitted? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Rebbi Eliezer fined him<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The answer to the question in theory is positive but R. Eliezer holds that the person forgetting an <i>eruv</i> should be fined.</i>. Rebbi Shammai asked, according to Rebbi Eliezer, does one fine anybody in error about a matter of their words<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Since by biblical law one could carry in the common courtyard, R. Eliezer’s restriction seems to be contrary to the rules.</i>? Rebbi Abba the son of Rav Pappai asked, if somebody said, I am annulling the right to my house, would the rabbis agree? If somebody said, I am annulling the right to my courtyard, would Rebbi Eliezer agree?
“Also I heard from him that one may satisfy one’s obligation on Passover with white stone-crop<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A medicinal plant which he authorizes as “bitter herb” for the Seder night.</i>.” They were of the opinion to say, about white stone-crop. It was found stated, about all of them<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">All statements of R. Illay are unsupported.</i>.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> With anything one may make an <i>eruv</i> or participate except with water or salt<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">An עֵרוּב חֲצֵרוֹתwhich allows carrying in and to a multi-family courtyard is made by depositing food from all inhabitants of the courtyard in one of the houses belonging to it. An עֵרוּב תְּחוּמִים is made by a single person who wants to move his Sabbath domain in a certain direction by depositing food for a meal near the border of his regular Sabbath domain before the start of the Sabbath with the intention that the place of this food be the center of his Sabbath rest, which gives him 2’000 cubits in every direction from his new place. שִׁיתּוּף מְבוֹאוֹת makes a dead-end alley (duly marked by beam or lath) a common Sabbath domain of its dwellers by deposition of food common to inhabitants of the alley in one of the houses belonging to it. The rules for <i>eruv</i> of courtyards and participation of alleys are identical.</i>. Anything may be bought with money of [Second] Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Second Tithe is the property of the farmer and should be eaten in purity in Jerusalem. The tithe may be redeemed for money and the money spent on food at the place of the Temple (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:16" href="/Deuteronomy.24.16"><i>Deut</i>. 24:16</a>). Edibles without calories are not considered food.</i> except water and salt. One who vows not to eat food is permitted water and salt. One may make an <i>eruv</i> for a <i>nazir</i> with wine and for an Israel with heave<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the <i>nazir</i> is forbidden wine and the non-Cohen heave, wine is food appropriate for people who may consume it.</i>. Symmachos says, also with profane food for a Cohen in a <i>bet happeras</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">A place where a source of impurity is known but its location is unknown. The Cohen may not enter by rabbinic decree but he could be carried there in a wooden box which shields against the impurity of the dead. The food may not be heave since heave in a <i>bet happeras</i> is at least rabbinically impure and must be burned; it is no longer food.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says among the graves since he could be shielded and eat.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “With anything one may make an <i>eruv</i> or participate,” etc. Rebbi Aḥa said, this is Rebbi Eliezer’s, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.10.1">Mishnah 7:10</a>, detailing the rules for <i>eruvin</i> of courtyards, where R. Joshua disagrees and asserts that a single loaf of bread is a valid <i>eruv</i>. It follows that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.1.1">Mishnah 3:1</a> refers to <i>eruv teḥumim</i>, an <i>eruv</i> of domains. (Note by Rashba).</i>, “with anything one may make an <i>eruv</i> or participate except with water or salt, the words of Rebbi Eliezer.” Rebbi Yose said, it is everybody’s opinion. One makes an <i>eruv</i> for courtyards and participates both for courtyards and for domains<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence makes no sense as it stands since one makes an <i>eruv</i> both for courtyards and domains and participates for alleys. A possible emendation would be to delete the first mention of “courtyards”; S. Liebermann proposes to read כן for בין “by the same rules one makes <i>eruv</i> or participates for courtyards and domains.”</i>. Our Mishnah is Rebbi Meïr’s, as it was stated: one may make an <i>eruv</i> with anything eaten raw as is; with bread, one does not make an <i>eruv</i> with it<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously a reference “the words of R. Meïr” is missing here. For an <i>eruv teḥumim</i> which represents two meals at a place where there are no cooking facilities, he requires food that can be eaten as is without any additions. He excludes vegetables eaten only with bread.</i>. Garlic and onions, in the opinion of Rebbi Meïr one does not make an <i>eruv</i> with them<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are eaten raw only with bread, or because they cause bad mouth odor, see below.</i>, as it was stated: Rebbi Jehudah said, it happened that Rebbi Meïr spent a Sabbath at Ardaksam<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 29a" href="/Eruvin.29a">Babli 29a</a> the place is ערדיסקא. It must have been in lower Galilee not far from Tivˋon.</i>; there came a person who said, I made an <i>eruv</i> with onions and Rebbi Meïr restricted him to his four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 6:4" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).6.4">Tosephta 6:4</a>. If he came from another place to hear R. Meïr on basis of an invalid <i>eruv</i>, he voluntarily left his Sabbath domain and is restricted to his 4 cubits as explained in Chapter 4.</i>. Even though Rebbi Meïr said, with everything one may make an <i>eruv</i> or participate except with water or salt, on condition that it be something eaten raw as is. Arum and colocasia: in the rabbis’ opinion one does not make an <i>eruv</i> with<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are not edible when raw.</i>.
We have stated two principles which are not comparable one with the other. “With anything one may make an <i>eruv</i> or participate except with water or salt,” whether it is something eaten raw as is or something which is not eaten raw. “Anything may be bought with money of [Second] Tithe except water and salt,” but only something eaten raw as is<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This text makes sense only if one switches the statements: For an <i>eruv</i> (<i>teḥumim</i>) only produce eaten raw is acceptable, for Second Tithe all produce.</i>. “With anything one may make an <i>eruv</i> or participate except with water or salt,” both according to Rebbi Aqiba and Rebbi Ismael. “Anything may be bought with money of [Second] Tithe except water and salt,” according to Rebbi Aqiba. But according to Rebbi Ismael?
<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This text also is in <i>Maˋaser Šeni</i> 1:4 (Notes 144–151, ד). Parallels in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael 8" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael.8"><i>Sifra</i> Introduction (8</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 27b" href="/Eruvin.27b">Babli <i>Eruvin</i> 27b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 54b" href="/Bava_Kamma.54b"><i>Bava Qamma</i>54b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 36a" href="/Bava_Kamma.36a">36a</a>; in slightly different version <i>Nazir</i>35b.</i> Rebbi Ismael stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:26" href="/Deuteronomy.14.26"><i>Deut</i>. 14:26</a>.</i><i>Spend the money on anything you want</i>, this is a general clause. <i>For cattle and sheep, wine and intoxicating drink</i>, this is detail. <i>And anything you want</i>, another general clause. General, detail, and general, you may judge only in light of the detail<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">One of the hermeneutical principles of R. Ismael. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael"><i>Sifra</i>, Introduction</a>.</i>. Just as the detail is explained as born from what is born from the earth, so only what is born from what is born from the earth<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Anything grown by sexual reproduction from plant or animal living on the land.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba explains<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">In detail explained <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 107 naturally without reference to R. Ismael’s rule.</i>: Just as the detail is explained as fruit, born from a fruit, or what prepares fruit, so only what is fruit, born from a fruit, or what prepares fruit<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry</i> called משביחי אכילה “food enhancers”, such as costus, amomum, other spices, benjamin, asa foetida, peppers, and saffron.</i>. What is the difference between them? Fish, locusts, truffles, and mushrooms which may be bought with tithe money following Rebbi Aqiba, following Rebbi Ismael they may not be bought<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifra</i>, R. Ismael explicitly excludes truffles and mushrooms.</i>.
“With anything one may make an <i>eruv</i> or participate except with water or salt.” Rebbi Yasa said, because the body is not fed by them. Rebbi Levi said, because they are agents of curse<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Water at the deluge; salt is mentioned <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 29:22" href="/Deuteronomy.29.22"><i>Deut</i>. 29:22</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, if he made them into brine it may be bought with Tithe money<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 27a" href="/Eruvin.27a">Babli 27a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Miasha: only if he mixed oil into it. Rebbi Yose asked, then should he be able to use it for <i>eruv</i> only in proportion<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the following a minimal quantity of oil for a valid <i>eruv</i> is determined, brine should be acceptable only if it contains that quantity of oil. Then the mention of brine would be unnecessary.</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The following paragraph also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 8:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.8.4.4"><i>Peah</i> 8:5 (Notes 56–64</a>,<b>ק</b>); a parallel is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 29a" href="/Eruvin.29a">Babli 29a</a>.</i> There we had stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Peah</i> 8:5 detailing minimal amounts of produce to be given as tithe of the poor.</i> “Half a <i>log</i> of oil. Rebbi Aqiba said, a quarter.” Rebbi Eleazar said, and so it is for an <i>eruv</i>. That is for wine, but not for oil, for which one makes an <i>eruv</i> with food for two meals<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 6:3" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).6.3">Tosephta 6:3</a>.</i>. It was stated: One makes an <i>eruv</i> with vinegar as food for two meals. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Samuel ben Rebbi Issac, enough to dip bundles of vegetable as food for two meals. Rebbi Isaac of Atosha said before R. Zeˋira in the name of the House of Rebbi Yannai: one may make an <i>eruv</i> with raw peas. For whom is this necessary? For Rebbi Meïr, lest you say, because they generate bad mouth odor one may not use them for <i>eruv</i>. One may make an <i>eruv</i> with salted fish. One may make an <i>eruv</i> with salted meat. One may make an <i>eruv</i> with raw meat. There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 11:7" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.11.7">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 11:7</a>, referring to the ram brought as sin-offering on a Day of Atonement that falls on the Sabbath; it must be eaten by the priests before the next morning but cannot be cooked. The priests from Babylonia (in the opinion of the Babli, those from Alexandria) ate it raw after the fast. The text of our Mishnah, “because their taste is refined” is a euphemism.</i>: “The Babylonians eat it raw because their taste is refined.” Rebbi Yudan asked, may one use χαλκίς<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">χαλκίς, -ίδος, ἡ, a kind of herring or sardine. This translation follows the text in <i>Peah</i>; the word here has not been identified. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 39b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.39b">Babli (<i>Avodah zarah</i> 39b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:5-6" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.5-6">Tosephta <i>Avodah zarah</i> 4:11</a>) the word appears as כילבית or כילכית, identified as a kind of sardine.</i> for <i>eruv</i> since the Samaritans eat it raw? Samuel bar Shilat in the name of Rav: One may use for <i>eruv paˋapuˋin, gudganiot,</i> and <i>ḥaluglugot</i>. They asked him, what are these? He said to them, καυκαλίς<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">A plant, <i>melilotus officinalis</i>. In Arabic ققل is cardamom, قاقلى an alkaline plant.</i>, melilot<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic حندقوقا, a kind of tall clover, only barely digestible.</i>, and purslain<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic فرفخ.</i>.
Paragraph. “One who vows not to eat food is permitted water and salt.” There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.1.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 6:1</a>.</i>: “One who made a vow to abstain from cooked food is permitted roasted and scalded food.” <sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the next are also in <i>Nedarim</i> 6:1 (Notes 6–14) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.9.2"><i>Nazir</i> 6:11 (Note 225</a>), cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 49a" href="/Nedarim.49a">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 49a</a>.</i> A Mishnah states that scalding is called cooking, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.9.1">Mishnah <i>Nazir</i> 6:11</a>. The verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:18" href="/Numbers.6.18"><i>Num</i>. 6:18</a> requires the sacrifice to be cooked.</i>: “If he cooked the well-being offering or scalded it.” A verse states that “roasted” is called “cooked” as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 35:13" href="/II_Chronicles.35.13"><i>2Chr</i>. 35:13</a>.</i>: <i>They cooked the Pesaḥ in fire</i>. If you say, against the rules, Rebbi Jonah from Bostra said, <i>as is the rule</i><sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Roasted in fire</i>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:9" href="/Exodus.12.9"><i>Ex</i>. 12:9</a>. Cooked would be on the fire, not in it.</i>. A Mishnah [states] that scalded is called cooked, and a verse that roasted is called cooked; but did we not state: “One who makes a vow to abstain from cooked food is permitted roasted and scalded food”? Rebbi Joḥanan said, in matters of vows one follows common usage. Rebbi Joshia said, in matters of vows one follows biblical usage. What is the difference between them? If one said, a <i>qonam</i><sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The Phoenician equivalent of Hebrew <i>qorba̅n</i>, “(forbidden like) sacrifice” which people were afraid to pronounce in vows. Cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Nedarim</i>.</i> that I shall not taste wine on Tabernacles. In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan he is forbidden on the last day of the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Which in popular consciousness is the last day of <i>Sukkot</i> but legally is a separate holiday with its own rules.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Joshia, is he permitted? Also Rebbi Joshia agrees that he is prohibited. Rebbi Joshia said it only for restrictions.
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, Rebbi Joḥanan ate bake-meats and said, I did not taste (anything) [food]<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The text of <i>Eruvin</i> (in parentheses) has to be emended to the text in <i>Nedarim</i> and <i>Nazir</i> [in brackets] to make sense. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 44a" href="/Berakhot.44a">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 44a</a>.</i> on that day. But did we not state: “He who made a vow not to eat food is permitted water and salt”? Explain it following Rebbi Joshia, who said, in matters of vows one follows biblical usage. And from where that everything is called food? Rebbi Aḥa bar Ulla said<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 45:23" href="/Genesis.45.23"><i>Gen</i>. 45:23</a>.</i>: <i>And ten</i><i>female donkeys carrying grain, bread, and food</i>. Why does the verse say, <i>and food</i>? From here that everything is called food<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 35b" href="/Berakhot.35b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 35b</a>.</i>.
If he forbade himself a loaf by a vow it may be used as <i>eruv</i>, if he dedicated it it may not be used as <i>eruv</i>. If he forbade himself a loaf by a vow it may be used as <i>eruv</i>, since another person may eat it. If he dedicated it it may not be used as <i>eruv</i>, since neither he nor another person may eat it<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 30a" href="/Eruvin.30a">Babli 30a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa asked, may not a person ask about his dedication<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since a dedication to the Temple is a vow, a dedication may be removed by rabbinic authority like any other vow. Then the dedicated item reverts to profane status and therefore always was potential food. Naturally the dedicated object should be redeemed and returned to profane status but this is not possible on the Sabbath and therefore not an option to validate an <i>eruv</i> on the Sabbath. The Babli notes that the disregard of possible removal of dedication is a separate rabbinic decree.</i>? Rebbi Aḥa said that Rebbi Miasha asked: There Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This must refer to R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i> said that Ḥiyya the son of Rav said, if one used idolatry<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Wood from an <i>ashera</i> or an idol, forbidden for all usufruct.</i> to make a lath it permits the alley, and here you are saying so? Rebbi Eleazar said, there in any case the alley was closed; here what do you have<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since the lath has no required minimal depth it is a sign, not a material presence. The material could not be used for a beam which has to be three-dimensional material.</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts a Genizah fragment edited by Ginzberg (pp. 95–101,G).</i>“One may make an <i>eruv</i> for a <i>nazir</i> with wine” for another person may drink it. “And for an Israel with heave,” for a Cohen may eat it. For a Cohen in a <i>bet happeras</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah in the Babli and in Maimonides’s autograph Mishnah this is a separate declarative sentence, independent of Symmachos’s statement who there forbids heave for the Israel and requires strictly profane food. In the Yerushalmi Mishnah Symmachos adds to the statement of the anonymous Tanna but he does not disagree with him. Therefore it is impossible to read the Babli’s Mishnah in the Yerushalmi text.</i>? The Mishnah follows the House of Shammai, for the House of Shammai say that one may not make an <i>eruv</i> for a person unless his personal vessels may be there<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of the Babli this means that for the House of Shammai an <i>eruv teḥumin</i> is possible only if a person transports his bed to the place of the <i>eruv</i>.</i>. Symmachos follows the House of Shammai as we have stated: “Symmachos says, also with profane food for a Cohen in a <i>bet happeras</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">A place where a source of impurity is known but its location is unknown. The Cohen may not enter by rabbinic decree but he could be carried there in a wooden box which shields against the impurity of the dead. The food may not be heave since heave in a <i>bet happeras</i> is at least rabbinically impure and must be burned; it is no longer food.</i>.” Then also between the graves? He might transgress a rabbinic prohibition and eat there<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence should be read before the preceding one. Since the prohibition of the <i>bet happeras</i> is rabbinical, the Cohen could disregard the prohibition without defiling himself biblically.</i>. For he could enter on a bench<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic سدّة. It is presumed that the bench is fixed to a flat wooden plank. Since wooden implements which do not enclose a volume are impervious to impurity, the Cohen sitting on the bench can be transported through the cemetery without violating the biblical prohibition of defiling himself with the impurity of the dead.</i>, in a box or a chest, make a hole less than a hand-breadth [wide]<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.5.1">Mishnah <i>Sukkah</i> 3:5</a>. The topic of the Mishnah is the <i>etro</i> g,<i>C</i><br><i>itrus medica</i>, a fruit which is part of the “four kinds” of plants carried on Tabernacles.</i>, stick a spindle or a splinter into it, and eat it.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may make an <i>eruv</i> with <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Demay</i> is produce of which one may assume that heave was taken but one suspects that it was not tithed. While a scrupulous person will not eat <i>demay</i> it may be given to the poor and the traveler and therefore is edible food.</i>, and with First Tithe from which heave has been taken<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Any food of which heave was not taken is <i>ṭevel</i> and forbidden for consumption; it is not food. First Tithe of which Heave of the Tithe was taken is wholly profane.</i>, and Second Tithe or dedicated [food]<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Donated to the Temple to be sold and the proceeds used for the upkeep of the Temple.</i> which was redeemed; Cohanim with <i>ḥallah</i> and with heave. But not with <i>ṭevel</i>, nor with First Tithe from which heave was not taken, and Second Tithe or dedicated [food] which was not redeemed. If somebody sends his <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For an <i>eruv teḥumim</i>, if the person wanting to change his Sabbath domain does not go by himself to deposit the food, he must send it by a legally competent person.</i> through a deaf-and-dumb or insane person, or a minor, or by somebody who does not accept <i>eruvin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A Sadducee who as a matter of principle denies the possibility of an <i>eruv</i>, cannot be an agent for its creation.</i>, it is not an <i>eruv</i>. If he told a third person to receive it from him it is an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In this case the identity of the carrier is irrelevant.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “One may make an <i>eruv</i> with <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Demay</i> is produce of which one may assume that heave was taken but one suspects that it was not tithed. While a scrupulous person will not eat <i>demay</i> it may be given to the poor and the traveler and therefore is edible food.</i>, and with First Tithe.” Rebbi Jacob the Southerner asked, is our Mishnah not following the House of Shammai? As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.5.1">Mishnah <i>Sukkah</i> 3:5</a>. The topic of the Mishnah is the <i>etro</i> g,<i>C</i><br><i>itrus medica</i>, a fruit which is part of the “four kinds” of plants carried on Tabernacles.</i>, “of <i>demay</i>, the House of Shammai declare invalid but the House of Hillel declare valid<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>demay</i> may not be eaten, for the House of Shammai it is not a fruit and therefore cannot be part of the “four kinds”. By analogy it should be forbidden to use <i>demay</i> as food for <i>eruv</i>. For the House of Hillel <i>demay</i> can always be turned into food even on Sabbath and holidays as explained later by the Elder R. Ḥiyya; therefore it is food in all respects.</i>. Of Second Tithe in Jerusalem one should not take but if he took it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Second tithe has to be consumed in Jerusalem; it is food there. But the use of the <i>etro</i> G for a religious ceremony is not consumption; while it is permitted it is inappropriate.</i>.”
Rav Sheshet in the name of the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya: It is permitted to move <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Any food of which heave was not taken is <i>ṭevel</i> and forbidden for consumption; it is not food. First Tithe of which Heave of the Tithe was taken is wholly profane.</i> which has a condition on it<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ṭevel</i> is neither food nor an implement and therefore is <i>muqṣeh</i>. But if heave and tithes are mentally removed, it becomes food and may be moved and eaten. If one decides on the Sabbath to give after the Sabbath heave and tithes from a certain part of the <i>ṭevel</i> produce, the remainder may be eaten. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 18:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.18.1.6"><i>Šabbat</i> 18, Notes 25</a> ff.</i>. How does he do it? He puts his eyes on a small part and eats the remainder. And it was stated: The House of Shammai say, one does not use Second Tithe as <i>eruv</i> in Jerusalem. Rebbi Jeremiah said, this you are saying about <i>eruv</i> of courtyards<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This is inappropriate use of Second Tithe since the <i>eruv</i> of courtyards represents a symbolic meal not an actual one. But the <i>eruv</i> of domains is to be eaten on the Sabbath; this is appropriate use.</i>. But for an <i>eruv</i> of domains, a villager<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">G shows that the correct text is בקרתני. This also was the text of the scribe, erroneously changed by the corrector into תני “it was stated”, which is the version of all printed editions but makes no sense here.</i> who deposits his <i>eruv</i> at Jerusalem can go there and eat it.
Samuel said, if he is nine or ten years old his <i>eruv</i> is an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Since at this age he is able to legally buy in a grocery store (Mishnah G<i>iṭṭin</i> 5:8, Halakhah 5:9 Notes 234 ff.) he is able to place his food. While a smaller child is not able to do anything of legal consequence, an <i>eruv</i> of courtyards is controlled by the recipient of the foodstuffs and for such a small distance an underage child may be used. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 31b" href="/Eruvin.31b">Babli 31b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this you are saying about <i>eruv</i> of domains. But for an <i>eruv</i> of courtyards, even if he is small. <sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The following also is in Chapter 7 (24c l. 74, Note 94, 7).</i> Rebbi Joshua [ben Levi]<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Added from G, 7, and its frequent quotes in Medieval sources.</i> said, why does one make <i>eruv</i> of courtyards<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Assuming that the courtyard participated in an alley, a separate <i>eruv</i> for courtyards essentially is superfluous. It is required because it is required if there is no alley and because it helps neighborly relations.</i>? Because of peaceful relations. It happened that a woman was hostile to another and sent her her <i>eruv</i> through her son. She took him, embraced him, and kissed him. He went and told it before his mother. She said, obviously she loves me and I did not know it. As a consequence they made peace. That is what is written, <i>its ways are ways of pleasantness and all its paths peace</i><sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:17" href="/Proverbs.3.17"><i>Prov</i>. 3:17</a>.</i>.
Paragraph. “If somebody sends his <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For an <i>eruv teḥumim</i>, if the person wanting to change his Sabbath domain does not go by himself to deposit the food, he must send it by a legally competent person.</i> through a deaf-and-dumb or insane person, or a minor,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">A somewhat differently organized parallel to this paragraph is in <i>Maˋserot</i> 2:1 (Notes 39–43).</i> Rebbi Eleazar said, he has to be with him. There Rebbi Yose said in the name of Rebbi Sheshet, Rebbi Eleazar the son of Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Maˋserot</i>: Abin.</i>: If somebody says to another I shall tithe for you, he does not have to be with him. And here, you say so? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, here about an adult, there about a minor. Rebbi Aḥa said in the name of Rebbi Ḥinena<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Maˋserot</i>: R. Ḥanania in the name of R. Ḥanina.</i>, you may even say in both cases we deal with an adult or in both cases we deal with a minor. There, when he says, I shall tithe for you, he does not have to be with him. But here, he says make an <i>eruv</i> for me! This learns from that and that learns from this. This learns from that, if he says I shall tithe for you, he does not have to be with him. That learns from this, if he says make an <i>eruv</i> for me, he has to be with him<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Since these are the statements one started out with it is clear that with the text in <i>Maˋserot</i> one has to read: “This learns from that, if he says I shall make an <i>eruv</i> for you, he does not have to be with him. That learns from this, if he says tithe for me, he has to be with him.” In any case if the initiative is from the other side he does not have to be there to check but if the initiative is his he has to supervise the minor. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Eruvin 31b:15:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Eruvin.31b.15.1">Tosaphot 31b <i>s. v</i>. כאן</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he deposited it in a tree higher than ten hand-breadths it is not an <i>eruv</i>; below ten hand-breadths it is an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If the tree is standing in the public domain and the place where the <i>eruv</i> is deposited is at least 4-by-4 hand-breadths wide, this place is a separate domain. Since it is standing in the public domain the intent of the person making the <i>eruv</i> is to spend the Sabbath in the public domain. But then the <i>eruv</i> is in a separate private domain if it is at least 10 hand-breadths high. Since climbing on trees is rabbinically forbidden on the Sabbath there is no possibility that the person keep the Sabbath at the place of his <i>eruv</i>.</i>. If he put it in a cistern even 100 cubits deep it is an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is a private domain by construction even if built in the public domain.</i>. If he put it on top of a stick or a pole<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κοντός, ὁ. Since stick or pole are not 16 hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup> wide at the top they cannot form a separate domain.</i> as long as it was uprooted and inserted it is an <i>eruv</i> even if 100 cubits high. If he put it into a tower it is an <i>eruv</i> even if the key was lost. Rebbi Eliezer said, if it is not known that the key is in its place it is no <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbis hold that one can break into the tower even without a key. R. Eliezer holds that one needs a key. If the owner forgot where he put the key it is no obstacle but if the key might be lost it is.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “If he deposited it in a tree more than ten hand-breadths high,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 2:4:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.2.4.2-5"><i>Sukkah</i> 2:4</a> (52d l. 66, <b>ס</b>). The next one also is found there but rearranged that “here” and “there” correctly point to <i>Sukkah</i> and <i>Eruvin</i>.</i> It is an <i>eruv</i> but it is forbidden to move it<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">As is clear from G, this refers to an <i>eruv</i> placed on a tree less than ten hand-breadths high. The prohibitions here are rabbinic (“forbidden” but not “liable”).</i>; below three it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Anything below 3 hand-breadths is as lying on the ground, not on the tree. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 33a" href="/Eruvin.33a">Babli 33a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 2:13" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).2.13">Tosephta 2:13</a>.</i>. This is difficult. If his <i>eruv</i> is a [valid] <i>eruv</i> one should be permitted to move it. If one is forbidden to move it his <i>eruv</i> should not be a [valid] <i>eruv</i>. He might transgress the rabbinic prohibition and eat. Then also higher than ten [hand-breadths]<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">If one entertains the possibility that people would disregard rabbinic prohibitions, why would an <i>eruv</i> placed higher than 10 hand-breadths be invalid since one could climb the tree in violation of the rabbinic prohibition.</i>? Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel, explain it if its stem was four [hand-breadths]<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If the tree is standing in the public domain and the place where the <i>eruv</i> is deposited is at least 4-by-4 hand-breadths wide, this place is a separate domain. Since it is standing in the public domain the intent of the person making the <i>eruv</i> is to spend the Sabbath in the public domain. But then the <i>eruv</i> is in a separate private domain if it is at least 10 hand-breadths high. Since climbing on trees is rabbinically forbidden on the Sabbath there is no possibility that the person keep the Sabbath at the place of his <i>eruv</i>.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, only if the public domain surrounds it on all sides; he says my Sabbath rest shall be below it<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Not all <i>eruvim</i> placed 10 hand-breadths high are invalid; only if the tree is in the public domain, the place of the <i>eruv</i> forms a separate private domain of 4-by-4 hand-breadths, and the person making the <i>eruv</i> does not intend to climb the tree at sundown (which would be permitted).</i>. It was stated: If he put it in a basket and hung it on a tree, higher than ten hand-breadths high it is not an <i>eruv</i>; below ten hand-breadth it is an <i>eruv</i> but it is forbidden to move it; below three it is permitted. This is difficult. If his <i>eruv</i> is a [valid] <i>eruv</i> one should be permitted to move it. If one is forbidden to move it his <i>eruv</i> should not be a [valid] <i>eruv</i>. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥinena: He might turn it upside down and annul the private domain created by it<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Since the basket can be turned and emptied while hanging on the tree it is not moved from its place, the food is accessible, and the <i>eruv</i> valid. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 33a" href="/Eruvin.33a">Babli 33a</a>,b.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this implies that a bench in the public domain high ten and wide four [hand-breadths], since one may tilt it and annul the private domain created by it, it implies that his <i>eruv</i> is a [valid] <i>eruv</i> and it is permitted to move it<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">If the food is on the bench, one may tilt the bench, the food falls down and is accessible. In this and the preceding case the prohibition of transporting cannot apply to the food.</i>.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.2.4.1">Mishnah <i>Sukkah</i> 2:4</a>. A booth (<i>sukkah</i>) for Tabernacles must have a thatched roof and at least three walls. Up to two walls can be tree trunks. Since biblically one is forbidden to cut or pluck anything from a tree on a holiday, rabbinically one is forbidden to climb on it. The Mishnah permits the use of the trunks as walls on the intermediate days of Tabernacles but not on the holiday.</i>: “Two human-made and one a tree, or two as trees and one human-made is valid but one may not enter it on the holiday.” [Here you are saying it is valid but one may not enter it on the holiday]<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Added from G (and the parallel in <i>Sukkah</i>).</i> but there you are saying his <i>eruv</i> is a [valid] <i>eruv</i> and it is permitted to move it<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">If the food is on the bench, one may tilt the bench, the food falls down and is accessible. In this and the preceding case the prohibition of transporting cannot apply to the food.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah said, one on top, the other at the side<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Since the roof must be on top of the walls, in the case of the Mishnah it must rest on the top of the trees (or top of branches) which is rabbinically forbidden. But the basket is on the side of the trunk and the statement follows the opinion that the sides of tree trunks may be used on Sabbaths and holidays.</i>. This learns from that and that learns from this. This learns from that, if there were two pegs protruding and he thatched over them it is valid and one uses it on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 5:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.5.2.2"><i>Šabbat</i> Chapter 5 Notes 39–43</a>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 154b" href="/Shabbat.154b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 154b</a>.</i>. That learns from this, if he put it on a tree branch his <i>eruv</i> is a [valid] <i>eruv</i> and it is forbidden to move it<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">This is the corrector’s text. The scribe wrote correctly אין “you cannot consider …”.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, in both cases it is at the side. How is that? As Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said in the name of Rebbi Zeˋira: This is Rebbi Eleazar ben Simon’s, as Rebbi Eleazar ben Simon says, one is permitted to use the sides of an animal on the Sabbath. There is no difference between sides of an animal and sides of a tree<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> that an <i>eruv</i> in a cistern always is valid. If the cistern is surrounded by public domain, it is a separate private domain. How can anybody have an <i>eruv</i> in a domain different from where he would be on the Sabbath? The answer is that the cistern must be dry and he intends to take the bottom of the cistern as his place of Sabbath rest.</i>.
How<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">This is the corrector’s text. The scribe wrote correctly אין “you cannot consider …”.</i> can you consider deep as high? Abba bar Rav Ḥuna said, if one said, my Sabbath rest is below<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> that an <i>eruv</i> in a cistern always is valid. If the cistern is surrounded by public domain, it is a separate private domain. How can anybody have an <i>eruv</i> in a domain different from where he would be on the Sabbath? The answer is that the cistern must be dry and he intends to take the bottom of the cistern as his place of Sabbath rest.</i>.
“If he put it on top of a stick or a pole<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κοντός, ὁ. Since stick or pole are not 16 hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup> wide at the top they cannot form a separate domain.</i> as long as it was uprooted and inserted it is an <i>eruv</i> even if 100 cubits high.” Because it was uprooted and inserted; therefore if it was not uprooted and inserted his <i>eruv</i> is not a [valid]<i>eruv</i>. Does this not disagree with Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">He explained earlier (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.3.2">Note 95</a>) that the <i>eruv</i> becomes invalid only if it is in an isolated private domain in the public domain. This has nothing to do with the difference between a tree and a pole. A different explanation in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 34b" href="/Eruvin.34b">Babli, 34b</a>.</i>, as Rav Jehudah said in the name of Samuel, explain it if its stem was four [hand-breadths]? Rebbi Mana said, only if a table was put on its top<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">If the pole or column was topped by a table measuring at least 4-by-4 hand-breadths, the <i>eruv</i> deposited there would be as invalid as one deposited in a tree.</i>.
It was stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 2:15" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).2.15">Tosephta 2:15</a>, about the <i>eruv</i> deposited in a tower when the key was lost.</i> “Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">With G and the Tosephta read: Eleazar.</i> says, if it was lost in the field his <i>eruv</i> is not a [valid] <i>eruv</i>; if it was lost in town his <i>eruv</i> is a [valid] <i>eruv</i>.” If it was lost in the field his <i>eruv</i> is not a [valid] <i>eruv</i>, for he cannot bring it<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">A replacement key.</i> in a Sabbath way. If it was lost in town his <i>eruv</i> is a [valid] <i>eruv</i>, for he can bring it through exempt space<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Since the roofs of houses are exempt space (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 1:1:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.1.1.16"><i>Šabbat</i> 1 Note 109</a>), in theory one may move things there even without an <i>eruv</i> by climbing over rooftops.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said it correctly: what is the reason (of Rav) [of the rabbis]<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The correct reading is that of G [in brackets]. The rabbis are the opponents of R. Eleazar in the Tosephta.</i>? Rebbi Abba the son of Rav Pappai said, it is Rebbi Meïr’s since Rebbi Meïr said, “he even originally reduces and takes.<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.4.3.1">Mishnah <i>Besah</i> 4:3</a>. If produce is stored in a makeshift building formed by bricks without mortar, R. Meïr permits removing bricks to create an opening to get to the produce.</i>” Rebbi Meïr said this only for a holiday, maybe for the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">It is true that removing bricks which were laid without mortar cannot be classified as destroying a building by biblical standards, but this does not imply that the rabbinic prohibition of this activity was waved on a Sabbath where preparation of food is forbidden.</i>? But here we are concerned with the Sabbath! Rebbi Abba Mari said, it is Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob’s, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Shabbat 15:2" href="/Mishnah_Shabbat.15.2">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 15:2, Note 6</a>.</i>, “Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob said, one may tie in front of an animal that it should not leave.” There is no difference between tying and locking. That means, about a stone tower. But for a wooden tower he is like one who breaks the amphora to eat dried figs from it<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Which is permitted on the Sabbath, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 22:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.22.3.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 22:3</a>. Quoted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Eruvin 34b:14:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Eruvin.34b.14.1">Tosaphot 34b <i>s. v</i>. ואמאי</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If it rolled outside the Sabbath boundary, if a pile fell on it, or it was burned or heave which became impure on Friday afternoon it is not <i>eruv</i>. After dark it is an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The food must be available at the beginning of the Sabbath. Since it is a symbolic Sabbath meal it may be eaten at any time on the Sabbath; therefore if it is lost during the Sabbath the <i>eruv</i> remains in force.</i>. If it is a matter of uncertainty, Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Jehudah say, he is a donkey driver-camel driver<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The donkey driver walks behind his animal, the camel driver in front of it. The donkey-camel driver is in an impossible situation, by analogy his situation is impossible; the person’s Sabbath rest is not at his home since he made an <i>eruv</i>; it is not at the place of the <i>eruv</i> since the latter might be invalid. He might be able to walk between house and <i>eruv</i> but at each endpoint not one step further.</i>. Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon say that in case of doubt an <i>eruv</i> is qualified. Rebbi Yose said, Autolas<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In other sources the name is אבטולמוס (Ptolemaios).</i> testified in the name of five Elders that in case of a doubt an <i>eruv</i> is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>eruv</i> is a rabbinic institution to allow carrying in or walking to places biblically permitted, in cases of doubt one has to permit.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “If it rolled outside the Sabbath boundary,” etc. They said only “if it became impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>, the case of an <i>eruv</i> made with heave, which is food only if pure. The Mishnah only deals with the case that there is certainty that the heave became impure, the only question is when it happened. The <i>eruv</i> is valid if and only if the heave was pure at the beginning of the Sabbath at sundown or in early twilight. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 36a" href="/Eruvin.36a">Babli 36a</a>.</i>” A doubt whether it became impure when it still was daylight, a doubt whether it became impure in the night. But if there was a doubt whether it was pure or impure one may use it for an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>eruv</i> was deposited in the public domain, any doubt whether there is purity has to be resolved in favor of purity (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.2.5"><i>Soṭah</i> 1:2 Note 88</a>). The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 36a" href="/Eruvin.36a">Babli 36a</a> disagrees.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: They followed the <i>status quo ante</i><sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Both groups, R. Meïr and R.
