Jehudah who are restrictive and R. Yose and R. Simeon who are lenient agree with the principle that in cases of doubt one presumes the permanence of the prior state; cf. G<i>iṭṭin</i> 6:3 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.2.5">Note 87</a>).</i>.
Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Jehudah objected to Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meïr and R. Jehudah define the prior state by the fact that a person is prohibited from leaving his place on the Sabbath. This prohibition can only be liftedif it is certain that there was a valid <i>eruv</i>. R. Yose and R. Simeon define the prior state by the fact that a valid <i>eruv</i> was made sometime on Friday; it may be invalidated only by the certain knowledge that it was invalid at the beginning of twilight. An <i>eruv teḥumim</i> may be eaten immediately after nightfall.</i>: If it had been eaten when it still was daylight (could it be permitted?) [would it not be forbidden?]<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is that of the scribe of L and of G, which has to be accepted as the original text. The text in brackets is the corrector’s and all printed editions. The meaning is the same; the original text had two rhetorical questions, the corrected text two correct statements.</i> It remains in its permitted state until it becomes known to you that it is forbidden. Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon objected to Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Jehudah: If it had been eaten after nightfall (could it be prohibited?) [would it not be permitted?]<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is that of the scribe of L and of G, which has to be accepted as the original text. The text in brackets is the corrector’s and all printed editions. The meaning is the same; the original text had two rhetorical questions, the corrected text two correct statements.</i> It remains in its prohibited state until it becomes known to you that it is permitted.
Does one give him the 2’000 [cubits] to his house<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.4.1">Note 16</a>, the question is whether he may walk in the domain which is common to the Sabbath domain of his house and the Sabbath domain defined by his <i>eruv</i>. The distinction between the argument of R. Meïr and that of R. Jehudah refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>:10 about a person who was going to another place and promised to make an <i>eruv</i> on the way for the townspeople but then did not keep his word, where R. Jehudah lets him go since by his action he has shown that he does not want to be counted with the townspeople, whereas R. Meïr makes him donkey-camel driver with only 4 cubits to move in. A different version is given later (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.1.2">Chapter 4, Note 21</a>); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.3.3"><i>Qiddušin</i> 3:3 (Note 128</a>): R. Yose said, R. Meïr said this only as a restriction. In this version, he agrees in principle with R. Jehudah but fines people acting stupidly.</i>? Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, one gives him. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, one does not give him. Rebbi Shammai said before Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: They disagree following Rebbi Meïr, but following Rebbi Jehudah everybody agrees that one gives him. The donkey driver-camel driver of Rebbi Meïr cannot be compared to the donkey driver-camel driver of Rebbi Jehudah. The donkey driver-camel driver of Rebbi Meïr cannot use his <i>eruv</i> since his <i>eruv</i> did not have the power to pull out his feet away from the people of his place; the donkey driver-camel driver of Rebbi Jehudah was thinking to pull out his feet away from the people of his place.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Mikvaot 2:2" href="/Mishnah_Mikvaot.2.2">Mishnah <i>Miqwaot</i> 2:2</a>. The Mishnah states that if a person was impure by biblical standards he can be purified only by immersion in a <i>miqweh</i> which is unquestionably valid. But if his impurity is rabbinic, immersion in a <i>miqweh</i> will purify him unless the <i>miqweh</i> is unquestionably invalid. R. Yose disagrees and holds that the principle of permanence of the <i>status quo ante</i> also applies to rabbinic impurity and the <i>miqweh</i> must be unquestionably valid.</i>: “If there is a doubt, it is pure; Rebbi Yose declares it impure.” Rebbi Jonah said, Rebbi Yose declared it impure only because of proof<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">If there are a group of items, the status of one is certain but that of the others is uncertain, one may assume that the other items share the status of the certain one unless proven otherwise (cf. <i>Demay</i> 2:1 2<sup class="footnote-marker">nd</sup> of paragraph; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 4:7:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.4.7.7"><i>Terumot</i> 4:8 Note 83</a>.) The Mishnah quotes the case that there be two <i>miqwaot</i>, one of them known to be invalid, while the other might be valid. If the person does not know in which of the two he immersed, R. Yose holds that it must have been the invalid one.</i>. And Rebbi Jonah said, Rebbi Yose declares impure even (one place) [one <i>miqweh</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is from L, that in brackets from G. The preceding argument is rejected, R. Yose applies the principle of permanence of the <i>status quo ante</i> even if nothing is certain and there is only one item.</i>. The argument of Rebbi Yose seems inverted, as we have stated: “Rebbi Yose said, Autolas<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In other sources the name is אבטולמוס (Ptolemaios).</i> testified in the name of five Elders that in case of a doubt an <i>eruv</i> is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>eruv</i> is a rabbinic institution to allow carrying in or walking to places biblically permitted, in cases of doubt one has to permit.</i>.” And here you are saying so? There in his own name, but here in the name of five Elders. They wanted to say that he who says there “pure” says here “permitted”; he who says there “impure” says here “prohibited”. But even he who says there “impure” agrees here that it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The two cases cannot be compared. The rules of impurity are biblical even if they are extended to cover cases of only rabbinic impurity. The rules of <i>eruv</i> are all rabbinic.</i>. Rebbi Ḥinena said, do they not only disagree about their words? And a doubt about their words is for leniency<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">He holds the opposite view. The Mishnah <i>Miqwaot</i> clearly distinguishes between biblical and rabbinic impurity and decrees leniency only for rabbinic cases; for him the rules of <i>eruv teḥumim</i> are all biblical; only <i>eruv ḥaṣerot</i> is rabbinical.</i>. But an <i>eruv</i> is a word of Torah; and a doubt about a word of the Torah is for restriction<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">A generally recognized principle, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 1:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.1.1.4"><i>Ketubot</i> 1:1 Note 21</a>.</i> But is <i>eruv</i> a word of the Torah? Rebbi Jonathan said before the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi Yose ben Laqonia: One whips because of Sabbath domains as word of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 17b" href="/Eruvin.17b">Babli 17b</a>. This proves at least that leaving one’s Sabbath domain is a biblical violation.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya the Elder said to him, but for Sabbath there is only stoning or extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">A Sabbath violation of one of the 39 forbidden categories of work is punishable by stoning if there are witnesses or divine extirpation otherwise. We do not find flogging as punishment for any Sabbath violation.</i>? He said to him, is there not written<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:9" href="/Exodus.12.9"><i>Ex</i>. 12:9</a>.</i>, <i>do not eat from it raw</i>? He said to him, is there written לֹא? No, it is written אַל! He said to him, is there not written<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:29" href="/Exodus.16.29"><i>Ex</i>. 16:29</a>. Even though this is the sequence of the sentences also in G, it is clear that the order has to be inverted. R. Jonathan first quoted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:29" href="/Exodus.16.29"><i>Ex</i>. 16:29</a> as proof that leaving one’s domain on the Sabbath is a biblical violation. Since no punishment is stated, the standard sanction of flogging applies. To this R. Ḥiyya replies that the standard sanction applies only to prohibitions introduced by לֹא, not to admonitions formulated with אַל. R. Jonathan retorts that this explanation is impossible since eating the Passover sacrifice raw is a punishable transgression forbidden by אַל.</i>, <i>stay everybody where he is, no person shall leave his place on the Seventh day</i>? He said to him, is there written לֹא? No, it is written אַל. Rebbi ben Rebbi Abun said, nevertheless each one kept to his tradition<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">It is unresolved whether leaving one’s domain on the Sabbath is a transgression punishable in court. But the institution of <i>eruv</i> certainly is a rabbinic interpretation of the rules.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Sosarta said, they treated <i>eruv</i> as a doubt involving a deaf-mute person<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of biblical prohibitions, matters of doubt are treated differently when a person is involved who can be interrogated about the situation. Then the rules of resolution of doubts can be invoked only after the facts have been investigated. But if the person involved is deaf mute and unable to communicate by sign language the rules are applied immediately.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah asked, so far if it exists, or even if it was burned<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The preceding makes sense if the <i>eruv</i> still exists. But if it was burned (as mentioned in the Mishnah) it should be impossible to invoke a principle of permanence of the <i>status quo ante</i>.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, I confirmed this following what Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">This is the text of L which probably is correct. In G: Rav.</i> Hoshaia said: You must conclude that the boundaries of Sabbath domains are not clear in the words of the Torah. Rebbi Mana asked, it is accepted that 2’000 cubits is not clear<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">The 2’000 cubits counted from the city walls are in imitation of the suburban space allotted to the levitic cities (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:5" href="/Numbers.35.5"><i>Num</i>. 35:5</a>) where the Sabbath is not mentioned. The measure therefore has only rabbinic status. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 36a" href="/Eruvin.36a">Babli 36a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 36b" href="/Beitzah.36b"><i>Beṣah</i> 36b</a>. The Sadducee Damascus Document (CD A x) accepts a limit of 1’000 cubits (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:4" href="/Numbers.35.4"><i>Num</i>. 35:4</a>) for humans and 2’000 cubits for animals (CD A xi) as biblical.</i>. Are 4’000 cubits not clear? Rebbi Simeon bar Carsana in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: The only clear case among all of them is the domain of twelve <i>mil</i> of the camp of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">This is the general tradition that the diameter of the encampment of the Israelites as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 2" href="/Numbers.2"><i>Num</i>. 2</a> was 3 <i>parsah</i> (12 <i>mil</i> or 90 itinerant <i>stadia</i>): <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 6:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.6.1.7"><i>Ševiˋit</i> 6:1 (Note 28</a>), copied in G<i>ittin</i> 1:2 (Note 94), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 54b" href="/Berakhot.54b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 54b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 53b" href="/Eruvin.53b"><i>Eruvin</i> 53b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 75b" href="/Yoma.75b"><i>Yoma</i> 75b</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person may make his <i>eruv</i> conditional and say, if Gentiles come from the East my <i>eruv</i> shall be in the West, but if they come from the West my <i>eruv</i> shall be in the East, if they come from both sides I shall be able to go where I want, if they come from neither side I shall be like the people of my village<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">He can make two <i>eruve teḥumim</i>, deposit each one at an appropriate place and determine on Sabbath morning which one to use. While we do not accept retroactive determinations for biblical commandments, they are acceptable for rabbinic institutions.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the next are from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:3:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.3.2-4"><i>Qiddušin</i> 3:3</a>, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:4:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.4.2-5">Notes 116–131</a>. It is clear that the text here is a copy from there since in the text “here” refers to <i>Qiddušin</i> and the discussion between R. Ḥaggai and R. Yose at the end of the paragraph has absolutely nothing to do with the Mishnah in <i>Eruvin</i> but asks why the conditional distribution of land to the tribes of Gad and Reuben (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 32:29-30" href="/Numbers.32.29-30"><i>Num</i>. 32:29–30</a>) required a double condition, both positive and negative; this is relevant only for <i>Qiddušin</i>. Similarly the next paragraph has no connection with the topics of <i>Eruvin</i>.</i> Paragraph. “A person may make his <i>eruv</i> conditional,” etc. So is the Mishnah: “A person can impose a condition on his <i>ˋeruvin</i>.” Rebbi Eleazar said, who is the Tanna of “if they came”, “if they did not come”? Rebbi Meïr! Which Rebbi Meïr? The colleagues say, Rebbi Meïr of <i>Qiddušin</i>. As it was stated: If somebody say to a woman: You are preliminarily married to me on condition that there will be rain. If rain fell, she is preliminarily married, otherwise she is not preliminarily married. Rebbi Meïr says, she is preliminarily married whether or not rain fell unless he doubled his condition. Everybody agrees that if he said, after rainfall, if it rained she is preliminarily married, otherwise she is not preliminarily married. (But following the rabbis she is preliminarily married whether rain fell or not.)<sup class="footnote-marker">138A</sup><i class="footnote">To be deleted with G and <i>Qiddušin</i>.</i> Rebbi Yose said, it follows Rebbi Meïr in <i>Eruvin</i>, as we have stated there: “If it is a matter of uncertainty„ Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Jehudah say, he is a donkey driver-camel driver.” Rebbi Yose said, Rebbi Meïr said this only as a restriction. Rebbi Mana said, that is correct. He cannot acquire his ˋ<i>eruv</i> since he is not able to enjoy it. [Why not] like the people of his place? If he had decided to leave his place. And here, she is not preliminarily married to the first, since no rain came. She is not preliminarily married to the second, since the first did not double his condition. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Yose: Is this “if” not a reaction? He answered, there is a difference since the land was in their hand and he wanted to take it away from them.
<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.3.4"><i>Qiddušin</i> 3:3, Notes 133–136</a>.</i> Rebbi Jehudah ben Shalom, Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazy in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan. They formulated the <i>symphon</i><sup class="footnote-marker">139A</sup><i class="footnote">Greek συμφώνημα, -ατος, τό, “agreement”; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.2.3"><i>Qiddušin</i> 3:2, Note 69</a> for details and legal standing of this marriage contract.</i> according to Rebbi Meïr in <i>Qiddušin</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah: Rebbi Ḥananiah, the colleague of the rabbis, asked: Why does it have to follow Rebbi Meïr but not also the rabbis? Did not Rebbi Abbahu say in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The following is the contract text: “I, X son of Y, contract a preliminary marriage with you, Z, daughter of U, on condition that I shall give you property A and definitively marry you by day B. If that day should pass without me having taken you in, I shall have no claim on you.” Why can he not say “on condition” but not double his stipulation? If he did not double his stipulation, could this eliminate the preliminary marriage? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, everywhere Rebbi Meïr holds that from “no” you infer “yes”, except here? Rebbi Mattaniah said, one is more restrictive in matters of incest and adultery.
If he did not stipulate? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 3:4" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).3.4">Tosephta 3:4</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 50b" href="/Eruvin.50b">Babli 50b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 37b" href="/Beitzah.37b"><i>Beṣah</i> 37b</a>. Somebody made two different <i>eruvim</i> for the same Sabbath or holiday thinking that he would be able to use both of them.</i>: “If somebody made <i>eruvim</i> for two domains he can go to the South according to his <i>eruv</i> in the North, to the North according to his <i>eruv</i> in the South. If he split the domain<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">If the two domains intersect only at one point he cannot move from that point.</i> he may not move from his place.” An animal belonging to two partners one of which made an <i>eruv</i> in the North and the other in the South. It may walk to the South according to the <i>eruv</i> of the one in the North and to the North according to the <i>eruv</i> of the one in the South. If they split the domain it may not move from its place. If they slaughtered it<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">On a holiday when slaughter is permitted. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 37b" href="/Beitzah.37b">Babli <i>Beṣah</i> 37b</a>.</i>. Rav said, limbs suck from one another. Ulla bar Ismael said, limbs do not suck from one another<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rav the partners can move the parts only in the common domain; according to Ulla bar Ismael each owner may move his part anywhere in his domain.</i>. Rav agrees about an amphora that it was his part from before<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was determined that <i>eruv</i> is a rabbinic institution one accepts that a later distribution retroactively determines property rights. By biblical standards retroactivity is excluded.</i>.
So is the Mishnah: If Gentiles come from both sides, if Gentiles do not come from either side<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">For לא באו one has to read אם לא באו.</i>. “If Gentiles come from the East my <i>eruv</i> shall be in the West.” Some Tannaim state, to the East<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">If the Gentiles come to the East, his eruv shall be to the East. Why should he want to go to the East?</i>. He who says to the East, about these officers<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ταξεώτης, -ου ὁ, “officer of a magistrate, member of <i>militia palatina</i>”, whom it is important to humor.</i>, he who says to the West, about these Romans<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Common soldiers, notoriously badly disciplined in the later Empire.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a Sage comes from the East my <i>eruv</i> shall be to the East, if he comes from the West my <i>eruv</i> shall be to the West, on both sides I shall be able to go where I want<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">He has no preacher at his place; he intends to go to any place where there is instruction. If two preachers come he may choose which lecture to attend.</i>, if they come from neither side I shall be like the people of my village.<br>Rebbi Jehudah says, if one of them was his teacher he has to go to his teacher<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since we presume that his intention is to hear his teacher, the second <i>eruv</i> becomes void automatically.</i>, if both were his teachers he may go to where he wants.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “If a Sage comes from the East,” etc. Some Tannaim state, to the West. He who says, in the East, about these Sages. He who says, in the West, about a fundraiser<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">S. Liebermann, <i>Tarbiz</i> 4 (1933) pp. 378–379.</i>.
Paragraph. “Rebbi Jehudah says, if one of them was,” etc. Rebbi Jehudah says <sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Added from G; missing in L and the printed editions. It probably was omitted since it flatly contradicts the preceding statement from the discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.6.1">Mishnah 6</a>. But that describes what people do while in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.6.1">Mishnah 7</a> R. Jehudah prescribes what people should do.</i>[if one of them was a fundraiser, he should go to the fundraiser; if both of them were fundraisers he may go to the place of his choosing.]

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Eliezer says, if a holiday is next to a Sabbath either before or after, a person may make two <i>eruvin</i> and say: my first <i>eruv</i> shall be to the East and the second to the West, the first to the West and the second to the East, my first <i>eruv</i> but on the second like the people of my town. My <i>eruv</i> for the first [day], but the second like the people of my town; the second [day], but the first like the people of my town.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since on a holiday one may carry, by necessity this refers to <i>eruv teḥumim</i>. R. Eliezer holds that since Sabbath and holiday are distinct entities, one may make different <i>eruvim</i> for the two days. The Sages agree but they hold that an <i>eruv</i> can be made only in preparation on a working day; therefore a different <i>eruv</i> for the second day can never become effective.</i>.<br>But the Sages say, either one makes an <i>eruv</i> to one side or one does not make an <i>eruv</i> at all; one makes an <i>eruv</i> for both days or one does not make an <i>eruv</i> at all<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since on a holiday one may carry, by necessity this refers to <i>eruv teḥumim</i>. R. Eliezer holds that since Sabbath and holiday are distinct entities, one may make different <i>eruvim</i> for the two days. The Sages agree but they hold that an <i>eruv</i> can be made only in preparation on a working day; therefore a different <i>eruv</i> for the second day can never become effective.</i>. What should he do? He brings it on the first day, stays there when it gets dark, takes it and goes home, On the second day he brings it, stays there when it gets dark, eats it and goes home<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This works only if Friday is a holiday. He brings the <i>eruv</i> on Thursday to the place he has selected, waits there for the start of the holiday to enter into force and takes the <i>eruv</i> home lest it become lost. On Friday he brings the <i>eruv</i> back to the place he chose the day before, waits for the start of the Sabbath but then, since he is forbidden to carry on the Sabbath, eats the <i>eruv</i> before returning to his house.</i>. It turns out that he profits from his going and his <i>eruv</i>. If it is eaten on the first day, it is an <i>eruv</i> for the first but is not an <i>eruv</i> for the second. Rebbi Eliezer told them, you agree with me that they are two kinds if sanctities<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Sages agree that the <i>eruv</i> must exist at the beginning of the Sabbath they imply that the Sabbath is not the extension of the holiday.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Eliezer says,” etc. “But the Sages say, either one makes an <i>eruv</i> to one side.” So is the Mishnah: Either one makes an <i>eruv</i> to one side for two days or one does not make an <i>eruv</i> at all<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">The words “in one direction” are missing in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.7.1">Mishnah 9</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer agrees that one cannot make an <i>eruv</i> for half a day in the South and one for half a day in the North, for half a day in the South and one for half a day (in the North)<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">The words in parentheses are clearly out of place but they are also found in G.</i> for the people of his place. They told him, just as you did not split the day for us, so do not split two days for us<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.7.1">Note 22</a>. Even though the sanctity of the Sabbath is different in kind from that of the holiday, for the <i>eruv</i> to become effective on the Sabbath one would have to invalidate the <i>eruv</i> for the holiday some time before sundown.</i>. They agree that he should not put them<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>eruvim</i> for the two days.</i> in a basket. Rebbi Abba said, that he should not forget and eat it. If he did put it into a basket, he should bring the basket there. If he eats one of them he is a donkey driver-camel driver<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">If it cannot be determined which day’s <i>eruv</i> was eaten both are potentially valid or invalid both days.</i>.
There we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ṭevul Yom</i> 2:2.</i>: “A flask<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λάγυνος, ὁ.</i> which had been immersed on the same day and [they filled it]<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Added from G and the Mishnah text.</i> from an amphora with <i>ṭevel</i> tithe, if he said that this shall be heave of the tithe after nightfall then it is heave of the tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Purification from impurity is in two steps. Immersion in a <i>miqweh</i> removes the impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:6" href="/Leviticus.22.6"><i>Lev</i>. 22:6</a>) but only the following sundown creates purity for <i>sancta</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:7" href="/Leviticus.22.7"><i>Lev</i>. 22:7</a>). Tithe given to the Levite is forbidden for everybody as <i>ṭevel</i> before heave of the tithe has been separated; the heave is a <i>sanctum</i> for the Cohen, the remainder becomes purely profane in the Levite’s hand. The <i>ṭevel</i> filled into the flask is not a sanctum; since the flask is not impure it does not change the status of the fluid which it contains. After sundown it becomes pure for <i>sancta</i>; if at the same point in time the contents become heave, the heave is pure.</i>. If he said this is <i>eruv</i>, he did not say anything.<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Since an <i>eruv</i> must be valid before sundown but at that time the contents of the flask still were <i>ṭevel</i> and forbidden as food even to the Cohen, the <i>eruv</i> is invalid.</i>” Rebbi Jonah said, Rebbi Ḥama bar (Aqiba) [Uqba]<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The correct reading is from [G], the incorrect one from (L).</i> asked, is this Mishnah not following Rebbi Eliezer, since Rebbi Eliezer said, one does not make an <i>eruv</i> for a person after nightfall<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is the reason while the <i>eruv</i> is invalid.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Zeˋira: There it is everybody’s opinion. But here he acquires a place for Sabbath rest for two days while it still is a working day<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbis also hold that on the day itself one cannot make an <i>eruv</i>; the Mishnah in <i>Ṭevul Yom</i> has no relevance for the disagreement here.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked, if it was Thursday and he said, this should acquire for me a place for Sabbath rest, in Rebbi Eliezer’s opinion he acquired, in (Rav’s) [the rabbis’]<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The correct reading is from [G], the incorrect one from (L).</i> opinion did he not acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Eliezer permits to make on Thursday a separate <i>eruv</i> for the Sabbath if Friday is a holiday, he certainly must permit making on Thursday an <i>eruv</i> for a regular Sabbath. But the rabbis’ opinion in this matter cannot be determined from the Mishnah.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, why can a person not make an <i>eruv</i> from holiday to Sabbath? Because a person makes an <i>eruv</i> from Sabbath eve to the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">Rashba (col. 239) and Meïri (col. 133b) emend the text to read: Because a person <i>cannot make</i> an <i>eruv</i> from Sabbath eve (which is a holiday) to the Sabbath. If one refrains from emending the text one has to read the sentence as a rhetorical question: Because could a person make an <i>eruv</i> from Sabbath eve (which is a holiday) to the Sabbath?</i>. But here, since he can make an <i>eruv</i> from Sabbath eve to the Sabbath, he may make an <i>eruv</i> even from Thursday to the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">If there is no holiday intervening.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">A slightly different text <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 4:1" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).4.1">Tosephta 4:1</a>; still differently <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 39a" href="/Eruvin.39a">Babli 39a</a>.</i>“Rebbi Eliezer said to them, do you not agree with me that if he used a loaf to make an <i>eruv</i> for the first day, that he may use a loaf to make an <i>eruv</i> for the second day, that if he ate it on the first day it is an <i>eruv</i> for the first day but is not an <i>eruv</i> for the second? Therefore these are two sanctities. They answered him, do you not agree with us that a person does not make a new <i>eruv</i> on a holiday? Therefore it is one sanctity.”

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah said, if a person was afraid that the New Year’s Day might be complemented<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the times when the calendar was not computed but the day of the New Moon was determined by observation. Outside the seat of the Synhedrion the day of the New Moon of Tishre, which is New Year’s Day in the Baylonian calendar, had to be observed for two days since it could be either the 30<sup>th</sup> or the 31<sup>st</sup> of Elul. This is the only holiday falling on a New Moon. While the holiday lasts two days, it is clear that this is not a question of two distinct sanctities but in fact one of the days is really a weekday, the other a holiday, only it is not known which is which. Rebbi Jehudah, the student of R. Eliezer’s student, follows R. Eliezer and permits two different <i>eruvin</i>. The Sages hold that the two days are legally one long holiday.</i>, he makes two <i>eruvin</i> and says, my first <i>eruv</i> should be to the East and the second to the West, the first to the West and the second to the East, my <i>eruv</i> for the first day and on the second like the people of my village, for the second day but the first day like the people of my village. But the Sages did not agree with him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “Rebbi Jehudah said, if a person was afraid that the New Year’s Day might be complemented<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the times when the calendar was not computed but the day of the New Moon was determined by observation. Outside the seat of the Synhedrion the day of the New Moon of Tishre, which is New Year’s Day in the Baylonian calendar, had to be observed for two days since it could be either the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> or the 31<sup class="footnote-marker">st</sup> of Elul. This is the only holiday falling on a New Moon. While the holiday lasts two days, it is clear that this is not a question of two distinct sanctities but in fact one of the days is really a weekday, the other a holiday, only it is not known which is which. Rebbi Jehudah, the student of R. Eliezer’s student, follows R. Eliezer and permits two different <i>eruvin</i>. The Sages hold that the two days are legally one long holiday.</i>,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 9:1:8-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.9.1.8-9"><i>Ševiˋit</i> 9:1 (Notes 24–30</a>,7) and <i>Beṣah</i> 1:1 (60a l. 62,י). The latter text is the original. The introductory sentence, “because they disagreed” makes no sense here but is intelligible in the two parallel sources.<br>While the starting quote is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.8.1">Mishnah 10</a>, the entire discussion refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.7.1">Mishnah 9</a>, a Sabbath preceded or followed by a holiday which is not New Year’s Day.</i> Because they disagreed: May the remainders of a wick, a fire, or oil that burned out on the Sabbath be lit on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">If the holiday falls on a Sunday, since it is forbidden to light fire on the Sabbath, the unburned oil or wood that was burning at the onset of the Sabbath, but later burned out or was extinguished by the wind are no longer usable on the Sabbath, they become <i>muqṣeh</i>. The rule that (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:5" href="/Exodus.16.5"><i>Ex</i>. 16:5</a>) <i>they have to prepare what they are</i> G<i>oin</i> G<i>to use</i> is given for the Sabbath but is transferred to holidays. Then since oil or wood were not usable a minute before the start of the holiday they cannot become usable on the holiday. If the rule is not transferred, the fuel becomes permitted at the end of the Sabbath.</i>? Rav and Rebbi Ḥanina both say it is forbidden, but Rebbi Joḥanan says, it is permitted. Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yudan, is it the reverse regarding an egg<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:1:2-10:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.1.2-10.3">Mishnah <i>Beṣah</i> 1:1</a>: “An egg laid on the holiday, the House of Shammai say, may be eaten; the House of Hillel say, it may not be eaten.” According to the Yerushalmi, in the opinion of the House of Shammai the egg is prepared since the hen itself is potential food and an egg inside a slaughtered hen is permitted food. According to the House of Hillel, the egg becomes something new by being laid. The new egg was not in existence before the holiday.</i>? He said to him, since the rabbis compare it, it means that the two cases are identical<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">At least for the House of Hillel. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta <i>Yom Tov</i> 1:3</a>: “An egg laid on the Sabbath may be eaten on the holiday. R. Jehudah said in the name of R. Eliezer, the dispute is the same.”</i>. They said in the name of four Elders<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 38b" href="/Eruvin.38b">Babli 38b</a> the Elders are Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, R. Ismael ben R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa, R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon, and R. Yose ben R. Jehudah. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 4:2" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).4.2">Tosephta 4:2</a> the statement is R. Meïr’s.</i>: If somebody’s <i>eruv</i> was eaten on the first [day] he is like the people of his town on the second [day]. Rav Ḥuna said in the name of Rav: Practice follows the four Elders<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">“There” is <i>Eruvin</i>, “here” is <i>Beṣah</i>, about the leftover fuel. If the status regarding the <i>eruv</i> can change from day to day, it is difficult to see why using the fuel should be forbidden. The Babli notes that Rav Ḥisda asked his question only after Rav Ḥuna’s death.</i>. Rav Ḥisda asked: the argument of Rav seems to be inverted. There, he makes it two sanctities, but here he makes it one sanctity. Because they disagreed: May the remainders of a wick, a fire, or oil that burned out on the Sabbath be lit on the holiday? Rav and Rebbi Ḥanina both say it is forbidden, but Rebbi Joḥanan says, it is permitted. Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yudan, is it the reverse regarding an egg? He said to him, since the rabbis compare it, it means that the two cases are identical<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Aḥa it is a tradition which he learned from his teacher, for R. Yose it is a logical inference.</i>.
Rebbi Aḥa said it from study, Rebbi Yose said it from reflection<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Aḥa it is a tradition which he learned from his teacher, for R. Yose it is a logical inference.</i>; the four Elders concur with Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliezer holds that on two consecutive days, one a Sabbath and one a holiday, a person may make two different <i>eruvim</i>. The Elders hold that if the <i>eruv</i> is nonexistent at the start of the second day, the person is no longer bound by it and therefore moves in a different domain on the second day.</i>. Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yose: between Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Jehudah, does practice not follow Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">A generally recognized principle, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 46b" href="/Eruvin.46b">Babli 46b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 3:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.3.1.6">Yerushalmi <i>Terumot</i> 3:1 (Note 26</a>).</i>? The four Elders concur with Rebbi Eliezer! Since there where his <i>eruv</i> did not enter in force did I not say that he removed his feet from the inhabitants of his town, here where his <i>eruv</i> did enter in force not so much more<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">The argument here and in the following refers to the disagreements between R. Meïr and R. Jehudah in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.4.1">Mishnaiot 4:10–11</a>. In Mishnah 10 R. Meïr permits only a poor person to make an <i>eruv</i> without anything, just by being at the Sabbath boundary at the start of the Sabbath, as if he were a traveller arriving at that spot which becomes his Sabbath center. R. Jehudah permits the same for everybody. Mishnah 11 is about a person sent to make an <i>eruv</i> for an entire village when on the way he was persuaded by somebody not to complete his errand where R. Jehudah, whom practice follows, holds consistently with his opinion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.8.1">Mishnah 10</a> that he can walk as if he made the <i>eruv</i> since near sundown he was on his way.<br>In case of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>:11 the fact that he started going away lets him move his Sabbath domain without an <i>eruv</i>, in the case of the four elders where there was a valid <i>eruv</i> for the first day it is difficult to understand why the <i>eruv</i> should have disappeared for the second day unless one follows R. Eliezer whom practice is not supposed to follow.</i>? If he made an <i>eruv</i> with a loaf on the first [day], he makes the <i>eruv</i> with a loaf on the second, but it must be the same loaf. If he made an <i>eruv</i> with his feet on the first [day], he makes the <i>eruv</i> with his feet on the second<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 39a" href="/Eruvin.39a">Babli 39a</a> in the name of Samuel. As the Babli explains, one may not start a new <i>eruv</i> on a Sabbath or holiday; therefore either one uses the loaf which had been used for the first day or one makes the <i>eruv</i> by walking following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.8.1">Mishnah 4:10</a>.</i>. If he made an <i>eruv</i> with his feet, may he make an <i>eruv</i> with a loaf, with a loaf may he not make an <i>eruv</i> with his feet? Since you are saying there, with his feet he may not make an <i>eruv</i> with a loaf, then with (his feet) [with a loaf]<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe wrote “his feet”, the corrector wrongly corrected to “a loaf”.</i> may he not make the <i>eruv</i> with his feet<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">If one may not start a new <i>eruv</i> on a Sabbath or holiday; then maybe one may not go to validate the <i>eruv</i> for the second day since it will look as if one made it new for that day.</i>? To make it easy for the poor person who has no loaf. Since you are saying there, with his feet he may not make an <i>eruv</i> with a loaf, then with with a loaf may he not make the <i>eruv</i> with his feet? To make it easy for the rich person that he does not have to go and make the <i>eruv</i> with his feet<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">Using the loaf first and then walking there also looks as if the <i>eruv</i> for the second day were different from that for the first and should be forbidden.</i>. Since you are saying there, with his feet he may not make an <i>eruv</i> with a loaf, then with a loaf may he not make the <i>eruv</i> with a loaf? Rebbi Abun in the name of the rabbis there, they made him as if going to his town<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">Using the loaf twice will not look like making two <i>eruvim</i> but since the loaf was at its place at sundown the first day he only goes home to where his Sabbath or holiday center was at the beginning of the first day.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> In addition, Rebbi Jehudah says that a person may stipulate about a basket on the first day<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Still about New Year’s day. <i>Ṭevel</i> may not be eaten; heave and tithes may not be taken on a holiday. R. Jehudah permits to designate heave and tithes on the first day after a declaration that heave and tithe are designated if and only if the day was a weekday. If the same declaration is repeated on the second day, exactly one of them is valid and the remainder of the produce in the basket may be consumed.</i> and eat from it on the second; similarly an egg which was laid on the first may be eaten on the second<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since the egg either was laid or consumed on a weekday.</i>; but the Sages did not agree with him<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since the two days are legally one long holiday and the egg was not food at the start of the holiday.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “And he says if today is holiday my words should be void but he gives a name to his tithes but does not eat from it. On the second [day] he gives a name to his tithes and eats from it.<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).4.3">Tosephta 4:3</a>, attributed to R. Jehudah. This refers to Mishnah 11, about a person who on the New Year wants to eat <i>ṭevel</i> produce. He cannot eat it on the first day, but if on both days he designates the same produce as heave and tithe, one of the designations will have been made on a non-holiday (presuming that none of the two is a Sabbath) and the remainder may be consumed on the second day.</i>” Rav’s<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">This must read “R. Jehudah’s” even though G agrees with L in reading “Rav’s”.</i> argument seems inverted. There he treats it as one sanctity but here he treats it as two sanctities. There it is one long sanctity but here one is holy, the other profane<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">If either Friday or Sunday is a holiday it was established that R. Jehudah disagrees with the four Elders and holds that both Sabbath and holiday together form one sanctity and an <i>eruv</i> is either valid for both or not valid at all. But the two days of the New Year he treats as different sanctities.</i>. The rabbis’ argument seems inverted. There they treat it as two sanctities, but here they make it one sanctity. There a holiday next to a Sabbath is like weekday next to Sabbath but here both are equal<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">Since practice follows the four Elders who permit two different domains for Sabbath and holiday but consider the two days of the New year as one long day.</i>. The Sages agree with Rebbi Jehudah about the two days of the New Year which are an institution of the early prophets<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">That the two days of the New Year be kept as holidays even in the Land of Israel and treated as one sanctity.</i>
<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Midrash Cant. rabba</i> 1(43), <i>Pesiqta dRav Cahana Šimˋu</i> (ed. Buber p. 118a).</i> Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 1:6" href="/Song_of_Songs.1.6"><i>Cant</i>. 1:6</a>.</i>: <i>My mother’s sons bore ill-will against me,</i> (etc.) [<i>they made me a guard of the vineyards; my vineyard I did not guard</i>.]<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">Addition from G.</i> What caused me to guard the vineyards? Because <i>my vineyard I did not guard</i>. What caused me to keep two days in Syria<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">Those parts of the kingdom of David and Salomon which were not part of the Second Commonwealth; its status was intermediary between that of the Land and the diaspora but holidays were kept there for two days as in the diaspora; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 7:5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.7.5.6"><i>Peah</i> 7:6 Note 119</a>.</i>? Because I did not keep one day in the Land. I was thinking that I would be rewarded for both, but I am rewarded only for one. What caused me to separate two <i>ḥallot</i> in Syria<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">Biblical law prescribes <i>ḥallah</i> only for produce of the Land (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:18-19" href="/Numbers.15.18-19"><i>Num</i>. 15:18–19</a>). Rabbinic practice extends the obligation to the rest of the world but, since the soil outside the Land is intrinsically impure, any <i>ḥallah</i> outside the Land is impure and must be burned. In Syria, some dough has to be given to the Cohen to be consumed in imitation of the obligation in the Land; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 4:4:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.4.4.2-5"><i>Ḥallah</i> 4:7</a>.</i>? Because I did not separate one <i>ḥallah</i> in the Land. I was thinking that I would be rewarded for both, but I am rewarded only for one. Rebbi Joḥanan read for them, <i>but I</i> G<i>ave them prescriptions which are not</i> G<i>ood</i>[<i>and laws by which they cannot live</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 20:25" href="/Ezekiel.20.25"><i>Ez</i>. 20:25</a>. He considers the second day of a holiday in the diaspora as a kind of punishment.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu went to Alexandria and made them carry <i>lulavim</i> on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">In a year in which the first day of <i>Sukkot</i> was a Sabbath he told the Jews of Alexandria to keep only one day of the holiday since he knew the (as yet unpublished) rules of calendar computation. Since the obligation to take the “four kinds” essentially is restricted to the first day (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev</i>. 23:40</a>) he permitted the ceremony on a Sabbath against the rule that in the diaspora the “four kinds” may not be taken on the Sabbath. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:11:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.11.2"><i>Sukkah</i> 3:13</a>).</i>. Rebbi Immi heard it and said, who (feeds them) [brings them]<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">The text of G [in brackets] is preferable over that of L (in parentheses).</i> Rebbi Abbahu every year<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">In the absence of a published calendar, the exact computation of the date of the holiday depends on the presence of a competent scholar, which is rare outside the Land.</i>? Rebbi Yose sent and wrote to them: even though we wrote you the order of holidays, do not change the usage of your departed ancestors<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">The action of R. Abbahu is officially disapproved of with the publication of the calendar rules. While today anybody who knows how to add and subtract can compute the Jewish calendar and there is no longer any ambiguity in fixing the holidays, the calendar rules were published by the Academy of R. Yose (the talmudic sources do not mention any participation of the Patriarch) with the explicit stipulation that they may be used only if the second day of holidays be observed outside the Land (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 4b" href="/Beitzah.4b">Babli <i>Besah</i> 4b</a>). {Quoted by R. Ḥananel <i>ad</i>40b, <i>Roqeaḥ</i> §198, <i>Maˋase Roqeaḥ</i> §93, <i>Or zaruaˋ</i> §140 (vol. 2 p. 211a in the edition Jerusalem 2010).}</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Dosa ben Hyrkanos said, the reader<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Who leads the congregation in prayer and has to mention the holiday. The Sages reject conditional prayers and therefore hold that the New Moon should not be mentioned in the prayers of New Year’s day.</i> on New Year’s Day says “save us, Eternal, our God, on this Day of the New Moon, whether it be today or tomorrow.” The next day he says, “whether it be today or was yesterday”; but the Sages did not agree with him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph: “Rebbi Dosa ben Hyrkanos said, the reader<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Who leads the congregation in prayer and has to mention the holiday. The Sages reject conditional prayers and therefore hold that the New Moon should not be mentioned in the prayers of New Year’s day.</i>”, etc. Because<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages might disagree with R. Dosa because one does not formulate prayers intimating that the day might not be holy and holiday prayers inappropriate.</i> he says “whether it be today or tomorrow.” Or it comes following what Rebbi Jacob the son of Rebbi Aḥa said, the reader<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Who leads the congregation in prayer and has to mention the holiday. The Sages reject conditional prayers and therefore hold that the New Moon should not be mentioned in the prayers of New Year’s day.</i> on New Year’s Day does not have to mention the New Moon<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>musaf</i> prayer of the holiday the fact that it is a Day of New Moon is mentioned only indirectly in the quote of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 10:10" href="/Numbers.10.10"><i>Num</i>. 10:10</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 40b" href="/Eruvin.40b">Babli 40b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.4.5"><i>Ševuˋot</i> 1:5 Note 156</a>).</i>. Also here it is so.

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody was taken out by Gentiles or an evil spirit<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">He was insane and therefore not responsible when he left. He regained his senses outside and now must follow the rules of the Sabbath.</i> he only has four cubits. If they brought him back<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentiles who had removed him without his cooperation brought him back without his initiative.</i> it is as if he had not left. If they conducted him to another town or put him into a sheep-pen, or a sheep corral<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.3.1">Mishnah 2:3</a>. These places are not human dwellings but they are fenced in, which makes them similar to private domains.</i>, Rabban Gamliel and Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah say he may walk its entirety; Rebbi Joshua and Rebbi Aqiba say, he only has four cubits. It happened that they came from Brundisium<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">With most Mishnah mss. read פרנדיסין, Brundisium, the usual harbor of departure for a trip from Italy to Palestine. The ship left on the Sabbath.</i> when the ship went out to sea; Rabban Gamliel and Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah walked its entirety<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">While they left the Sabbath domain of Brindisi on the Sabbath, this was a consequence of the Gentile mariner’s action. Since on dry land one permits in such a case to walk the entire town in which they happen to find themselves, they may walk on the ship as their town.</i>; Rebbi Joshua and Rebbi Aqiba did not stir out of their four cubits, for they wanted to be strict with themselves.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody was taken out by Gentiles,” etc. Otherwise<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Translating G; the text of L makes little sense.</i> what would we say? He should be considered like someone who left with permission<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since he did not leave intentionally or even accidentally. Either he was taken by force or he left when insane and not obligated to keep any commandment.</i> and he should have 2’000 cubits in every direction. Therefore [it is necessary]<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Added from G.</i> to say that “he only has four cubits.” From where the four cubits? <i>Stay everybody at his spot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:29" href="/Exodus.16.29"><i>Ex</i>. 16:29</a>.</i>. And from where 2’000 cubits? <i>A person should not leave his place on the Seventh Day</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:29" href="/Exodus.16.29"><i>Ex</i>. 16:29</a>.</i>. Or switching, as (we have stated) [it was stated so:]<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The correct text is that of G in brackets since the quote is a <i>baraita</i>, not a Mishnah as implied by the text of L (in parentheses). <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i>, ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 262 (<i>Yalqut Shimˋony Qonteros Aḥaron</i>, ed. Ginzberg p. 318, <i>Siddur Rash</i> i #461.)</i> “Issy ben Aqiba says “place, place”<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">An argument by “equal cut”; the meaning of a word does not change from one use in the Pentateuch to another.</i>. It is said here <i>place</i>, and it is said there, <i>I shall</i> G<i>ive you a place</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:13" href="/Exodus.21.13"><i>Ex</i>. 21:13</a>. The reference is to the cities of refuge which as levitic cities included a domain extending 2’000 cubits outside the built-up area.</i>. Since the <i>place</i> mentioned there includes 2’000 cubits, so the <i>place</i> mentioned here includes 2’000 cubits.” <sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 3:11" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).3.11">Tosephta 3:11</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 48a" href="/Eruvin.48a">Babli 48a</a>, Tanḥuma Buber <i>Bemidbar</i> #9 (p. 8, Note 74)</i>“The four cubits which they mentioned are his full length and outstretched arms<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">When he lies down with arms out-stretched behind his head. These justifications point more to a (Greek) cubit of 63cm than one of 55cm; cf. the Introduction.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, he and three cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the Tosephta seems to make more sense: “3 cubits for his body and in addition 4 cubits so he can take …”. This reading is required since the bottom of an amphora is at least one cubit in diameter; if he is permitted to move it from above his head to below his feet he already has at least 5 cubits.</i>, so he can take an amphora from above his head and put it below his feet.” If he relieves himself, he relieves himself at the side; if he prays, he prays in the diagonal<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of this sentence is not clear; every explanation must be tentative. Since a person may not stay in two different domains on one Sabbath, and since the person started the Sabbath in his domain, it would be logical to infer that once he leaves this domain he is without a domain and if his 4 cubits intersect with an established Sabbath domain he may walk through the entire domain. But if his 4 feet constitute his new domain then intersection with any other domain is irrelevant for him.</i>.
Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi asked, the four cubits which they mentioned: are they a domain or are they not a domain<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of this sentence is not clear; every explanation must be tentative. Since a person may not stay in two different domains on one Sabbath, and since the person started the Sabbath in his domain, it would be logical to infer that once he leaves this domain he is without a domain and if his 4 cubits intersect with an established Sabbath domain he may walk through the entire domain. But if his 4 feet constitute his new domain then intersection with any other domain is irrelevant for him.</i>? If you say that they are a domain, one does not give him four cubits from two places. If you say that they are not a domain, one gives him four cubits from two places. Rebbi Zeˋira said, the Mishnah says that the four cubits which they mentioned are a domain, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.6.1">Mishnah 6</a>, referring to people keeping the Sabbath on the road, who never had another Sabbath domain, and for whom there should be no question.</i>, “on condition that one not export into the other’s [domain].”
It only said, “if they brought him back.” Therefore, if he returned by himself, he is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The same inference drawn in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 41b" href="/Eruvin.41b">Babli, 41b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa said, this is Rebbi Neḥemiah’s. As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 41b-42a" href="/Eruvin.41b-42a">Babli, 41b/42a</a>, the statement is about produce which was taken outside the Sabbath domain. The text here should not be amended since it is quoted in its original form in <i>Roqeaḥ</i> #184. and is implied by the quote in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Eruvin 42a:6:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Eruvin.42a.6.1">Tosaphot 42a <i>s.v</i>. מכלל</a>. Since the food was moved illegally from its domain it becomes rabbinically prohibited for use on the Sabbath. The prohibition cannot be biblical since the produce itself was not changed (Maimonides <i>Hilkhot Šabbat</i> 6:24); it has the character of a fine.</i>: “Produce which came into the public domain and returned, if in error it may be eaten, if intentional it may not be eaten. Rebbi Neḥemiah says, whether in error or intentional it may not be eaten unless it was returned in error.” Rebbi Neḥemiah parallels Rebbi Meïr, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 3:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.3.1.10"><i>Šabbat</i> 3, Note 39</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 2:1:19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.2.1.19"><i>Terumot</i> 2:3 Note 70</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 2:15" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).2.15">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i> 2:15</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 34a" href="/Ketubot.34a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 34a</a>.</i>: “If somebody tithed or cooked on the Sabbath, if in error it may be eaten, if intentional it may not be eaten, the words of Rebbi Meïr.”
If he acquired a place of Sabbath rest<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">A person staying on the road during the Sabbath, outside of town. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 42a" href="/Eruvin.42a">Babli 42a</a>.</i> when Gentiles came and surrounded him with a barrier. Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Read: “Rav”.</i> Ḥuna said, the barrier is of no use for him<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">He denies the validity of any barrier made on the Sabbath.</i> but he may walk 2’000 [cubits] and carry within four. Ḥiyya the son of Rav said, he walks and carries within 2’000<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">He accepts the validity of a barrier erected for the Sabbath that it permits carrying. If the barrier had been made before the Sabbath and for human dwelling, the area enclosed by the barrier would be the dwelling and the Sabbath domain would extend outside for 2’000 cubits in every direction. But since the barrier was erected on the Sabbath, the domain where he may carry is extended but not the one where he can go (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Eruvin 42a" href="/Rashi_on_Eruvin.42a">Rashi, 42a</a>).</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Abuna in the name of Ḥiyya the son of Rav: He walks within 2’000 and carries within 4’000 cubits by throwing<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the object thrown comes to rest within the barrier no Sabbath violation occurred, the restriction to 2’000 cubits being rabbinic in nature.</i>. They wanted to say, their disagreement is following Rebbi Joshua and Rebbi Aqiba; but following Rabban Gamliel and Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah they do not disagree. Even following Rabban Gamliel and Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah they disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is documented in the writings of Spanish/Provençal Medieval authors (Rashba <i>ad</i> 42b col. 261, Ritba p. 74c, Meïri p. 159a), but it was missing in the Yerushalmi text available to Ashkenazi authors who assert that Rav Huna and Ḥiyya bar Rav must follow R. Joshua and R. Aqiba (Tosaphot 42a s.v. נכרים, <i>Or zarua</i> part 2 §144, p. 215b, Rosh Chapter 4 #2).</i>; one who had acquired a place of Sabbath rest is in better shape than one who had not acquired a place of Sabbath rest<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">There is less reason to be lenient with a person forcibly abducted from his domain than with one who stays in his domain, only that the circumstances changed without his action.</i>.
If they deposited him at the door of the sheep-pen<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This now refers directly to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>, about a person abducted by Gentiles on the Sabbath.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Joshua and Rebbi Aqiba one gives him two cubits inside and two cubits outside. In the opinion of Rabban Gamliel and Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah one does not allow two cubits inside and two cubits outside, for if you would say that one allows him two cubits inside and two cubits outside then he could carry in the entire sheep-pen<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Once one agrees that he can enter the sheep-pen, according to Rabban Gamliel he has the entire enclosed area (at least if this area is no more than 5’000 cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>).</i>. If they deposited him in the middle of the sheep-pen and the sheep-pen was partially inside the Sabbath domain and partially outside the Sabbath domain. Rebbi Aḥa said it from study, Rebbi Jehudah said it from reflection<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Even though it was stated (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.1.5">Note 43</a>) that the cases cannot be compared, the basic arguments of Rav Huna and Ḥiyya ben Rav can be applied here. Since for Ḥiyya the person may carry into the Sabbath domain, it is as if the Gentiles returned him and it is as if he had not left. For Rav Huna he must stay where he is (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.1.3">Note 33</a>).</i>: This is the disagreement of Rav Ḥuna and of Ḥiyya the son of Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah states that they only stayed within their 4 cubits as personal restriction but not because they insisted that this was practice to be followed.</i>.
Rebbi Zeˋira, Rav Ḥuna in the name of Rav: Since Rebbi Joshua and Rebbi Aqiba wanted to be strict with themselves, this implies that practice follows Rabban Gamliel and Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah states that they only stayed within their 4 cubits as personal restriction but not because they insisted that this was practice to be followed.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ashi: also for a sheep-pen, or a sheep corral, practice follows Rabban Gamliel and Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 42b" href="/Eruvin.42b">Babli 42b</a> in the name of Rav.</i>. Ḥananiah the son of Rebbi Joshua’s brother says, the entire day they were arguing one with the other until my father’s brother came, decided between then, and instituted that practice follow Rabban Gamliel and Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah for a ship and Rebbi Joshua and Rebbi Aqiba for a sheep-pen, or a sheep corral<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 43a" href="/Eruvin.43a">Babli 43a</a>.</i>. What is the difference between a sheep corral and a ship? The colleagues say, for a ship because its walls are part of itself<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Since the deck of the ship is enclosed by its railing the passenger does not necessarily notice the ship’s motion; for him nothing changes during the day.</i>; Rebbi Zeˋira said, because one removes it from these four cubits and puts it into those four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 42b" href="/Eruvin.42b">Babli 42b</a>. Since the ship is permanently in motion, if one would define the Sabbath domain by the place on the surface of the earth where the person was at nightfall, he could not move at all, not even 4 cubits, since every moment he is at another place.</i>. What difference results between them? If it was a covered walkway<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἐξέδρα, ή. The <i>exedra</i> has no walls. In analogy one considers a ship without railing, perhaps a raft.</i>, according to the colleagues it is permitted, according to Rebbi Zeˋira it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">One has to switch the statements. Since there is no railing, the argument of the colleagues does not apply; the person is restricted to his 4 cubits. For R. Zeˋira the absence of a railing does not make any difference.</i>. If the sea was calm, according to the colleagues it is forbidden, according to Rebbi Zeˋira it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Greek γαληνός, -ή, -όν “calm” (adj.). Assuming the ship being a sailboat which does not move in the absence of wind, it is difficult to see why walking the entire deck should be forbidden by anybody. It seems unlikely that here also one should switch the statements: for the colleagues there is no reason to forbid; for R. Zeˋira there might be an argument that he expected to be elsewhere on the Sabbath and now he is not there.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira said, the Mishnah says that the four cubits which they mentioned are not a domain<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Earlier R. Zeˋira is quoted as stating the opposite (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.1.3">Note 33</a>). But there the statement was about Sabbath domains on the ground, here the reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> and the topic is domains on a ship. This makes good sense; there is no need to emend the text by switching the statements of R. Zeˋira and the colleagues as proposed by S. Liebermann.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Once they only entered the port<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This is Babli spelling. With the Halakhah one should read לִמֵן, Greek λιμήν. They arrived at the harbor Friday evening after nightfall. If they entered the Sabbath domain after nightfall, RR. Joshua and Aqiba would have to stay within their 4 cubits. For the problem of Rabban Gamliel and R. Eleazar ben Azariah see the Halakhah (Notes 57–59).</i> after nightfall. They asked Rabban Gamliel, may we descend? He told them it is permitted since I already was observing and we were inside the Sabbath domain before nightfall.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “Once they only entered the port,” etc. Rabban Gamliel had a telescope<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of the text’s מצודית “citadels; traps” reading with R. Ḥananel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 43b" href="/Eruvin.43b"><i>ad</i> Babli 43b</a>), <i>Sefer Haˋitim</i> (p. 54, Note 72), <i>Raviah</i>§386 (p. 417 Note 14), <i>Or zarua</i> II §6(2) (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 12a" href="/Eruvin.12a">p. 12a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.1.7">Note 51</a> there is incorrect), and a similar expression in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 5:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.5.3.3">Chapter 5 (Note 120</a>): מְצוֹפִית “an instrument to see far”. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 43b" href="/Eruvin.43b">Babli 43b</a> שפופרת “a tube”.</i> where he could estimate with his eyes in the plane. And why before nightfall? Even after nightfall; is this not Rabban Gamliel’s<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Since the harbor is enclosed it should not be different from a sheep-pen where Rabban Gamliel permits to walk the entire domain.</i>? Explain it if the port was larger than two <i>bet se’ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Then the enclosure does not define a domain for the Sabbath unless it was for human dwelling, which a harbor may not be.</i>, or the walls were not ten [hand-breadths] high<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The minimum height for an enclosure, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.8.1">Mishnah 1:8</a>.</i> but were not open wider than ten [cubits] nor what was standing was opposite what was standing and what was breached opposite the breached<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.8.2">Chapter 1, Note 241</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody left with permission<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">If a border town is attacked by marauders, it may ask the nearby towns for armed help.</i> when they told him that the deed already had been done he has 2’000 cubits in each direction. If this reached him inside the Sabbath domain<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">If at the moment when the help is no longer needed the person was no more than 1’999 cubits from the border of the Sabbath domain of his home town, he may reach this domain and, since he left with permission, walks all the way to his house. If the built-up area was within 2’000 cubits the statement is unnecessary since any built-up area is counted as 4 cubits.</i> it is as if he never left, for all those who leave to save may return to their place.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “If somebody left with permission,” etc. For otherwise what would we say? That he should be treated like somebody who left by compulsion and he should have only four cubits. Therefore it was necessary to say, “he has 2’000 cubits in each direction.” Rav Ḥuna says, only if four cubits each intersect with the domain of Tiberias and with the domain of Magdala<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The distance from the Sabbath border of Tiberias to that of Magdala was less than 2’000 cubits. Therefore if somebody from Tiberias was informed just inside the border of the Sabbath domain of Magdala that he was no longer needed, he might return 2’000 cubits in the direction of Tiberias and end up 4 cubits inside the Sabbath domain of that city. Then he would be as if he had never left; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.3.1">Note 8</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 44b" href="/Eruvin.44b">Babli 44b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rav Ada bar Aḥawa, Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. Even if he went to Magdala and returned he is as if he had not left<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Being at Magdala with permission lets him go back 2’000 cubits outside its Sabbath domain towards Tiberias. Since then he is inside the Sabbath domain of Tiberias it is as if he never left, even if he was informed only inside the town of Magdala itself.</i>. Rebbi Joshua the Southerner asked: So far if he left in error<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> only refers to the case that he genuinely believed that his services were urgently needed.</i>. Even if he left intentionally? Rebbi Phineas said, the Mishnah says so: “for all those who leave to save may return to their place.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The inclusion of “all” means that the motives of those coming to help cannot be investigated.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 45a" href="/Eruvin.45a">Babli 45a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 3:5-6" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).3.5-6">Tosephta 3:5–6</a>.</i> It was stated: “Gentiles who attack villages near the border to rob, even straw or even wood, one goes out against them armed and brings the weapon back to its place. If they attack interior villages one goes out only if it is a matter of life<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">If they come to kill or kidnap.</i>. In earlier times they stored the weapons in a house close to the wall. Once they were attacked by enemies; they were in a crush to get their weapons and they killed one another more than what the enemies killed. They instituted that everybody should take from his house.”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody who sat down on the road<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Friday evening at twilight; he did not realize that he was close to a village but decided to spend the Sabbath on the road.</i>; when he gets up, behold, he is close to a town<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Within the town’s Sabbath domain.</i>: since it was not his intention he may not enter, the words of Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">He may not go more than 2’000 cubits from the place where he started the Sabbath. Since he did not intend to be like a town dweller for this Sabbath, for him the entire built-up area is not counted as 4 cubits.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, he may enter. Rebbi Jehudah said, it happened that Rebbi Tarphon entered without intention<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since he started the Sabbath with the Sabbath domain, he may walk the entire domain allowed to the town dwellers.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “If somebody slept on the road,”<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear whether this is a misquote of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.4.1">Mishnah 4</a> (to which the discussion is referring) or a correct quote of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.5.1">Mishnah 5</a>.</i> etc. They said, was not Rebbi Tarphon’s house of study within 2’000 cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Then the reference to R. Tarphon would not support R. Jehudah.</i>?
