Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: Where do they disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">R. Aqiba and the Sages in the second part of the Mishnah.</i>? If they put their <i>eruv</i> in a public square. But if they put their <i>eruv</i> in houses even Rebbi Aqiba will agree<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">It is not very clear what this means. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 61b" href="/Eruvin.61b">Babli 61b</a> it is formulated: If somebody makes Sabbath in a ruined city (he lives there in one of the ruined houses) he walks through the entire city and outside 2’000 cubits, like a regular city dweller. But if he puts his <i>eruv</i> into a ruined city he only has 2’000 cubits from the place of his <i>eruv</i> (consistent with the thesis that he who makes an <i>eruv</i> loses all additions to the Sabbath domain. One has to assume that “depositing an <i>eruv</i> in a house” means making Sabbath in that house.)</i>. Rebbi Abba the son of Rebbi Pappai asked: If it was a city which made an <i>eruv</i> and he put his <i>eruv</i> in a public square, is it not as if they gave their <i>eruv</i> in houses<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">If the inhabitants made an <i>eruv</i> to carry in the entire walled city, it is obvious that the entire city can be counted only as 4 cubits in the domain even for a person not domiciled there.</i>?
A town which was destroyed<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">But the ruins and an outline of the wall are still standing.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar says, he walks its entirety and outside 2’000 cubits. Samuel says, he only has 2’000 cubits [from] the place of his <i>eruv</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Cahana, Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Rab: If somebody deposits his <i>eruv</i> in a sheep-pen or a sheep-corral he walks its entirety and outside it 2’000 cubits. Are a sheep-pen or a sheep-corral not as if houses and apartments were destroyed<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Sheep pens were never built for human dwelling, houses were even if now they are ruins. Since one makes Sabbath in a human dwelling, what holds for sheep pens certainly holds for ruined houses.</i>?
For a new city one measures from the houses; for an old one one measures from the wall<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 26a" href="/Eruvin.26a">Babli 26a</a>.</i>. What is new and what is old? Rebbi Zeˋira in the name of Rav Ḥasdai: If he built houses and afterwards built a wall, that is new; if he built a wall and then built the houses, that is old. Rebbi Zeˋira said, in both cases it is new, and what is old? Anything which had dwellings and they were destroyed. Rebbi Zeˋira follows his opinion, since they disagreed: If he built nine as fore-court and one for dwelling. Rebbi Eleazar said, even if he built the tenth as dwelling it is for dwelling<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">The text clearly is elliptic. It seems that it should read: If an enclosure was built by several people, for R. Zeˋira it counts as built for a human dwelling if all builders intend it as such; for R. Eleazar it counts as such if at least one builder had this intention.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Aqiba told them, do you not agree with me about one who puts his <i>eruv</i> at the mouth of a cave that he only has 2’000 cubits from the place of his <i>eruv</i>? They told him, when is this? If there are no dwellers in it, but if it is inhabited he walks through all of it<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">If the cave is inhabited it becomes a house and the entire cave is counted as 4 cubits.</i> and outside 2’000 cubits. It turns out that the interior is easier than its top<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody puts his <i>eruv</i> on top of a cave where nobody dwells, he only has 2’000 cubits from the place of his <i>eruv</i>.</i>. But for one who measures about whom they talked one gives 2’000 cubits even if his measure reaches its limit inside the cave<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">As noted before, by making an <i>eruv</i> one reduces his Sabbath domain to a circle of radius 2’000. If the <i>eruv</i> was deposited outside the cave he cannot walk inside the cave to a distance farther than 2’000 cubits from the <i>eruv</i> even though for the cave dwellers the entire cave is only counted as 4 cubits.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “Rebbi Aqiba told them, do you not agree with me,” etc. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Eleazar. The one who acquires rest<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">It is his place of residence for this Sabbath.</i> in a fore-court has it easier than one who puts his <i>eruv</i> in a fore-court. He who acquires rest in a fore-court walks in its entirety and outside of it 2’000 cubits. He who puts his <i>eruv</i> in a fore-court only has 2’000 cubits from the place of his <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Since anybody who makes an <i>eruv</i> loses all additions to the circular domain of radius 2’000 cubits.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira asked: If one said, rest should be acquired for me in the fore-court? Rebbi Ḥanania the son of Rebbi Hillel said, this is the disagreement of Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 4:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.4.8.2">Chapter 4, Note 83</a>.</i>. Rebbi Meïr is of the opinion, the main point of an <i>eruv</i> is by a loaf. If he puts his <i>eruv</i> in a fore-court he only has 2’000 cubits from the place of his <i>eruv</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said, the main point of an <i>eruv</i> is by his feet. If he said, rest should be acquired for me in the fore-court, he walks in its entirety and outside of it 2’000 cubits.
The roof of a tower is considered [part of] the town. The roof of a cave is considered as fields. What, really dwellers<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah 10. Do we require that a cave be inhabited or only that it should be habitable?</i>? Or maybe just inhabitable? Since Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman said in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: one considers it as if filled by water and clay<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">His definition of “uninhabited” is “unhabitable”; therefore for him “inhabited” means “habitable”.</i>, this means even inhabitable. Rebbi Isaac ben Rebbi Eleazar said, this implies (a city)<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that for עיר “city” one should read מערה “cave”.</i> which has two exits, if between one and the other there are 3’999 cubits one walks in its entirety and outside it 2’000 cubits, 4’000 cubits he only has 2’000 cubits from the place of his <i>eruv</i>. Rabbi Aḥa said; Rebbi Ze`ira explained it. If he would put his sandal anywhere he could go outside and put it on; and if he would put his sandal anywhere he could go outside and put it on. What is the difference from which side he comes? From one side it is forbidden, from both sides it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">A line segment can be the border of the completion of a town only if all of its points are accessible from the town on the Sabbath. Therefore if the radius of the semi-circle is &gt; 2’000, a point distant almost 2’000 from one side is not accessible from the other and the center is not accessible from anywhere. Therefore the chord cannot be the border of the completion; the Sabbath domain only is the set of all points of distance at most 2’000 from any point of the semi-circle. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 55a" href="/Eruvin.55a">Babli 55a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rav Ḥinena: If the roof of the cave was 4’000 cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Probably this again should read “4’000 - 1”.</i> he walks in its entirety and outside it 2’000 cubits, on top of a wall it is unlimited. If the roof of the cave was 6’000 cubits he walks in its entirety and outside it 2’000 cubits by the help of <i>nibariot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">Possibly “protuberances”, Arabic نبرة. If the cave below is considered a human dwelling, the roof above it can be made similar to the roof of a house by subdividing it into several roofs by natural outcroppings.</i>. What about tops of roofs? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Aeneas bar Pazi in the name of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Read: “Rav Jehudah”.</i> Jehudah, Rebbi Aḥa brings it in the name of Samuel. On top of the roofs one carries even a <i>kor</i>, even two <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning <i>bet</i> <i>kor</i>, a surface area of 30 <i>bet seah</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.10.2">Chapter 1, Note 288</a>).</i>. Rebbi Meïr said, one carries up to two <i>bet se’ah</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, the word of Rav disagrees with him<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">With both Samuel as quoted later, and R. Aḥa whose attribution is shown wrong.</i>, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.1.1">Mishnah 9:1</a>. Even though the houses below define different private domains, the roofs, although divided by walls, form a single domain on which one may carry from one house to the next</i>: “All roofs of a city form a single domain.” Samuel said, up to two <i>bet se’ah</i>. Rav said, one carries there even a <i>kor</i>, even two <i>kor</i>. It is the same for tops of roofs and tops of ships<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">If ships are tied together in a harbor, one carries from ship to ship as one carries from roof to roof.</i>.

Chapter 6



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If someone dwells with a Non-Jew in a courtyard or with one who rejects the notion of <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A member of a Sadducee sect.</i>, that one makes it forbidden for him<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For the anonymous Tanna even a single Gentile prevents the Jew from using the courtyard on the Sabbath. For R. Eliezer ben Jacob the Gentile becomes a problem (to be discussed in connection with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>) only if there are at least two Jewish dwellers in the courtyard.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says that he<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A Gentile is nonexistent for the rules of the Sabbath; he can neither permit nor prohibit.</i> never forbids unless there are two Jews who mutually forbid<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If two Jews (including Sadducees) dwell in houses opening to the same courtyard they may not carry in the courtyard unless there be an <i>eruv</i>. The problem of the Sadducee is treated in the next Mishnah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody dwells with a Non-Jew in a courtyard,” etc. “A Non-Jew’s courtyard is like a sheep-pen or a sheep-corral.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:19" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.19">Tosephta 5:19</a> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 5:7:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.5.7.5">Chapter 5, Note 156</a>).</i>” The rabbis objected to Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob: If a Jew and an animal<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The animal belongs to another (Jewish) owner. Since the courtyard contains at least one dwelling, it is not subject to the 5’000 square cubits rule. The rabbis consider the barn as a dwelling if the owner is not the owner of the house. R. Eliezer ben Jacob denies that a barn is a dwelling.</i> were living in a courtyard, would the animal not forbid? Just as an animal forbids so the Gentile forbids. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob objected to the rabbis: If a Jew and an animal were living in a courtyard, what would the animal forbid? Just as an animal does not forbid so the Gentile does not forbid.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Practice follows Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.3">Chapter 1, Note 62</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 46a" href="/Eruvin.46a">Babli 46a</a>. R. Joḥanan ben Nuri holds in contrast to the rabbis (representing R. Meïr) that a Sabbath domain can be acquired in sleep.</i>. Rebbi Hoshaia asked, for what is this needed? For what we have stated, “Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:20" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.20">Tosephta 5:20</a>: “Practice follows Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob.” Both in the case of R. Joḥanan ben Nuri and in that of R. Eliezer ben Jacob one follows the rule that “in matters of <i>Eruvin</i> one follows the lenient opinion.” The concurrence of the cases of R. Joḥanan ben Nuri of R. Eliezer ben Jacob establishes this principle in general.</i>”. But following the rabbis? The rabbis disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore the rule that one follows the lenient opinion must be accepted.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rabban Gamliel said, if happened that a Sadducee was dwelling with us in the alley in Jerusalem and my father told us be quick and bring all the vessels into the alley before he can bring out and forbid for you<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The argument behind the institution of <i>eruv haṣerot</i> is that a limited-access courtyard is not a public domain; it can be formally turned into a private domain by organizing a common meal of all dwellers in the courtyard. The <i>eruv</i>, a collection of food to which every family dwelling there has contributed, represents this common meal which does not have to be realized. If a rabbinic Jew does not want to be friendly with his neighbors, he can refrain from contributing to the <i>eruv</i> and instead cede his rights to carry in the courtyard to the members of the <i>eruv</i>. Then they will be able to carry there (as explained in the subsequent Mishnaiot) but he will be forbidden to do so.<br>We do know that Sadducees objected in principle to the institution of all kinds of <i>eruvin</i>, but the fragments of Sadducee <i>halakhah</i> available do not permit us to decide whether they considered a courtyard with a single exit to the street as private or public domain. It seems that different Sadducee sects had different opinions in this matter. All Sadducee sects did consider a dead-end alley as a street and public domain. If a Sadducee considers the courtyard as public domain he will not carry there; therefore he can be considered as ceding his rights to the rabbinic dwellers in the courtyard (Rashi on the Mishnah, 61b) and they can make the <i>eruv</i> among themselves; this argument certainly applies to alleys. If he considers the courtyard as private domain it will be impossible to make an <i>eruv</i>. If only one rabbinic family is living in the alley they can lay claim to the use of the alley by bringing a table and chairs from the house there before nightfall. If at sundown the alley was their dining space, anything the Sadducee will do later is irrelevant.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says it in another formulation, be quick and work in the alley before he can bring out and forbid for you<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">He does not require the set-up of pieces of furniture, only that the alley be working space at sundown.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “Rabban Gamliel said, if happened that a Sadducee,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe of the ms. wrote: או גרש בראשיה דפרקיה קדמייה דראשיה דמסכתא “One reads this at the start of the first Chapter at the start of the Tractate”. This was deleted by the corrector and replaced by the following text which is a slightly shortened copy from there, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.3">Chapter 1, Notes 58–65</a>.</i> Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥinena in the name of Cahana: Practice does not follow Rebbi Jehudah. If it were not so what would we say? Rebbi Jehudah versus the Sages, practice should follow Rebbi Jehudah? But since Rebbi Jacob bar Idi said in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, practice follows the lenient opinion in <i>Eruvin</i>, and following Rebbi Naḥman bar Isaac in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, practice follows Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri, and we asked about it, did not Rebbi Jacob bar Idi say in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, practice follows the lenient opinion in <i>Eruvin</i>, and did not Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman say in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, practice follows Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri? We asked about it, we would think to say that this refers to an individual versus an individual but not an individual versus the Sages. There came Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, practice follows Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri even though the Sages disagree with him. Why should practice not follow Rebbi Jehudah even though the Sages disagree with him?
Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rav: If a courtyard has two gates<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The courtyard opens into two alleys; the question is whether it is required to participate in both alleys.</i> and an Israel and a Gentile live there; for the Israel one goes after the usual, for the Gentile one goes after the unusual<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">If the Israel participates in the alley which he most frequently uses, he does not prohibit the use of the second alley if he does not participate there (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 49a" href="/Eruvin.49a">Babli 49a</a>). But a Gentile interferes with the Sabbath use of any alley to which his courtyard opens and where at least two Jews are residing; both alleys must rent or accept a cession of his space from him.</i>. If there were two Israel and the Gentile in between them, both for Israel and Gentile one goes after the usual<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If most of the dwellers of one part of the courtyard all use the same gate, one may assume that the minority in that part does the same.</i>. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">If the Gentile either cedes or rents out his right to the use of the courtyard to the dwellers in one alley, he automatically becomes part of the dwellers in the other alley and they also must rent from him or ask for a cession.</i> ceded the usual domain, the unusual becomes the usual. If he rented the usual domain, the unusual becomes the usual. If the unusual made an <i>eruv</i>, the usual one is not permitted.
A semi-convert<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">A non-Jew who publicly has renounced idolatry. He has no obligation to keep the Sabbath.</i> and a semi-converted slave<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">A slave who refuses to be circumcised but has renounced idolatry. He has no obligation to keep the Sabbath.</i> as well as an apostate acting in public<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">He violates the Sabbath in public. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:18" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.18">Tosephta 5:18</a>.</i> are like Gentiles in every respect<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">In all matters regarding the Sabbath. In other matters the apostate cannot escape being Jewish.</i>. There are Tannaïm who state<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:22" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.22">Tosephta 5:22</a>, more or less the second version.</i>: “A quaestor<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>quaestor</i>, an official of the government’s revenue service.</i> prohibits immediately and a troop of soldiers<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ξενία, ἡ, from ξενός in the sense of mercenary soldier.</i> after thirty days.” There are Tannaïm who state: “A quaestor forbids after thirty days but a troop of soldiers never ever forbids.” He who says, a quaestor prohibits immediately, if it is routine<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">If he comes regularly to audit the local accounts he is a resident, not a passer-by, and one has to lease from him his right to use courtyard or alley on the Sabbath. But soldiers may be called to duty at any moment; they become residents according to one opinion if they stay for 30 days, in the other opinion never.</i>, and a troop of soldiers after thirty days, if it is not routine. But he who says a quaestor forbids after thirty days, if he enters by permission<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">A government commission specifying his place of activity. This excludes an official charged to control the local tax office by making unannounced and unpredictable audits.</i>, but a troop of soldiers never ever forbids, if they enter without permission<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">On the authority only of a local commander.</i>.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, they<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The first tradent in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> (who is R. Meïr), about the position of a Sadducee in matters of <i>eruv</i>.</i> disagree about ceding rights. Rebbi Meïr says, he may cede rights, but the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah in the Mishnah.</i> are saying, he may not cede rights. Rebbi Meïr says, he may cede rights, and you are saying, “be quick”<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Why should any action be needed when the Sadducee may cede his rights and resolve the problem by some words?</i>? Even though Rebbi Meïr says, he may cede rights, he agrees that they may lay claim to the alley beforehand<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">While the Sadducee may cede his rights, he is not obligated to do so and accommodate rabbinic Jews. Therefore preventive action may be preferable since R. Meïr asserts that the action of using the alley bars the Sadducee from claiming his rights to it.</i>. It was stated thus: Because he may cede rights like a Jew, the words of Rebbi Meïr. “Whether in error or intentionally, he forbids.” So is the <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">While the text is identical with a sentence from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>, one refers to a <i>baraita</i> whose subject is a Sadducee. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 69a" href="/Eruvin.69a">Babli 69a</a>.</i>: he does not forbid. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥinena: Everybody agrees that he<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The Sadducee. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 68b" href="/Eruvin.68b">Babli 68b</a>.</i> may cede rights. Where do they disagree? To change his mind. Rebbi Meïr says he may cede his rights and retract it, but the rabbis say, he may cede his rights and cannot retract it. Rebbi Meïr says he may cede his rights and retract it, and you are saying, “be quick”? Even though Rebbi Meïr says, he may cede rights, he agrees that they may lay claim to the alley beforehand. It was stated thus: Because he may cede rights like a Jew, the words of Rebbi Meïr. “Whether in error or intentionally, he forbids.” So is the <i>baraita</i>: he does not forbid.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> In a courtyard where one dweller forgot to make an <i>eruv</i>, his house is forbidden for him and them to bring anything in or take out<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">If he does not participate in the <i>eruv</i>, the courtyard has the status of (rabbinic) public domain for his house. This status is of the house, not the person; it applies to everybody.</i>, but theirs are permitted for them and him<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>eruv</i> makes the courtyard the common domain of the remaining houses; this status also is of the houses, not the persons; it applies to everybody.</i>. If they ceded their rights to him, he is permitted but they are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In this case there is no <i>eruv</i>.</i>. If there were two they forbid one another since a single individual cedes rights and accepts rights but two may cede rights but cannot accept rights<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">While they may not accept ceded rights from a Jew they may buy the rights to carry in the courtyard from a Gentile dwelling there. Once the Gentile has accepted payment he cannot retract his cessation even if he should use courtyard or alley for which he received the money.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i>. “In a courtyard where one dweller forgot to make an <i>eruv</i>,” etc. “But theirs are permitted for them and him<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>eruv</i> makes the courtyard the common domain of the remaining houses; this status also is of the houses, not the persons; it applies to everybody.</i>.” Because he ceded his rights. May this be repeated cyclically<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">If <i>n</i> people dwell in a courtyard and they made no <i>eruv</i>, may <i>n</i>-1 of them temporarily cede their rights to one to use the courtyard on the Sabbath, until each of them had his possibility of use, and then start the procedure anew? <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 69b" href="/Eruvin.69b">Babli 69b</a>.</i>? The students of Rav in the name of Rav: it may be repeated cyclically. Samuel said, it may not be repeated cyclically. The Mishnah disagrees with Samuel: “theirs are permitted for them and him<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">How could it be permitted to him if not that the others had ceded their rights to him?</i>.” Because he ceded his rights<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The preceding argument is no good. Since he ceded his rights he is like a visitor to the courtyard whose presence is irrelevant.</i>. But did we not state: “If they ceded their rights to him, he is permitted but they are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In the first sentence of the Mishnah, one ceded to the many. In the second sentence, the many ceded to one. This seems to prove that one may change the arrangements on the Sabbath.</i>”? He explains it that the Mishnah treats different cases<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah describes different situations occurring on different Sabbaths.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:17" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.17">Tosephta 5:17</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 70a" href="/Eruvin.70a">Babli 70a</a>.</i>: “One who did not make an <i>eruv</i> cedes his rights to two who did make an <i>eruv</i> but two who made an <i>eruv</i> do not<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.3.3">Note 60</a>) “do cede”. This text seems to be required.</i> cede to one who did not make an <i>eruv</i>. And two who made an <i>eruv</i> may not cede to two who made no <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since two who made no <i>eruv</i> prohibit the use of the courtyard to one another, cession of rights by other inhabitants is ineffective.</i>. Also two who made no <i>eruv</i> may not cede to two who made no <i>eruv</i>. Everybody may cede his rights and accept rights except two who made no <i>eruv</i> who may cede rights but may not receive<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since two who made no <i>eruv</i> prohibit the use of the courtyard to one another, cession of rights by other inhabitants is ineffective.</i>.”
Rav Ḥasdai<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In Raviah (vol. 1, p. 462, n. 20) “Rav Ḥisda”; but in R. Ḥananel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 71a" href="/Eruvin.71a"><i>ad</i> 71a</a>) as in our text “Rav Ḥasdai.”</i> said, each single one of ten Israel who dwell in one house must cede his rights<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Or make an <i>eruv</i>. “Dwellers” are permanent residents. Similarly, to make an <i>eruv</i> in the staircase of a multi-family house one must rent the use of the space from all Gentile dwellers in the house.</i>. Rebbi Yasa said, each single one of ten Gentiles who dwell in one house must rent out his rights. Rebbi Abba said, it happened that a Persian’s wife rented out her courtyard without informing her husband. The case came before Rebbi Samuel and he declared it legal<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since the husband has the usufruct of his wife’s property during marriage, a Jewish court could not accept the validity of a rental agreement of real estate concluded without the husband’s knowledge. But since renting the use of the space for the Sabbath is a pure formality, it is accepted from wife, servant, or farm-hand. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 80a" href="/Eruvin.80a">Babli 80a</a>, bottom.</i>. They wanted to say, even his servant and farm-hand. Practice: An Israel cedes and the Gentile rents out<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥananel (<i>ad</i> 71a) has a more complete text: [R. Yasa in the name of R. Joḥanan: If an Israel and a Gentile live in one house,] practice is that … The same text is in <i>Šibbole Halleqet</i> (ed. Buber, p. 70) and is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Eruvin 66a:2:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Eruvin.66a.2.1">Tosaphot 66a <i>s. v</i>. מערב</a>, paraphrased by Ritba (ed. Warsaw, <i>ad</i> 66a, p. 109) “an Israel and a Gentile who live in a house of a courtyard, the Jew had to make an <i>eruv</i> and from the Gentile one has to rent”; similarly <i>Sefer Miswot Gadol</i> (ed. Venice p. 244a 1.9).</i>. Then why should the Gentile not cede? He might change his mind. Then even if he rented out he might change his mind! From then on he would use it in robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">From the moment he accepted money, what he does is irrelevant.</i>.
How little? Should it come as what Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Mana bar Tanḥum, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: real estate cannot be acquired with less than a <i>peruṭa</i>’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3 Note 337, repeated there 1:5 p. 127, <i>Ma</i>`<i>aser šeni</i> 4:3 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.3.3">Note 60</a>). In <i>Qiddušin</i> the name tradition is: R. Yasa in the name of R. Mana, R. Tanḥum, R. Abbahu in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>? The word of Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa implies even a walnut, even a date<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since the lease is a formality, not a reality, the qualifications of real estate law do not apply.</i>.
When Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa went to a hostelry<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ξενία, ἡ.</i>, when it was possible for him to put it in order, he did it<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">If there were other Jews, he tried to arrange an <i>eruv</i>.</i>. Otherwise he would spread out his clothing. He would put his staff at one place, his sandals at another, his wallet at another<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δισάκκιον, τό. He spread his things out in all rooms to that he could carry on the Sabbath in the entire building.</i>. Rabbi Mattaniah said, this you are saying if the hostel keeper<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Greek adjective, πάνδοκος, -ον, “of a hostelry”.</i> was a Gentile. But if the hostel keeper was an Israel, he is not suspected to carry<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The hostel keeper is not suspected to carry in the rooms he prepared for the guests. If there are no other guests there is no need to disperse his things.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> When may one start giving away rights? The House of Shammai say, as long as it is daylight<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that this cessation is a legal act and therefore prohibited on the Sabbath; the House of Hillel hold that it is an agreement among friends and a purely negative statement, a decision not to exercise one’s rights, and as such permitted on the Sabbath.</i>, but the House of Hillel say, when it gets dark. If somebody gave away his rights and then brought out, whether in error or intentionally, he forbids, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah says, intentionally he forbids, in error he does not forbid<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Both R. Meïr and R. Jehudah consider the case that the person ceding his rights uses the courtyard before the others could use it. If the use was intentional it proves that the cessation was retracted; if it was in error the cessation still is valid. R. Meïr agrees in principle but he treats unintentional infraction as an intentional one as a precaution, a “fence around the Law”.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “When may one start giving away rights? The House of Shammai say,” etc. “When may one start giving away rights?” Is the Mishnah so: “When may one start ceding rights? The House of Shammai say as long as it is daylight<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that this cessation is a legal act and therefore prohibited on the Sabbath; the House of Hillel hold that it is an agreement among friends and a purely negative statement, a decision not to exercise one’s rights, and as such permitted on the Sabbath.</i>, but the House of Hillel say, when it gets dark”? So is the Mishnah: The House of (Shammai) [Hillel]<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is the scribe’s, the one in brackets the corrector’s correction.<br>The scribe’s text is easily explained: If the Mishnah were permissive, it should be formulated as “until when may one cede.” Since it says “when may one start” it is prescriptive, that cession of rights before this time is invalid. Since the Mishnah is not on the list of those where the House of Shammai is more lenient than the House of Hillel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 4" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.4"><i>Idiut</i> 4</a>), one has to emend the Mishnah and switch the places of “Shammai” and “Hillel”. But the next paragraph shows that this emendation is impossible; one has to accept the corrector’s emendation and read the Mishnah as: but the House of Hillel say, <i>even</i> when it gets dark.</i> say, when it gets dark; because otherwise it would be of the leniencies of the House of Shammai and the strictures of the House of Hillel.
“If somebody gave away his rights and then brought out.” So is the Mishnah: If somebody ceded his rights, “whether in error or intentionally, he forbids.” So is the Mishnah: He does not forbid<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meïr is consistent that a Gentile living in a courtyard forbids the use of the courtyard on the Sabbath as long as one does not lease the use of the courtyard from him and he abides by the terms of the lease at least until sundown. But following R. Eliezer ben Jacob, who represents practice to be followed, one has to correct the Mishnah to: He does not forbid.</i>.
It was stated: “If two people were partners in a courtyard and one of them died, and the house fell as inheritance to an outside person. If before it became dark, this makes it forbidden; after dark it is not forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:13" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.13">Tosephta 5:13</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 70b" href="/Eruvin.70b">Babli 70b</a>. The Tanna of the Mishnah holds that the heir as owner makes the courtyard forbidden without an <i>eruv</i> even if he does not live there, but if the courtyard was permitted at sunbdown it remains permitted for the entire Sabbath even if the situation changes.</i>.” Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥinena: This is the House of Shammai’s, for the House of Shammai say, one may not cede rights after dark, but the House of Hillel say, one may cede rights after dark<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:13" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.13">Tosephta 5:13</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 70b" href="/Eruvin.70b">Babli 70b</a>. The Tanna of the Mishnah holds that the heir as owner makes the courtyard forbidden without an <i>eruv</i> even if he does not live there, but if the courtyard was permitted at sunbdown it remains permitted for the entire Sabbath even if the situation changes.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, since he dies there is no greater cession of rights than this<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Since he does no longer live there. The heir becomes a neighbor only by actually living there.</i>. “If an outside person owned a house in the courtyard with them when he died and the house fell as inheritance to a dweller in the courtyard. If before it became dark, this makes it forbidden; after dark it is not forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:14" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.14">Tosephta 5:14</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 70b" href="/Eruvin.70b">Babli 70b</a>.</i>” Still this is the House of Shammai’s, for the House of Shammai say, one may not cede rights after dark, but the House of Hillel say, one may cede rights after dark. May one cede rights<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Since practice follows the House of Hillel it is obvious that one may cede rights after dark. The problem really is, “may one lease rights after sundown”? If the lease is a transaction, it is forbidden, if a formality, it is permitted.</i> after dark? Rebbi Ḥiyya, Rebbi Yasa, Rebbi Immi went up to Ḥammat Gader<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 65b" href="/Eruvin.65b">Babli 65b</a> the story is told of R. (Ḥanina) [Ḥama] bar Joseph, R. Ḥiyya, and R. Yasa (Assi) and it is clear that the owner of the hostelry was a Gentile; the question is about leasing, not ceding. The Gentile came only during the Sabbath.</i>. They asked Rebbi Ḥama bar Joseph and he permitted. Rebbi Joḥanan heard them and said, you did the correct thing. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish heard it and said, you acted incorrectly. Do they disagree? Rebbi Zeˋira said, they did not disagree. He who said, you did the correct thing, that you leased. But he who said, you acted incorrectly, that you moved<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">They should not have moved their things in the hostelry before they leased the space since the appearance of the owner always was a potentiality.</i>. Rebbi Abba said, they disagree. He who said, you did the correct thing, that you leased and moved. But he who said, you acted incorrectly, that you leased and that you moved<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">He treats the lease as a transaction.</i>.
Rebbi Abba asked, is it the same in the matter of partitions<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Which make it possible to carry at the place of rest of a caravan, as explained in Chapter 1.</i>? How is this? If there were two and they established one <i>eruv</i>, and a Gentile came and added; the Sabbath already had started in prohibition. If there were three but they established two <i>eruvim</i> and a Gentile came and opened; the Sabbath already had started in permission<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:10:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.10.3">Chapter 1, Note 294</a>.</i>. The strength of Rebbi Abba is from the following: Any Sabbath which started in permission is permitted, in prohibition is prohibited<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 70b" href="/Eruvin.70b">Babli 70b</a>.</i>, except one who cedes rights; and he<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ze`ira and all authorities who permit leasing from a Gentile on the Sabbath.</i> would say, except one who cedes or leases. May one lease rights from the owner of a hostelry? Rebbi Ḥinena and Rebbi Jonathan went to Ḥammat Gader<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The place chosen for proclamations about matters of the calendar.</i>. They said, we shall wait until the Elders of the South arrive here. There came Rebbi (Peres) {Nathan]<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">In the quotes of this paragraph in the Responsa of Rashba (part 1 # 626; Responsa attributed to Naḥmanides #218) he is quoted as R. Efes, who is known as Rebbi’s scribe.</i> the Southerner; they asked him and he permitted. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish heard it and said, since the Gentile may remove us<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">If he finds better paying hotel guests. He denies the validity of leasing rights in a Gentile hostelry as a matter of principle.</i>, we did not do anything. Simeon bar Abba said, Rebbi Joḥanan asked, then our houses are not ours<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Since at any time the army may send soldiers to be quartered with us.</i>? Rebbi Justus ben Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Boethos: Our houses are not ours, to dwell with us<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The army may cause inconvenience but the troops cannot remove us from our houses; one may accept R. Simeon ben Laqish’s point of view and still practice leasing in courtyards or alleys.</i>; but they cannot force us to leave; but in a hostelry they remove us.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the owner of a house participated with several neighbors with wine, they do not need to make an <i>eruv</i>. If with wine with one but oil with the other they need to make an <i>eruv</i>. Rebbi Simeon says neither of them has to make an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If all the people living around a courtyard or in an alley are in business together and own wine stored at one place in their courtyard or alley they do not need an <i>eruv</i> since some of the wine might be used as common food. R. Simeon extends this to several kinds of food belonging to several people as long as the foods are sometimes used together as food</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “If the owner of a house participated with several neighbors,” etc. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Read: Rav.</i> Jehudah: They stated this in commercial practice<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The language of the Mishnah is inconsistent. “Participation” refers to making an <i>eruv</i> in an alley, “neighbors” refer to dwellers in a courtyard. The Mishnah does not refer explicitly to either one; it uses the expression “participate” to describe partnership.</i>. Everybody with wine, since he does not care about mixing<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Since everything is in a large vessel and the relative shares of the partners have not been separated.</i>, it does not need an <i>eruv</i>. If with wine with one but oil with the other, since he cares about mixing, it needs an <i>eruv</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Cahana, Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Rav: The Mishnah refers to the case that all is in one vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 71a" href="/Eruvin.71a">Babli 71a</a>. Since it is not intentionally collected as <i>eruv</i> but is used as such because it happened to be available, it must be in one vessel.</i>. Rebbi Zeriqan said, the reason of Rebbi Simeon is that they are used to drink οἰνόγαρον<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">“Wine-fish sauce”.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:9" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.9">Tosephta 5:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 71b" href="/Eruvin.71b">Babli 71b</a>.</i>: Rebbi Eleazar ben Thaddeus says, in every case they are forbidden until they make an <i>eruv</i>. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Eleazar ben Thaddeus. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name Rebbi Ze`ira: Rebbi Eleazar ben Thaddeus follows Rebbi Meïr. Which [statement of] Rebbi Meïr? Rebbi Mana said, Rebbi Meïr who said, by an <i>eruv</i>, by participation<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 6:6" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).6.6">Tosephta 6:6</a>. He requires separate participation in an alley and <i>eruv</i> for courtyards in all cases.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Meïr who said that one may not make an <i>eruv</i> for a person without his knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:1:2-10:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.1.2-10.4">Mishnah 7:11</a>.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Mana, following Rebbi Ze`ira, in the opinion of Rav following Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">R. Mana interprets the statement of R. Ze`ira to imply that Rav accepts the position of R. Meïr.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: In matters of <i>eruvin</i> and public fast-days everybody follows Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name Rebbi Joḥanan: Even about the reading of the Esther scroll everybody follows Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>eruvin</i> one follows R. Meïr that <i>eruv</i> for courtyards and participation for alleys must be separate acts, later in the Chapter, 23d l. 47; then <i>Ta</i>`<i>aniot</i> 67b 4:1 l. 57 (that on a public fast day the priestly blessing is given three times), <i>Megillah</i> 2:1 73b l. 26 (that the scroll has to be read from beginning to end). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 72a" href="/Eruvin.72a">Babli 72a</a>. These rules are popular usage, not rabbinic decrees.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Five groups who rested in one big hall<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>triclinium</i>.</i> during the Sabbath, the House of Shammai say, a separate <i>eruv</i> for each of them, but the House of Hillel say, one <i>eruv</i> for all of them; however they agree that if some of them stayed for the Sabbath in separate rooms or on a second floor that each group needs a separate <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The people in question are all renters. If they have different units as bedrooms the relationship of the hall to the bedrooms is that of a courtyard to houses and the different groups must contribute to the <i>eruv</i>. A group is composed by people eating together; since an <i>eruv</i> is represented by a symbolic meal, an actual meal together obviates the need for an <i>eruv</i>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “Five groups who rested in one big hall,” etc. The big hall for them is like a courtyard for houses<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">For the House of Shammai (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.6.1">Mishnah 6</a>) even in one big hall an <i>eruv</i> is needed if people do not eat together.</i>. If there were tents<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>papilio</i>, -<i>onis</i>, <i>m</i>. It seems that the reference is to curtains providing privacy to the different groups.</i> there, it is as if they were houses. Where do we hold? If the tents reach to the ceiling, everybody agrees that every group has to contribute to the <i>eruv</i>. If the tents do not reach to the ceiling, everybody agrees that one <i>eruv</i> is valid for all of them<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">If the hall is open to a courtyard, any contribution to the courtyard’s <i>eruv</i> is to the benefit of all the dwellers there.</i>. But we must hold that the tents were ten [hand-breadths] high<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">They are legal separations for the House of Shammai but do not provide privacy for the House of Hillel.</i>. They equally agree that if they join in dough and dishes that one <i>eruv</i> is valid for all of them<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Since an <i>eruv</i> is a symbolic common meal, all participants in an actual meal are counted as one for the <i>eruv</i>.</i>. Rebbi Ashian asked, then<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">If a common meal unites all dwellers in a house, the fact that they sleep in different rooms should not have any influence on the <i>eruv</i>. The Babli disagrees with the entire discussion, 72a.</i> even if part of them dwell in separate rooms or on upper floors?
Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: Two houses, one inside the other<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">“House” usually means “one-room apartment.” The inner apartment has no direct exit to the courtyard; its dwellers must leave through the outher apartment.</i>. If the inner one made an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">For the courtyard reachable through the outer apartment.</i>, the outer one does not have to make an <i>eruv</i>. If the outer one made an <i>eruv</i>, the inner one must contribute to the <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Since the contribution of the dweller in the outer apartment does not say anything about the inner one whereas the outer apartment is necessary for the inner one as a porter’s lodge.</i>. Rebbi Phineas said, but in Egypt it was the other way. If he gave on the inner, the outer has to give. If he gave on the outer, the inner does not have to give<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Assuming that at the Exodus there was an inner apartment reacheable only through the outer one, smearing blood of the Passover sacrifice on the outer door-post (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:7" href="/Exodus.12.7"><i>Ex</i>. 12:7</a>) also protected the inner one while blood on the inner door-posts did not protect the outer ones [S. Liebermann, <i>Tarbiz</i> 6 (1935) p. 235].</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Even if the inner one did not make an <i>eruv</i>, the outer one does not have to make an <i>eruv</i>. Why? It is treated as a porter’s lodge<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">This is not treated as a separate house in the courtyard if nobody lives there; one does not need to include it in the <i>eruv</i> and one may not deposit the <i>eruv</i> in it. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 76a" href="/Eruvin.76a">Babli 76a</a>.</i>. His statement implies that there is no porter’s lodge for a single dweller. Rav’s statement implies that there is a porter’s lodge for a single dweller, for Rebbi Abba bar<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Read: Rav. Rebbi Abba, Rav Jehudah in the name of Rav.</i> Jehudah said in the name of Rav: This refers to a porter’s lodge of a single dweller. But for a porter’s lodge of multiple dwellers it is an <i>eruv</i>, and one who dwells there forbids him<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 8, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 25a" href="/Eruvin.25a">25a</a> l. 41; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 75b" href="/Eruvin.75b">Babli 75b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: If there are two people, one is entitled, the other one is not entitled<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">A squatter.</i>. If the one entitled made an <i>eruv</i>, the one not entitled is permitted. If the one not entitled made an <i>eruv</i>, the one entitled is not permitted.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Brothers sharing property who were eating at their father’s table<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">And all food is provided by the father.</i> but sleeping in their own houses<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In the same courtyard.</i> need an <i>eruv</i> for each of them. Therefore if one of them forgot and did not make an <i>eruv</i> he is deemed to have ceded his rights<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Automatically.</i>. When? If they all bring their <i>eruvin</i> to one place<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">In most Mishnah mss: למקום אחר “to another place” which is not their father’s house.</i> but if the <i>eruv</i> came to them, or there were no other dwellers with them<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>eruv</i> is at the father’s house, the father’s food counts for the entire family. Similarly a family compound does not need an <i>eruv</i>.</i>, they do not need an <i>eruv</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “Brothers sharing property who were eating at their father’s table,” etc. Where is the main dwelling<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Which determines the obligation for <i>eruv</i>.</i>? Rebbi Jonah said, Rav and Samuel disagreed. One said, at the place of their bread, but the other said, at the place of sleeping. We did not know who said what but since Samuel said, higher than ten [hand-breadths] is rabbinically forbidden, he must say, at the place of their bread<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">S. Liebermann writes: I am at a loss to explain this.<br>As explained in <i>Šabbat</i> 1, Note 109, public domain extends only to 10 hand-breadths above the ground and any transport at this height is at most rabbinically forbidden on the Sabbath. This does not apply to private domain, such as a space 4 by 4 hand-breadths in a tree (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.3.1">Mishnah 3:3</a>). One might conjecture that Samuel recognizes the possibility of placing his <i>eruv</i> in the tree so that he cannot stay there on the Sabbath but could eat the <i>eruv</i> standing up. The <i>eruv</i> is invalid for him who states that the main place is where one sleeps but valid for him who insists on the place of the food.</i>. The Mishnah disagrees with Samuel, “Brothers who were eating”<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If they all eat at the same place, why should they have individually to contribute to the <i>eruv</i>?</i>. Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Rav: The Mishnah is if they are sustained by their father<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">If they do not pay for their food, the only person relevant at that place is the father. This is emphasized by R. Abba who insists that the sons have absolutely no say over the food. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 72b" href="/Eruvin.72b">Babli 72b</a> statement by Rav.</i>. In any case, are they not partners in what they are eating? Rebbi Abba said, Rebbi Samuel explained to me that their father gives them property rights only for what they are eating. Then even if the <i>eruv</i> does not come to them? They treated it as a house where they deposited the <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the next paragraph where it is discussed whether the dweller in the house in which the <i>eruv</i> is deposited has to contribute or not.</i>. Samuel said, he and his sons and the people of his house<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">His wife.</i> make an <i>eruv</i> with one loaf. Does he disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Contradict himself?</i>? There he says, at the place of their bread, and here he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The statement that the loaf contributed by the head of the household is valid for all members of the household seems to imply that this is true even if they are eating somewhere else. This contradicts the statement that the place of the meal is decisive.</i>? He explains it if two persons were partners in it<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">It may be a loaf with multiple ownership so that everybody contributes something.</i>. This is similar to what Rebbi Aḥawa bar Rebbi Ze`ira said, one may make an <i>eruv</i> with half a loaf. But we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.10.1">Mishnah 7:10</a>, where R. Joshua, whom practice follows, requires an undivided loaf for any <i>eruv</i>.</i>: “Even if it was baked from a <i>se’ah</i> and was sliced one may not use it for <i>eruv</i>”, and you are saying so? He explains it if two persons were partners in it<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">It may be a loaf with multiple ownership so that everybody contributes something.</i>.
A house where they deposited the <i>eruv</i>. The mother of Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa had died<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Since the mourner is forbidden to study Torah, he could not be asked about the status of the house. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 6:9-11:32" href="/Genesis.6.9-11.32"><i>Tanḥuma Noaḥ</i> 16</a>, R. Jacob bar Aḥa asked R. Abbahu who emphasized that practice follows the House of Hillel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 6:9-11:32" href="/Genesis.6.9-11.32"><i>Tanḥuma Buber Noah</i> 22</a>).</i> when Rebbi Yasa said to Rebbi Abbahu, state it, and he stated: The House of Shammai forbid and the House of Hillel permit<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">If the dweller in the house does not contribute to the <i>eruv</i>, the House of Shammai declare the <i>eruv</i> invalid, the House of Hillel declare it valid (but maybe not desirable). The opinion of Rav Hamnuna is quoted as that of the House of Hillel in <i>Tanḥuma Noaḥ</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:11" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.11">Tosephta 5:11</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 49b" href="/Eruvin.49b">Babli 49b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Hamnuna said, the house in which the <i>eruv</i> is deposited does not need anything. Rebbi Ḥasdai said, he is made like a partner<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">He joins with the other dwellers in the courtyard. Therefore if he has a loaf of bread in his house it is not required to be together with the <i>eruv</i> but if he has no bread he is a person who did not contribute to the <i>eruv</i>.</i>. Rebbi Hamnuna told him, the words of the rabbis do not let you, as they disagreed<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">This is from Chapter 2, Notes 52,53.</i>: An alley in which on one side dwelt a Gentile and on the other a Jew, Rav Ḥuna in the name of Rav: even if they made an <i>eruv</i> through doors the Gentile prohibits it for them. Abba bar Ḥuna<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">In Chapter 2 correctly: Abba bar bar Ḥana.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, even if they made the <i>eruv</i> through windows the Gentile does not prohibit for them. If you are saying that he is like a partner, he should forbid<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">If there are no exceptions to the duty to contribute the presence of a Gentile always should be an obstacle unless permission is bought from him.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If the people of the alley deposited the <i>eruv</i> in two places, if because the space was not enough, it is permitted, if in order to split the <i>eruv</i> it is forbidden.
Rebbi Yose made light of a person who said, this is a large ring<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">He speaks of the <i>eruv</i> in vulgar terms.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun recited the benediction of Sabbath nights over it because it was used for a commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>eruv</i> is food it may (or should) be used after sundown, preferably for the bread used for <i>Qiddush</i>, the sanctification of the Sabbath, since already it is dedicated to religious purposes. Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 117b.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.9.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭtin</i> 5:9</a>, Halakhah 5:9 Note 231. If the <i>eruv</i> usually is deposited in one specific house in the courtyard it may be removed to another location if the old inhabitant is no longer there.</i> There, we did state: “One puts an <i>eruv</i> in an old house, for communal peace”. Rebbi Abun said, the Mishnah speaks of a prior dweller.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Five courtyards were open to one another and open to an alley. If they made <i>eruvin</i> in the courtyards but did not participate in the alley, they are permitted in the courtyards and forbidden the alley, but if they participated in the alley the are permitted both here and there<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Participation in an alley makes separate <i>eruvin</i> in the courtyards unnecessary; defects in these <i>eruvin</i> become irrelevant. Participation in the alley is by courtyards, not by individual houses.</i>. If they made <i>eruvin</i> for the courtyards and participated in the alley and one of the dwellers in a courtyard forgot and did not make an <i>eruv</i> all parties are permitted; if he dwelt in the alley and did not participate, they are permitted in the courtyards but forbidden in the alley since the relationship of alley to courtyards is like the one of courtyards to houses<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Also the <i>eruv</i> of a courtyard obviates the necessity for separate <i>eruvin</i> in multi-family buildings in that courtyard, but <i>eruvin</i> in multi-family buildings in a courtyard do not take the place of an <i>eruv</i> for the courtyard.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “If five courtyards were open to one another,” etc. “But forbidden in the alley since the relationship of alley to courtyards is like the one of courtyards to houses<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Also the <i>eruv</i> of a courtyard obviates the necessity for separate <i>eruvin</i> in multi-family buildings in that courtyard, but <i>eruvin</i> in multi-family buildings in a courtyard do not take the place of an <i>eruv</i> for the courtyard.</i>”. But if they were open to one another but not open to the alley they would be forbidden even if they made an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">If the inner courtyards can reach the alley only through the outermost courtyard and each courtyard made an <i>eruv</i> for itself, those courtyards where others have a right of access remain prohibited as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.9.1">Mishnah 9</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yasa said, this is Rebbi Aqiba’s, since Rebbi Aqiba said, a right of access prohibits<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.9.1">Mishnah 9</a>. But for the rabbis a right of access is not a right of transport and separate <i>eruvin</i> are possible.</i>.
“If the courtyards made <i>eruvin</i> but did not participate in the alley.” The Mishnah is Rebbi Meïr’s since Rebbi Meïr said, by an <i>eruv</i> and by participation<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 6:6" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).6.6">Tosephta 6:6</a>. He requires separate participation in an alley and <i>eruv</i> for courtyards in all cases.</i>. Everybody follows Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Even about the reading of the Esther scroll everybody follows Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>eruvin</i> one follows R. Meïr that <i>eruv</i> for courtyards and participation for alleys must be separate acts, later in the Chapter, 23d l. 47; then <i>Ta</i>`<i>aniot</i> 67b 4:1 l. 57 (that on a public fast day the priestly blessing is given three times), <i>Megillah</i> 2:1 73b l. 26 (that the scroll has to be read from beginning to end). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 72a" href="/Eruvin.72a">Babli 72a</a>. These rules are popular usage, not rabbinic decrees.</i>. In matters of <i>eruvin</i> and public fast-days everybody follows Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name Rebbi Joḥanan and in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Even though Rebbi Meïr says that he may cede rights he agrees that if one of them forgot and did not contribute to the <i>eruv</i>, that he may rely on his participation<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Even though R. Meïr requires separate <i>eruvin</i> for courtyards and participation for alleys in all situations, this is only a precautionary measure as explained later in the paragraph; in an emergency, such as a defective or incomplete <i>eruv,</i> he will agree that in principle the participation in the alley permits carrying in the courtyards.</i>. Who holds “by an <i>eruv</i> and by participation,” not Rebbi Meïr? Since Rebbi Meïr said, by an <i>eruv</i> and by participation. <sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the text here is corrupt. The argument is that R. Meïr requires both <i>eruv</i> and participation because otherwise there would be the danger that people (in the formulation of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 73b" href="/Eruvin.73b">Babli 73b</a>, the children) would forget the principles of <i>eruv</i> and participation as explained by RR. Ila and Aḥa.</i> He told him, why do you make us forget the practices of <i>eruvin</i>? Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Ila; it should be like one of them. For one of them said, maybe he comes to a courtyard which does not need participation. Which is a courtyard which does not need participation? That is the customary courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">The courtyard which always is used to deposit the participation. Since the house where the <i>eruv</i> is deposited in a courtyard does not have to contribute, so the courtyard where the participation is deposited does not have to contribute by the rule formulated at the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.8.1">Mishnah 8</a>.</i>. He sees them preparing an <i>eruv</i> but not participating. Then he will infer to say that maybe an <i>eruv</i> is valid without participation? But no <i>eruv</i> is valid without participation<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">This is proposed as R. Meïr’s argument which only is alluded to in the tannaitic sources here, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 71b" href="/Eruvin.71b">Babli 71b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 6:6" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).6.6">Tosephta 6:6</a>. It is obvious that this applies only if there is no participation at all, not if one person forgot his contribution. This justifies the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.8.3">Note 138</a>.</i>. And the other one will say, maybe he comes to a courtyard which already participated. He sees them preparing an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Clearly this must read “neither making an <i>eruv</i> nor participating” since the Mishnah does not require <i>eruv</i> if there is participation in the alley.</i> but not participating. Then he will neither make an <i>eruv</i> nor participate<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">In this situation there is no support for the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.8.3">Note 138</a>; therefore the text stated that this argument applies only “according to one of them”.</i>.
It is obvious that “one makes <i>eruv</i> for courtyards and participates in alleys. If one makes <i>eruv</i> for courtyards, if they want to participate in the alley they may not participate<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">An <i>eruv</i> must be deposited in a house in the courtyard; participation can be deposited anywhere in the alley. An <i>eruv</i> must be made with a whole loaf of bread; the rules for participation are much more flexible. It is obvious that an <i>eruv</i> cannot simultaneously be used as participation since it cannot be at two places at the same time. On the other hand, participation makes an <i>eruv</i> unnecessary for everybody except R. Meïr. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 71b" href="/Eruvin.71b">Babli 71b</a>.</i>. But if they participated in an alley, if they want to make it <i>eruv</i> they may do so<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">To treat the participation also as <i>eruv</i> if it is a valid <i>eruv</i> and deposited in that particular courtyard.</i>.” And it was stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 6:6" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).6.6">Tosephta 6:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 71b" href="/Eruvin.71b">Babli 71b</a>.</i> “One makes <i>eruv</i> and participates simultaneously, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, one makes <i>eruv</i> for courtyards or participates in alleys.” If they made <i>eruv</i> for the courtyards, they are permitted in the courtyards and forbidden in the alley. But if they participated in the alley they are permitted here and there. He told them, also you will cause the rules of <i>eruv</i> to be forgotten among us<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">This is proposed as R. Meïr’s argument which only is alluded to in the tannaitic sources here, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 71b" href="/Eruvin.71b">Babli 71b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 6:6" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).6.6">Tosephta 6:6</a>. It is obvious that this applies only if there is no participation at all, not if one person forgot his contribution. This justifies the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.8.3">Note 138</a>.</i>, for the relationship of alley to courtyards is like the one of courtyards to houses<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Also the <i>eruv</i> of a courtyard obviates the necessity for separate <i>eruvin</i> in multi-family buildings in that courtyard, but <i>eruvin</i> in multi-family buildings in a courtyard do not take the place of an <i>eruv</i> for the courtyard.</i>.
How many courtyards must be in an alley? Rav and Samuel both say no less than two<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.19">Chapter 1, Note 137</a>. Differently <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 74a" href="/Eruvin.74a">Babli 74a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Even a courtyard on one side and a house on the other, a house on one side and a store on the other. Rav Naḥman bar Jacob asked: An alley has no less than two courtyards, does a courtyard have no less than two houses? <sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 1, Notes 133, 135.</i> An alley whose length and width are equal is not permitted by lath or beam but by planks like a courtyard. Samuel asked Rav, by how much must their length exceed their width? He told him, anything. The dwellers in the Land of Israel make them square.
Rebbi Yasa said, when I still was there I heard the voice of Rav Jehudah asking Samuel, if somebody put aside his <i>šeqel</i> and died? He told him, it should be used for voluntary gifts<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">It is stated in Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6 that “a promise to Heaven is like delivery to an individual.” Therefore if money was dedicated for the yearly Temple tax and the person died before it was delivered to the tax collectors, it can neither be given as tax nor revert to profane status. It is stated here that it should be given to the Temple for its special fund with which animals are bought for elevation offerings at times when no other offerings are on the altar. The Temple tax itself may be used only for obligatory offerings.</i>. The excess of his tenth of an <i>epha,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">The daily flour offering of the High Priest, to be paid from his own pocket (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:13" href="/Leviticus.6.13"><i>Lev</i>. 6:13</a>). If the High Priest designated money for his offering at a specified day and not all of it is used, the situation is similar to that of the Temple tax of a deceased person.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, he should bring it to the Dead Sea<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Where it would be dissolved by the chemicals in the water, i. e., it should be destroyed so that nobody could have usufruct from it.</i>; Rebbi Eleazar said, it should be used for voluntary gifts. They asked before him<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this refers to R. Yasa, whose traditions in a different matter are quoted preceding the question.</i>: May one cede rights from courtyard to courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">This may refer either to the situation described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.9.1">Mishnah 9</a>, an inner courtyard which has right of egress through an outer one and did not make an <i>eruv</i> together with the outer yard, or two houses on both sides of a collapsed building where one cannot make an <i>eruv</i> for the space between the houses since <i>eruvin</i> are instituted only for courtyards (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Eruvin 66b" href="/Rashi_on_Eruvin.66b">Rashi <i>ad</i> 66b</a>). The answer given here is in the Babli attributed to R. Joḥanan.</i>? He told them, one may cede rights from courtyard to courtyard.
<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 19:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.19.1.3"><i>Šabbat</i> Chapter 19, Notes 36–43</a>.</i> They asked before Rebbi Yasa: Since you say about implements which were resting anywhere in the courtyard, one may move them in the entire courtyard, similarly concerning implements which were resting anywhere in a dead-end street, may one move them in the entire dead-end street? They asked him in <i>Šabbat</i> and he did not answer since Rebbi Yasa said, each matter only at its place. When they came to <i>Eruvin</i> he told them in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: one may move implements which were resting anywhere in a dead-end street in the entire dead-end street. Rav said, one may move them only four cubits. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rav is consistent and Rebbi Johanan is consistent. Since Rebbi Joḥanan says that a beam permits without participation, it is he who says that one may move in the entire dead-end street. Since Rav said that a beam does not permit without participation, it is he who says that one may move it only within four cubits. In Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion, why does one participate in the entire dead-end street? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, to make all domains as one.
It is obvious that one may rely on participation in alleys<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Maimonides (<i>Eruvin</i> 5:14) reads the statement as referring to intentional participation for the alley. However, the example given in the next paragraph indicates that any partnership, even if not intended as formal participation, may be counted as one for the rules of the Sabbath.</i>. May one rely on participation in courtyards? Both Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa and Rebbi Ze`ira, one in the name of Mar Uqban, the other in the name of Rav Naḥman bar Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">A name not otherwise mentioned in the Talmudim; this name does not appear in the quote of this paragraph by Ravad in his Note on Maimonides <i>Eruvin</i> 5:14</i>: One may rely on participation in courtyards in an emergency on the first Sabbath. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav Jehudah: For example those of Bar Delaiah who do not insist on their small change<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Both Maimonides and Ravad read  פרוסתן“their piece of bread” instead of פרוטתן “their small change”. Their reading seems preferable since an insistence on one’s own piece of bread negates the idea of participation in a common meal. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 49a" href="/Eruvin.49a">Babli 49a</a> the people of ורדינא (“town of roses”, which in the opinion of S. Liebermann is the same as ברדליה by a common change of letters א-ה ,נ-ל ,ו-ב) are characterized as misers; it must not read the word “not” in this sentence.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: Even not like those of Bar Delaiah who do not insist on their small change.
