It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Since the story is Amoraic, probably the word תני should be deleted.</i>: It happened that they were assembled for a meal in the open courtyard when the Sabbath turned holy<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">All the dwellers in the courtyard were at a (wedding?) feast held in the open courtyard on Friday afternoon.</i>. The case came before Samuel who said, since they started eating the dish when it still was daytime it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Since they all ate from the same food at sundown, the common food may represent an <i>eruv</i> for this courtyard.</i>. This implies even assembled for a meal in the open courtyard. This implies, even if they did not make an <i>eruv</i>. This implies, even if they did not intend to<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody intended from the start that the food should be used as <i>eruv</i>, Samuel’s ruling was forced by the fact that if it could not be counted as <i>eruv</i> they could not continue with their festivity. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 85b" href="/Eruvin.85b">Babli 85b</a>, in Rav’s name 73b.</i>. This implies that two domains may not use one domain<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence makes no sense here; it is copied from a similar chain of inferences in Chapter 7, discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.5.1">Mishnah 5</a>.</i>. This implies, one may rely on participation in courtyards in an emergency<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">Since one refers to an emergency it is not necessary to state that permission is given only for a single Sabbath.</i>.
It is obvious that an <i>eruv</i> needs a house<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.4.1">Mishnah 8:4</a>. The <i>eruv</i> is valid only if deposited in one of the houses of the courtyard for which it is intended, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.7.3">Note 128</a>.</i>. Should a participation need a house? <sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">This text was written by the scribe but deleted by the corrector. S. Liebermann points out that the correction is erroneous since it is needed to understand the text and is quoted by Rashba (col. 448) from his text of the Yerushalmi. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 85b" href="/Eruvin.85b">Babli 85b</a> explicitly disagrees with this ruling and excludes the airspace of the alley.</i>(Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rav: One may put it either in the airspace of a courtyard or the airspace of the alley.<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">In each clause the second בֵּין is redundant and missing in Rashba’s quote.</i>) The word of Rav implies that an <i>eruv</i> needs a house<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Since he authorizes airspace only for participation.</i>. The word of Samuel implies that participation needs a house. A <i>baraita</i> of Samuel disagrees with him: Participation may be put into the porter’s lodge<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>baraita</i> recited in Samuel’s academy, explicitly disagreeing with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.4.1">Mishnah 8:4</a> (quoted by Tosaphot 72a, <i>s. v</i>. בפת).</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: one may put it either in the airspace of a courtyard or the airspace of the alley.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Two courtyards, one inside the other<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The inner courtyard has no direct exit to the street; its dwellers have the right to use the outer courtyard for exit and entry. An <i>eruv</i> in the inner courtyard has no influence on the outer one. An <i>eruv</i> for the outer one alone is incomplete since the inner courtyard has the same status as a multi-party dwelling opening into the courtyard.</i>. If for the inner one they made an <i>eruv</i> but not the outer one, the inner one is permitted and the outer one forbidden. If the outer one but not the inner one, both are forbidden. If both made separate <i>eruvin</i>, both are permitted separately; Rebbi Aqiba forbids the outer one since the right of access makes it forbidden but the Sages say the right of access does not make it forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">A right of exit and entry is not necessarily a right of carrying things on the Sabbath.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “Two courtyards, one inside the other,<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.9.1">Mishnah 9</a>. All of the Mishnah except the Sages’ statement is R. Aqiba’s.</i>” etc. Rebbi Yose said, this is Rebbi Aqiba’s since Rebbi Aqiba said, the right of access makes it forbidden <sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.9.1">Mishnah 9</a>. All of the Mishnah except the Sages’ statement is R. Aqiba’s.</i>. Rebbi La said in the name of Rebbi Yannai, forbidden right of access makes it forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Only if the people having right of access to the courtyard are forbidden to carry in the courtyard do they prohibit the people of the courtyard to carry there. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 59b" href="/Eruvin.59b">Babli 59b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 65b" href="/Eruvin.65b">65b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 75a" href="/Eruvin.75a">75a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one of the outer courtyard forgot and did not make the <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">And did not cede his rights.</i>, the inner one is permitted and the outer one forbidden. If of the inner one, both are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.9.1">Mishnah 9</a>, there cannot be an <i>eruv</i> in the outer one if there is none in the inner one.</i>. If they deposited they <i>eruvin</i> at one place<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Their status is as if outer and inner courtyards made separate <i>eruvin</i>.</i> and one of them forgot, whether from the inner or the outer one, both are forbidden but if they belonged to single individuals they do not have to make an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:19" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.19">Tosephta 5:19</a> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 5:7:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.5.7.5">Chapter 5, Note 156</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. “If one of the outer courtyard forgot,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">This is a discussion of the third case in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:1:2-10:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.1.2-10.3">Mishnah 10</a>, that the two courtyards deposited their <i>eruvin</i> at one and the same place and one dweller forgot to contribute. Then all dwellers are forbidden to carry in the courtyards but for different reasons. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 75b" href="/Eruvin.75b">Babli 75b</a>.</i> If one of the outer courtyard forgot and did not make the <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">And did not cede his rights.</i>, the outer one is forbidden since they do not have an <i>eruv</i>; the inner one is forbidden because they put their <i>eruv</i> at a forbidden place. If one of the inner courtyard forgot and did not make the <i>eruv</i>, the inner one is forbidden since they do not have an <i>eruv</i>; the outer one is forbidden because they put their <i>eruv</i> at a forbidden place, since the right of access makes it forbidden. “If one of the outer courtyard forgot and did not make the <i>eruv</i>;” one understands that the outer one is forbidden because they have no <i>eruv</i>. Why the inner one? Because they put their <i>eruv</i> at a forbidden place. But if they did not put their <i>eruv</i> at a forbidden place? But because the right of access makes it forbidden. There is no right of access which makes it forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>eruv</i> is in the inner courtyard and one of the outer ones failed to contribute, there seems to be no reason to forbid the inner one since they can move in their place without any special right of access and the place is correct. R. Yasa points out that since an <i>eruv</i> is invalid if it is not deposited in a house of its own courtyard, the fact that the two <i>eruvin</i> are deposited together is enough to make the two domains one and if one is forbidden so is the other.</i>! Rebbi Yasa said, because they put it at one place they all were made one domain.
Rav Ḥuna in the name of Rav: There is no rule of enclosure<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this is not “courtyard” but from the same root حٍظآر “wall, partition, screen.”</i> for a Gentile; there is no rule of stone partitions<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">An informal wall of دَكَة “cobblestone”.</i> for a Gentile. There is no rule of enclosure for a Gentile; for if he dwelt on an upper floor you take him down to forbid<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">If the Gentile lives on the upper floor of a multi-family house or in the inner courtyard behind a separate partition he still is counted as dwelling in the courtyard and prohibits carrying by the Jewish inhabitants as long as his rights are not leased by his Jewish neighbors.</i>. There is no rule of stone partitions for a Gentile for if he dwelt in the inner [courtyard] you would take him out to forbid. Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: There is a rule of enclosure for a Gentile, for if an Israel and a Gentile were dwelling in the inner [courtyard] he never forbids unless there be two Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. Eliezer ben Jacob in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: He who has a stone partition inside his house ten hand-breadths high, if an Israel he does not forbid, if a Gentile he forbids<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">A Jew who is aware of the rules of the Sabbath and erects a partition between his property and the courtyard shows that he is not to be counted as part of the courtyard. But a Gentile can be expected to change his mind at any time; therefore he cannot be considered separated until his rights have been leased from him; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.3.1">Note 10</a>.</i>. The argument of Rebbi Abba seems inverted. There Rebbi Abba said, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: If a wall was surrounded by ladders on both sides one makes two <i>eruvin</i> but not one <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">The following sentence makes it clear that S. Liebermann is correct to read וְאִין “and if” and not וְאֵין “but not”. In that case one must assume that a word רָצוּ “they so desire” is missing, probably because the scribe or his <i>Vorlage</i> misread וְאִין as וְאֵין; although one has to assume that the same error was made in Chapter 7 (24b line 70) and the text as in the ms. is quoted by R. Ḥananel <i>ad</i> 75a.. According to the text as it stands the two statements of R. Abba reinforce one another. If two courtyards which are connected by ladders, of difficult access, must have separate <i>eruvin</i>, then a Jew who barricades himself so that his access to the courtyard is difficult, should not be counted as dwelling there. But in the corrected reading the statements seem to contradict one another; the dwellers in the courtyard should not be able to disregard the one who barricaded himself in his house.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, there because of the ladders they have the status of doors, but here from the upper floors one is used to descend, from the lower one is not used to ascend<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, a dweller in the inner yard must pass through the outer one, but a dweller in the outer one has no reason to enter the inner.</i>.

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a window between two courtyards is four-by-four [hand-breadths] within ten [hand-breadths], one makes two <i>eruvin</i> but if they wish they make one <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The window is simply a rectangular opening in the wall between two courtyards, not closed with glass or anything else. If the opening is at least 4 hand-breadths wide and 4 high it is usable as a door. If the lower rim of the hole is no more than 10 hand-breadths from the ground, the wall at that place is not considered an obstacle. The status of the two courtyards is as if they were connected by a real door; it is up to the dwellers in the courtyards to decide whether to treat them as one or two.</i>. If less than four-by-four [hand-breadths] or higher than ten [hand-breadths], one makes two <i>eruvin</i> and cannot make one <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A wall 10 hand-breadths high is a partition which separates the courtyards completely. In addition, an opening less than 4-by-4 in size is difficult to use; it cannot substitute for a door.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a window between two courtyards,” etc. Lattice work<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">If the window was closed by vertical and horizontal bars it cannot be used as door and two separate <i>eruvin</i> are required.</i> diminishes it for the laws of Sabbath, but it does not diminish for the laws of presumed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">A person may prove ownership by a claim of lawful acquisition combined with proof of undisturbed possession during three years; for the laws of real estate properties connected by a window with bars may still be counted as one; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.8.1">Mishnah <i>Bava batra</i> 3:8</a>.</i>. Straw and provender do not diminish in it; pebbles and provender do diminish in it<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Anything which is disregarded for the rules of ditches (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>) is disregarded for windows.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, if grasses grew in it, they do not diminish it.
They asked before Rebbi Abba: Concerning what you are saying about a window between two courtyards, is it the same for a window between two houses? He told them, yes. Is it the same for a window between two roofs? He told them, yes. May one make an <i>eruv</i> across skylights<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Between the living quarters and the flat roof.</i>? Rebbi Abba Mari said, it is a disagreement between Rav and Samuel, as we have stated there: “All roofs of a city are one domain.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.1.1">Mishnah 9:1</a>.</i>” Samuel said, up to two <i>bet se’ah</i>; Rav said, one may move inside them even a <i>kor</i>, even two <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah does not spell out what kind of domain is formed by the roofs. For Samuel, roofs are places which are not fenced in for dwellings; they follow the rule of corrals where carrying is only allowed if the surface area is at most 5’000 square cubits. If the total area of the roofs is larger, an <i>eruv</i> would be ineffective; the roof cannot be part of the <i>eruv</i> domain of the house. But for Rav one is permitted to carry on connected roofs without limitations (even if the total area be larger than 2 <i>bet kor,</i> 60 <i>bet se’ah</i>); the <i>eruv</i> may be extended to the roof. The Babli has a very different tradition, 89a–90b.</i>.
Not only that the entire four-by-four [hand-breadths] must be within ten [hand-breadths] but even if part of four-by-four [hand-breadths] are within ten [hand-breadths]it is as if all four-by-four [hand-breadths] were within ten [hand-breadths]<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 76a" href="/Eruvin.76a">Babli 76a</a>.</i>. If the circumference was 96 hand-breadths, if only the most minute amount was within ten [hand-breadths] it is as if all of four-by-four [hand-breadths] were within ten [hand-breadths]<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">If the window is square, 4-by-4 <i>cubits</i>, each side is 24 hand-breadths, so that the total circumference is 96 hand-breadths. As S. Liebermann points out, the Yerushalmi implies that a window 4-by-4 hand-breadths must start visibly lower (“part of it”) than 10 hand-breadths from the ground but if it is 4-by-4 cubits the most minute amount, not visible by the naked eye, is sufficient.</i>. If it was round of circumference 90, etc<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is a corrector’s addition to the ms. and probably should be deleted. A circular hole is a qualifying window if it contains an inscribed square of side lengths 4. A circumference has to be about 11% larger than 16 hand-breadths to qualify as a 4 hand-breadth window or 96 hand-breadths for the 4 cubits rule.</i>.
May one reduce using utensils<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If the window in the wall is more than 10 hand-breadths from the ground, may one use utensils on the ground below the window to reduce the distance so that it would be permissible to make a common <i>eruv</i>? As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 77a" href="/Eruvin.77a">Babli 77a</a> points out the problem is that utensils may be moved on the Sabbath and therefore are disqualified as building materials.</i>? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ashi said, one may reduce using utensils. Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Isaac bar Ṭevlai: Rebbi Joḥanan said, one may not use utensils. There<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia.</i>, they said, it is a disagreement between the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya and Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose. One said, it is forbidden; but one said, it is permitted. He who said that it is forbidden, maybe he would forget and move it<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">As noted before, utensils may be moved but utensils used as part of the building become <i>muqṣeh</i>; this may easily be overlooked.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan bar<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This is the scribe’s text, erroneously changed by the corrector into כד.</i> Marius instructed to cover it with a heap of sand<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic كوفة.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 7:10" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).7.10">Tosephta 7:10</a>. “Anything” seems to mean any building material.</i>: “One may use anything to reduce, stones, bricks, a Tyrian or an Egyptian ladder if it was fastened<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">A Tyrian ladder is very heavy and not intended to being moved. Therefore the use of such a ladder to facilitate crossing from one courtyard to the other is unproblematic. But an Egyptian ladder is light and built to being moved; its use presents all problems connected with the use of utensils. Egyptian ladders are not mentioned in the Tosephta. Rashba (<i>ad</i> 77a, col. 470) prefers to read in the text that only Egyptian ladders must be permanently fixed to the wall.</i>.” Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: Even if it was not fastened. But was it not stated “if it was fastened”? Rebbi Abba said, if it was dedicated for this purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">An Egyptian ladder is acceptable even unfastened if it is intended to be a permanent fixture at the wall.</i>. Rebbi La instructed following this Tanna, only if it was fastened. Both Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose, one in the name of Mar Uqban, and one in the name of Rav Naḥman bar Jacob: It is necessary that between two steps<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Of any ladder used to climb up to the window.</i> there be less than three [hand-breadths] and each be four hand-breadths wide; the posts complement the four<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The total width of the ladder, posts and steps, must be 4 hand-breadths.</i>. Yasa said, a block in which he carved steps reduces<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">A big stone or a block of wood into which steps are carved is an acceptable ladder for the rules of <i>eruv</i>.</i>. In any case, the posts do not complement ten; it is like a high step<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">These are two different statements. The length of the posts of a ladder is irrelevant for the rules of <i>eruv</i>; only the steps count and the uppermost step must be within ten hand-breadths of the lower rim of the window. A block of wood with carved steps is not under the 3 hand-breadths rule since the block itself is a high step.</i>. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Abun bar Cahana: It must be stretched four hand-breadths from the wall as separate place. Rebbi Ḥizqiah in the name of Rebbi Abun bar Cahana: The step which reduces from ten must be stretched four hand-breadths from the wall as a separate place<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Ḥizqiah explains of the one of R. Yasa. Only the step which is counted as being less than 10 hand-breadths from the window must be a domain in its own right.</i>. Rebbi Jacob the Southerner asked: <sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.9.2">Chapter 1, Notes 253–255</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>:9 ropes represent a wall if they are less than 3 hand-breadths apart but in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.1.1">Mishnah 7:1</a> a hole is an opening only if it is at least 4 wide and high.</i> If one brought a board of seven hand-breadths and raised it less than three from the ground, is it permitted? There you are saying, everything less than three [handbreadths] is considered closed, and here you should be saying, everything less than four [handbreadths] is considered closed. If you want to object, object to the following: If one brought a wall of slightly more than four hand-breadths and raised it less than two hand-breadths from the ground, should it come following what Rebbi Joḥanan said, what is standing and empty space combine for four hand-breadths, on condition that what is standing be more than the empty space?

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a wall between two courtyards is ten [hand-breadths] high and four wide, one makes two <i>eruvin</i> and cannot make one <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A wall 10 hand-breadths high is a partition which separates the courtyards completely. In addition, an opening less than 4-by-4 in size is difficult to use; it cannot substitute for a door.</i>. If produce was lying on top of it, both partiess may climb up and eat them; only they may not take them down<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If on the top of the wall there is any food, it may be taken from either side on the Sabbath if it is accessible by ladders before the start of the Sabbath. If the top of the wall is 4 hand-breadths wide it defines a domain by itself which cannot be included in the <i>eruv</i> of either side. In this case the food may not be taken down.</i>. If a wall was torn down up to ten cubits, one makes two <i>eruvin</i> but if they wish they make one <i>eruv</i>. More than this, one makes one <i>eruv</i> but may not make two<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.1.1">Note 1</a>, any wall not higher than 10 hand-breadths may be (but need not be) disregarded.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. <sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i>“If a wall between two courtyards,” etc. Why does it have to be four [hand-breadths] wide? Even if it is not four [hand-breadths] wide? Because of what is stated later: “both sides may climb up and eat them.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If on the top of the wall there is any food, it may be taken from either side on the Sabbath if it is accessible by ladders before the start of the Sabbath. If the top of the wall is 4 hand-breadths wide it defines a domain by itself which cannot be included in the <i>eruv</i> of either side. In this case the food may not be taken down.</i>” If it is not four [hand-breadths] wide, Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav: it is forbidden for both sides. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav: it is permitted for both sides. The argument of Rebbi Abba seems inverted<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Since there seems to be no reason to forbid taking the food in this case.</i>. There Rebbi Abba said, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: If he threw it and it came to rest on top of a partition he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody stands in the public domain and throws something for more than 4 cubits and it come to rest on the narrow top of a fence of a private domain it is considered as coming to rest inside the private domain; the thrower is liable for a biblical Sabbath violation.</i>. And here, he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since he forbids access to the top of a narrow wall between private domains he must hold that this is a separate domain; then there is no difference whether the wall be four hand-breadths wide or not, against the statement of the Mishnah.<br>The answer is that if one considers the private domain as filled up with dust or pebbles to the height of the fence, the top of the fence clearly belongs to the private domain. But here one is speaking of a wall between two private domains where the argument is inappropriate.</i>? There one considers it as if filled with dust and pebbles. Here, what do you have? You should know this since Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥinena said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Ledges and walls high ten [hand-breadths] and wide four are permitted for both sides, on condition that he may not exchange<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Šabbat</i> Chapter 1 Note 170, Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 8b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 99b" href="/Eruvin.99b">99b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">For reasons of chronology it is difficult to accept the reading “R. Joḥanan”. <i>Pene Moshe</i> emends to “R. Ḥanan”.</i> asked. The argument of Rebbi Abba seems inverted. There Rebbi Abba said, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: If he threw it and it came to rest on top of a partition he is liable. And here, he says so? You are saying there, one considers it as if filled with dust and pebbles; here it is the same.
We heard about partitions in a private domain, we heard about partitions in a public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The statement about partitions in private domains is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, about public domains is the one of Rav Jehudah quoted in the preceding paragraph.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:10:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.10.3">Chapter 6 Note 178</a>. Here one is forced to read אֵין. It is clear from the earlier discussion (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.1.6">Note 37</a>) that two connecting ladders on a wall permit but do not require making a single <i>eruv</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 60a" href="/Eruvin.60a">Babli 60a</a>).</i> Rebbi Abba, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: If a wall is surrounded by ladders on both sides, one makes two <i>eruvin</i> and may not make one <i>eruv</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, since they are not at the same place, are they like doors?
It was stated: The raising of a ramp is one cubit when he progresses three except for the ramp of the altar which collects ten hand-breadths for three and one third fingers, since the altar was ten cubits [high] and its ramp thirty-two<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 63a" href="/Zevachim.63a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 63a</a>. The standard for building ramps was height: base = 1:3 except the altar where it was 10:32. The sentence “which collects ten hand-breadths for three and one third fingers” is corrupt. In <i>Arukh</i>, <i>s. v</i>. כבש the reading is וְכָל־תֵּל הַמִּתְלַקֵּט עֲשָׂרָה טְפָחִים מִתּוֹךְ חָמֵשׁ אַמּוֹת הוּא שְׁלִישׁ “any hill which rises 10 hand-breadths for 5 cubits (30 hand-breadths) is one third.”</i>.
If one made a bench along the entire wall which is four hand-breadths wide one makes two <i>eruvin</i> and may not make one; with four cubits one makes one <i>eruv</i> and may not make two<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">One might conjecture that for “and may not make one” one should read “and if they wish they may make one” since if the wall was not higher than 10 hand-breadths over the step which is 4 wide the Mishnah allows making one <i>eruv</i>. The new statement of the <i>baraita</i> is that if the step was easy to use, being 4 cubits wide, the wall less than 10 hand-breadths high becomes a permanently open door and one <i>eruv</i> is mandatory.</i>.
That is if it is large but if it is small by most of it. What is large and what is small? Any where most of it is at least ten, by ten; therefore less than ten by most of it. That is meant in the middle, but not from the side. What is on the side and what is in the middle? If what is standing is four [hand-breadths] it is in the middle, otherwise it is on the side<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">This is a direct commentary on the last statement of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> that if the wall is torn down up to 10 cubits one may make one <i>eruv</i>. If the entire wall is only 11 cubits and 10 are missing there is no wall and it should be impossible to make 2 <i>eruvin</i>. Therefore the statement of the Mishnah may apply directly only to walls more than 20 cubits long.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a ditch between two courtyards is ten [hand-breadths] deep and four wide, one makes two <i>eruvin</i> but cannot make one <i>eruv</i> even if it is full of straw or provender. If it is full of dust or pebbles, one makes one <i>eruv</i> but cannot make two <i>eruvin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A ditch 10 hand-breadths deep and 4 wide from wall to wall is treated as if it were a wall more than 10 hand-breadths high. If the ditch is used for storage but contents will be removed and used in the foreseeable future, it remains a separation for the rules of the Sabbath. But if it is filled with material which probably will not be removed, it does not divide from the moment that the actual ditch is no longer 10 hand-breadths deep.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. <sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Commentary on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>. The text is taken from a discussion of the impurity of the dead, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:2:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.2.7"><i>Nazir</i> 9:2 Notes 79–87</a>, <i>Pesaḥim</i> 7:7 34d l. 27.</i> Therefore straw to be disregarded cannot be disregarded. Does the Mishnah not follow Rebbi Yose, since Rebbi Yose said, straw said to be disregarded is disregarded<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the rules of the tent-impurity caused by a corpse. A “tent” is any covered space in which there is at least one hand-breadth of space between the corpse and the roof. If the space is enclosed, the impurity is restricted to the “tent”; anything above the ceiling and below the floor of the “tent” is pure. But if the entire space between floor and ceiling is filled with matter, there is no tent and the impurity extends indefinitely above and below the tent space. This is known as “squeezed impurity” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 15:1" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.15.1">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 15:1</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 15:5" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.15.5">5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 15:6" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.15.6">6</a>). It is implied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 15:5" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.15.5">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 15:5</a> that R. Yose restricts “squeezed impurity” to material permanently deposited; but a storage of straw which is to be removed in the future is not counted as filler.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav Ḥisda: It is everybody’s opinion. What Rebbi Yose said, if he mixed it with dust<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Not really “mixed with”, but “treated like,” as formulated in the Tosephta.</i>. “There is straw which is treated like dust and dust which is treated like straw. Straw not to be removed is like dust; dust to be removed is like straw.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Statement of R. Yose in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 15:5" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.15.5">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 15:5</a>; quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 79a" href="/Eruvin.79a"><i>Eruvin</i> 79a</a>. “Straw” stands here for “material to be removed,” “dust” for “permanent filling.”</i>” In the House of Rebbi Yannai they said: If he covered it with mats it is disregarded. Come and see: If he filled it with mats it is not disregarded<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since the filling can easily be removed, the ditch still separates.</i>, if he covered it with mats it is disregarded<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">If a ditch is filled with any material, even straw, but this is covered with mats to create a floor from one side to the other, the courtyards become one and require one <i>eruv</i>. <i>Šabbat</i> 100a.</i>. If he filled it with branches of date palms it is problematic<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">No ruling is available in this case.</i>. Rebbi Zeriqan, Rebbi Immi, in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Even thin sheets<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">This translation is tentative; it follows the <i>Pesaḥim</i> text, reading רַק. Neither the text here רוֹק “spittle” nor the one in <i>Nazir</i> רֵיק “emptiness” are appropriate.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one put a plank of any kind over it<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Over the ditch. While there are no requirements for the thickness of the plank it must be strong enough to carry a person. If the plank is less than 4 hand-breadths wide, people are afraid to use it and therefore it cannot be counted as a connection between the two sides and two separate <i>eruvin</i> are required.</i> which is four [hand-breadths] wide, or two balconies<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 11:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.11.2.1"><i>Šabbat</i> 11:2, Note 3</a>. The roadway between the two balconies represents a ditch. The statements of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.4.1">Note 6</a> apply here also.</i> one opposite the other, one makes two <i>eruvin</i> but if they wish they make one <i>eruv</i>. Less than this, one makes two <i>eruvin</i> but cannot make one <i>eruv</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>. Some state “one makes two <i>eruvin</i> and cannot make one <i>eruv</i>.” Some state, “one makes one <i>eruv</i> and cannot make two <i>eruvin</i>.” For him who says “one makes two <i>eruvin</i> and cannot make one <i>eruv</i>”, “less than this” refers to the plank. For him who says, “one makes one <i>eruv</i> and cannot make two <i>eruvin</i>,” “less than this” refers to the space<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">If the ditch is not 10 hand-breadths deep or the balconies not 10 hand-breadths above the ground.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a straw-heap between two courtyards is ten [hand-breadths] high, one makes two <i>eruvin</i> but cannot make one <i>eruv</i>. Each feeds from his side. If the straw became less than ten [hand-breadths] high, one makes one <i>eruv</i> but cannot make two <i>eruvin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">It is assumed that the straw heap fills the entire space between the two courtyards from wall to wall. Then the straw acts as a temporary wall. If at the start of the Sabbath one cannot move from one side to the other one must make two <i>eruvin</i>. But if the temporary wall is no more than 10 hand-breadths high it <i>must</i> be disregarded and one <i>eruv</i> is obligatory.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph. Rebbi Eleazar said, so is the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 5. One should not read the Mishnah as requiring that the cattle be brought to feed from the straw; the straw may be taken in containers and carried to the animal barn. Rejected by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 79a" href="/Eruvin.79a">Babli 79a</a>.</i>: “Each fills his pail and feeds from his side.” Rebbi Ḥaggai said, when we were sitting before Menaḥem we used to say, lest you say that he is not like one taking down tents but one who supports tents, for if the straw was flattened he supports it by his pail<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since the heap of straw acts as a wall between the courtyards, forcing two <i>eruvin</i>, should taking straw from it not be classified as tearing down a wall, prohibited on the Sabbath? Even taking straw to smoothe the surface of the heap might be construed as prohibited activity. Therefore the statement of R. Eleazar is required; changing the shape of a straw heap is not a building activity since, as noted in the sequel, these are only temporary changes whereas building leaves long term results.</i>. Rav Hoshaia asked: What if he used a sheet and supported it by a chair? It is meant here temporarily, but it is meant there for permanence.
It was stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 6:17" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).6.17">Tosephta 6:17</a>.</i> “A straw heap between two courtyards. Each one opens his door and feeds. If the straw was less than ten hand-breadths, both are forbidden until they make an <i>eruv</i>. Therefore if one of them wants to block his door and cede his rights, he is forbidden but his neighbor is permitted.” (And so it is with a cistern) [A ditch]<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses was originally written by the scribe; he himself then deleted it and replaced it by the text in brackets which makes the sentence a meaningless quote from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>. The original statement seems to be that from a cistern between two houses, where in general one may not draw water on the Sabbath (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.6.1">Mishnah 8:7</a>), if one of the owners locks his door and cedes his rights, the other party may use the cistern without problems.</i> between two courtyards. This implies that one may not cede from courtyard to courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since cession alone is not enough without locking the door.</i>. This implies, even if they did not make an <i>eruv</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 6, Notes 161,162.</i> This implies, even if they did not intend to. This implies that two domains may not use one domain. This implies, if each of them uses his door, both are forbidden. And so it is with a house between two courtyards, not following Rebbi Meïr, who said that a locked house is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 8:5.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How may one participate<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">How may one person arrange participation of all dwellers in an alley? This does not exclude that everyone may contribute his own share of food.</i> in an alley? One puts down an amphora and says, this shall be for all dwellers in the alley, and transfers rights through his adult son or daughter or his male or female Hebrew slave<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The institution of Hebrew slavery disappeared at the end of the First Commonwealth; cf. <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:2.</i> or through his wife<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">All these are legally persons in their own right; they may accept gifts from the owner in behalf of others.</i>. But he cannot transfer rights through his minor son or daughter or his Canaanite<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">All slaves that could be held during the Second Commonwealth and later, cf. <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3. These and minor children have no independent legal standing and no property separate from that of their owner or father; therefore giving to them is like giving to oneself, without legal consequences.</i> male or female slave because their hand is like his own.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.6.1">Mishnah 7:6</a>.</i>. It was stated: He cannot transfer rights to the amphora which he has in the cellar<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The amphora has to be kept separated lest it be accidentally used on weekdays. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 68a" href="/Eruvin.68a">Babli 68a</a>.</i>. Rav Hoshaia said, lest he forget and drink from it.
Where do we hold<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.6.1">Mishnah 6</a> the text is from <i>Ma</i>`<i>aser Šeni</i> 4:4, Notes 80–97.<br>The problem is that the Mishnah enables a Hebrew slave girl to act as an agent. But a Hebrew woman can only be sold into slavery by her father (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:7-11" href="/Exodus.21.7-11"><i>Ex</i>. 21:7–11</a>) while she is in his power and only for as long as she would be in his power. This means that when she is legally an adult she either is the wife of the man who bought her or she is a free woman. The inclusion of the female Hebrew slave seems to be inappropriate since the Mishnah explicitly excludes the owner’s own minor children.</i>? If she is an adult, she becomes free by the signs [of puberty]. If she is underage, may an underage person transfer rights? Rebbi Yudan bar Shalom said before Rebbi Yose: Explain it following him who said that a minor may give heave<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The status of a minor in matters of heave and tithes is a matter of dispute, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.13"><i>Terumot</i> 1:1 Notes 56–59</a>.</i>. He answered him, but even according to him who says that a minor cannot give heave, the minor can acquire rights. <sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The remainder of this paragraph also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:9:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.9.6"><i>Giṭtin</i> 5:9 Notes 234–238</a>.</i> This is acceptable following the opinion of the rabbis there. There, they are saying in the name of Rav Naḥman bar Jacob: One to whom one gives a nut and he throws it away, a pebble and he keeps it, what one finds in his hand is as if found on a garbage heap; a nut and he keeps it, a pebble and he throws it away, what one robs from him is robbery because of the ways of peace; a nut or a pebble and he takes, hides them, and produces them later, what is robbed from him is total robbery. He can acquire for himself but not for others. Rav Huna said, just as he can acquire for himself so he can acquire for others. Everybody agrees that his gift is not a gift, since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a>.</i>, <i>if a man give</i>, a man’s gift is a gift, the gift of a minor is no gift, the words of the Sages. Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, Rebbi Jacob [bar Aḥa]<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Added from the parallels; missing here but necessary.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Robbing from him is never clear robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Robbing a minor may be prosecutable under police law, not under biblical criminal law.</i> unless he grew two pubic hairs. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: That which you say is to make one pay by a law suit; but to have to bring a sacrifice for a [false] oath everybody agrees only after he grew two pubic hairs<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">A minor might be able to bring a suit to recover what was taken from him but since he is not subject to criminal law in case of perjury he may not atone by a sacrifice.</i>. But following to the rabbis here, Rebbi Yose asked that even for himself he should not be able to acquire since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a>.</i> <i>to his neighbor</i>, only if he be like his neighbor. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac: They came to it by the rules of school children as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.9.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭtin</i> 5:9</a>.</i>: “School children’s buying is buying and their selling is selling, for movables.” But did we not state: “But he cannot transfer rights through his minor son or daughter or his Canaanite<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">All slaves that could be held during the Second Commonwealth and later, cf. <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3. These and minor children have no independent legal standing and no property separate from that of their owner or father; therefore giving to them is like giving to oneself, without legal consequences.</i> male or female slave because their hand is like his hand.” The rabbis of Caesarea say, here a minor with knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">A minor whose vows may be valid.</i>, there a minor without knowledge.
<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is in <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3, Notes 383–390.</i> There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Bava mesia</i>` 8:4. The borrower of livestock is responsible if the animal is injured or dies while in his custody (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:13" href="/Exodus.22.13"><i>Ex</i>. 22:13</a>). The responsibility of the borrower starts the moment he takes control of the animal. As long as the animal is driven by the owner’s slave, it is in the owner’s hand and the borrower is not responsible. But (as stated in the part of the Mishnah not quoted here) if the borrower said, send it to me through your slave, and something happens on the way, the borrower is responsible.</i>: “If somebody borrows a cow and [the lender] sent her to him through his son, his slave, or his agent, in case it died he is not liable.” Does this not say that a slave is able to transfer rights from his master to another person? Rebbi Eleazar said, explain it about a Hebrew slave. Rebbi Joḥanan said, you can even explain it for a Canaanite slave, it he told him, open the gate for her and she will go by herself, as we have stated: If he led her, drew her, called her and she followed him, he is required to pay as a borrower. Rebbi Ze`ira understood it from here: “But he cannot make them acquire through his minor son or daughter or his Canaanite male or female slave, because their hand is not like his hand.” Does this not imply that a slave is not able to transfer rights from his master to another person? Explain it following Rebbi Meïr since Rebbi Meïr makes the hand of the slave the hand of his master. But did we not state “his wife”? Rebbi Meïr holds that the hand of the wife is the hand of her husband! Rebbi Ḥananiah said in the name of Rebbi Phineas, explain it following the Tanna who stated<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Ma</i>`<i>aser šeni</i> 4:7. The Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 24a restricts the statement of R. Simeon ben Eleazar to the case where the wife inherited Second Tithe. Since R. Meïr holds that Second Tithe is Heaven’s money, the Second Tithe does not become part of the estate. If the wife now redeems the tithe with household money, the money is the husband’s but the tithe is not; therefore, no additional fifth is due. The Babli rejects the conclusion of the Yerushalmi here.</i>: “His wife cannot redeem Second Tithe for him. Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says in the name of Rebbi Meïr, his wife can redeem Second Tithe for him.” For that Tanna, Rebbi Meïr makes the hand of the slave the hand of his master but not the hand of the wife the hand of her husband!

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the food became less<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If part of the food of a valid <i>eruv</i> was lost by evaporation or because it was eaten by mice.</i> he adds, transfers rights, but does not have to notify. If [dwellers] are added he adds, transfers rights, and has to notify<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This implies that the person who makes the <i>eruv</i> or arranges the participation from his own money (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.6.1">Note 9</a>) has to inform all dwellers benefiting from his act. Therefore he also has to inform the newcomers that they are included.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.7.1">Mishnah 7</a>. Why does he have to notify in the second case but not in the first? Not by logical necessity but by rabbinic decree.</i>. Why does he notify? By practice. Does this not imply that he does not transfers rights to the food proper<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">If the food became less than the required quantity because he took from it, it would be theft if he did not retain full ownership of everything. But this contradicts the nature of <i>eruv</i>. Therefore the case must be restricted to shrinkage by external causes and the first question also is moot.</i>? Rebbi Ḥanina said, explain it if rats dragged it away.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a straw-heap between two courtyards is ten [hand-breadths] high, one makes two <i>eruvin</i> but cannot make one <i>eruv</i>. Each feeds from his side. If the straw became less than ten [hand-breadths] high, one makes one <i>eruv</i> but cannot make two <i>eruvin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">It is assumed that the straw heap fills the entire space between the two courtyards from wall to wall. Then the straw acts as a temporary wall. If at the start of the Sabbath one cannot move from one side to the other one must make two <i>eruvin</i>. But if the temporary wall is no more than 10 hand-breadths high it <i>must</i> be disregarded and one <i>eruv</i> is obligatory.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.8.1">Mishnah 8</a>.</i>. Rav said, many are seventeen, few are sixteen. Rebbi Joḥanan said, any if it were distributed there would be the volume of a dried fig for everybody are many, otherwise they are few<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that the places of “many” and “few” have to be switched.</i>. (Paragraph.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This note is misplaced; it should refer to discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.9.1">Mishnah 9</a> but this is the next paragraph.</i>) They wanted to say that they do not disagree; what Rav said follows Rebbi Meïr, and he who follows Rebbi Joḥanan follows Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The basis of this discussion is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.2.1">Mishnah 8:2</a> where for a multi-party <i>eruv teḥumim</i> R. Meïr requires food for two weekday meals irrespective of numbers but R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa requires a loaf that can be bought for a<i>dupondius</i> if 4 <i>se’ah</i> (24 <i>qab</i>) are a tetradrachma (4 <i>denarii</i> = 48 <i>dupondii</i>) (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:24" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.24"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1 Notes 100–107</a>). Therefore his loaf has a volume of ½ <i>qab</i> or of 12 standard eggs which are 24 standard dried figs. Rebbi Simeon requires only ⅔ of this volume, 16 dried figs. Therefore one has to read “R. Simeon” instead of “R. Meïr” since the latter does not distinguish between the many and the few. In this interpretation “many” for R. Joḥanan are 25. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 80b" href="/Eruvin.80b">Babli disagrees, 80b</a>.</i>.
What are remains of an <i>eruv</i>? Similar to what Rebbi Yose said in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: A hyssop with which he sprinkled once is qualified; more that that are remains<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">If a hyssop was qualified and used once to sprinkle water with ashes of the Red Cow it remains qualified even if some leaves fall off (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 11:8" href="/Mishnah_Parah.11.8">Mishnah <i>Parah</i> 11:8</a>).</i>. And here it is the same.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Yose said, when has this been said? When one starts an <i>eruv</i>. But remains of an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The first week an <i>eruv</i> is used the correct amount is required. Once the <i>eruv</i> is valid, it remains so in future weeks as long as some of it is in existence.</i> [are valid] in any quantity. They said to make <i>eruv</i> for courtyards only lest the children forget<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of R. Meïr in the preceding Chapter. Children may not be aware of participation in the alley and get the impression that carrying on the Sabbath is permitted everywhere.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.2.4">Chapter 3, Notes 84–86</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, why does one make <i>eruv</i> of courtyards? Because of peaceful relations. It happened that a woman was hostile to another and sent her her <i>eruv</i> through her son. She took him, embraced him, and kissed him. He went and told it before his mother. She said, obviously she loves me and I did not know it. As a consequence they made peace. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:17" href="/Proverbs.3.17"><i>Prov</i>. 3:17</a>.</i> <i>its ways are ways of pleasantness and all its paths peace</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Anything and participation except water and salt, the words of Rebbi Eliezer. Rebbi Joshua says, an <i>eruv</i> is a loaf of bread. Even if a <i>se’ah</i> was baked but is sliced one may not use it for <i>eruv</i>; a loaf the size of an <i>as</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In Imperial Rome a small copper coin.</i> which is complete may be used for <i>eruv</i>.<br>A person may give an <i>obolus</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The smallest silver coin, ⅙ of a <i>denar</i>.</i> to a grocer or a baker to acquire part in his <i>eruv</i>, the words of Rebbi Eliezer. But the Sages say, his money did not acquire for him<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since movables are not acquired until taken in possession (Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5) the person would have to tell the grocer to be his agent and deliver his part to a third person who then acquires it for him. The only thing he cannot do is give money to acquire part of the loaf which the baker will bake as <i>eruv</i> without any further action.</i>. They agree for anybody else that his money does acquire for him, because one makes an <i>eruv</i> for a person only with his agreement. Rebbi Jehudah explained, when has this been said? For <i>eruv</i> of domains, but <i>eruvin</i> of courtyards one makes with or without his agreement since one may give benefits to a person in his absence but impose obligations on him only in his presence<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since the person who makes an <i>eruv</i> of domains loses the right to go in the opposite direction, such an <i>eruv</i> cannot be made for him without his agreement. But <i>eruv</i> of courtyards or participation in alleys gives him the right to carry there without imposing any obligations and therefore can be made without him being asked.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of Mishnah 11.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: This implies that money does not transfer title as word of the Torah. Then even if he comes to the grocer? There is a difference, maybe he would forget and sell it<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">If money did transfer title by biblical standards, the Sages could not abolish a biblical law and decree that his money could not be effective. But then it should be possible to deposit the <i>eruv</i> with the grocer, in which case the taking possession by the grocer should transfer property as required, which seems to be excluded by the Sages’ opposition to R. Eliezer. The answer is that as a matter of biblical law this is permitted, only rabbinically it is prohibited since one is afraid that the grocer inadvertently might sell the loaf designated as <i>eruv</i>.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥinena<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">He is R. Ḥanina, son of R. Abbahu (S. Liebermann.)</i>: My father did not say so but: “But the Sages say, his money did not acquire for him<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since movables are not acquired until taken in possession (Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5) the person would have to tell the grocer to be his agent and deliver his part to a third person who then acquires it for him. The only thing he cannot do is give money to acquire part of the loaf which the baker will bake as <i>eruv</i> without any further action.</i>.” This implies that money does transfer title as word from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 81b" href="/Eruvin.81b">Babli 81b</a>, <i>Bava mesiˋa</i> 47b, in the name of R. Joḥanan. Since the Mishnah states that in general money may be used to transfer title, this must be biblical law.</i>. “They agree for anybody else that his money does acquire for him,” because the grocer will transfer title through a third person. “Because one makes an <i>eruv</i> for a person only with his agreement;” the Mishnah is Rebbi Meïr’s<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.8.3">Chapter 6, Notes 99–102</a>.</i> since Rebbi Meïr said, by an <i>eruv</i>, by participation. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: In matters of <i>eruvin</i> and public fast-days everybody follows Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jacob [bar Aḥa]<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Added from the parallels; missing here but necessary.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Even about the reading of the Esther scroll everybody follows Rebbi Meïr.
Rebbi Isaac Ḥaqula<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Meïri (<i>ad</i> 80a, p. 313) reads: R. Jacob bar Idi.</i> in the name of Rebbu Jehudah the Prince: One forcibly makes an <i>eruv</i> for a person<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">If the majority of the dwellers in a courtyard or alley decide to make an <i>eruv</i> they can appeal to the court to force the minority to participate.</i>. It was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Bava meṣi`a</i> 11:18.</i>: “The dwellers in an alley force one another to install lath or beam,” and it was stated: If they told him to make an <i>eruv</i> and he does not object<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">In that case they can make the <i>eruv</i> also for him without a problem. The question is whether R. Jehudah Nesia also allows to force a person who explicitly objects to making an <i>eruv</i>.</i>. If he objects does one force him? Rebbi Yudan said, the <i>baraita</i> is Rebbi Meïr’s. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, one may explain it according to everybody if he is a Sadducee. But was it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:15" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.15">Tosephta 5:15</a>.</i>: “If one forgot and did not make an <i>eruv</i>, whether in error or intentionally he forbids, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah says intentionally he forbids, in error he does not forbid.” Intentionally he forbids; may one not make <i>eruv</i> for him forcibly? But it<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Since the problem can be eliminated by making the <i>eruv</i> for everybody without asking, why should anybody be forbidden?</i> must be a fine. Then he should be forbidden but everybody else permitted. Since they could make an <i>eruv</i> and did not make it one fines him<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">He is forbidden because he did not participate in the <i>eruv</i> and everybody else is forbidden since they did not make the <i>eruv</i> forcibly.</i>. But does Rebbi Meïr not hold that one may make something in favor of another person without his knowledge, only one may not impose a disadvantage on him<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">A general principle, Mishnah 11 here and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:5:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.5.2-9"><i>Giṭtin</i> 1:6</a>.</i>? Rebbi Meïr holds that this is not a favor; a person does not want that a neighbor may come to his house except on his invitation.
Rebbi Abba the son of Rebbi Pappai in the name of Rebbi Ḥama bar Ḥanina: It happened that a woman made an <i>eruv</i> for her mother-in-law without her knowledge. The case came before Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ismael ben R. Yose ben Ḥalaphta.</i> who wanted to forbid. Rebbi Ḥiyya told him, I heard from your father that if you can be lenient in <i>eruvin</i> you should be lenient<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 80a" href="/Eruvin.80a">Babli 80a</a>; there the roles are switched.</i>.

Chapter 8



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How does one participate for domains? One puts down<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">At the place where he wants to extend the Sabbath domain.</i> an amphora and says, this is for all people of my town who intend to go to a house of mourning or to a wedding feast<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Or any public meritorious act.</i>. Anybody who accepted when it still was daytime is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">It still needs a deliberate act by anybody who wants to use this <i>eruv</i> even though it was given for potentially everybody.</i>, after dark it is prohibited since one may not make an <i>eruv</i> after dark.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “How does one participate for domains,” etc. One does not state “one transfers rights.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.6.1">Mishnah 7:6</a>, about participation in an alley.</i>” Why does one not state “one transfers rights”? Samuel said, there<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.6.1">Mishnah 7:6</a>. He holds that for <i>eruvin</i> of domains no transfer of rights is necessary (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 80a" href="/Eruvin.80a">Babli 80a</a>). Probably in his eyes such a transfer would be impossible. For participation in an alley the persons to whom the rights are transferred form a well defined group: all dwellers in the alley. But for <i>eruvin</i> of domains the Mishnah requires a conscious act of joining the <i>eruv</i>; it cannot be known beforehand who is going to join and who won’t.</i> we stated that “one transfers rights,” one has to transfer rights; but here, where we did not state “one transfers rights”, one does not have to transfer rights. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is a matter of a conclusion <i>de minore ad maius</i>. If there, where <i>eruvin</i> of courtyards are a rabbinic institution you are saying that one has to transfer rights, not so much more <i>eruvin</i> of domains which are words of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that a transfer of rights “to whom it may concern” is required. This is not mentioned in the Mishnah since it is a logical consequence of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.6.1">Mishnah 7:6</a>.</i>? Rav follows Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In the Yerushalmi since is indicated only here; in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 80a" href="/Eruvin.80a">Babli 80a</a> it is stated that Rav and Samuel disagree about the matter.</i>, (and)<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe wrote ומר “and said” which was corrected (probably wrongly) by the corrector into אמר “he said”.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, to treat all domains equally<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe wrote ומר “and said” which was corrected (probably wrongly) by the corrector into אמר “he said”.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What is its measure? Food for two meals for everybody, food for weekday but not the Sabbath, the words of Rebbi Meïr; Rebbi Jehudah says for Sabbath but not for weekday. Both of them intended to be lenient<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In R. Meïr’s practice, people eat more on the Sabbath because they have more time to eat and it is a festive occasion; in R. Jehudah’s practice people eat more on weekdays since they need the energy for work whereas on the Sabbath there is no exertion and, therefore, less hunger.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa says, a loaf for a <i>dupondius</i> if four <i>seah</i> are a <i>tetradrachma</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>dupondius</i> is half an <i>obolus</i> or one twelfth of a <i>denarius</i> (<i>drachma</i>). Therefore a <i>dupondius</i> is 1/<sub>48</sub> of a <i>tetradrachma</i> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:24" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.24"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1, Note 100</a>.) A <i>qab</i> is ⅙ of a <i>se’ah</i> (cf. <i>Berkhot</i> 3:4 Note 164) or 1/24 of 4 <i>se’ah</i>. The Halakhah states that the baker takes ⅓ from above (which is ½ from below) for his overhead and profit. Therefore R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa requires a loaf made from <sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>/<sub>3</sub> of ½ (or ⅓) <i>qab</i> of flour.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, two thirds of a loaf if three are made from a <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">2/9 of a <i>qab</i>, 1/9 less than required by R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa.</i>; half of this for the leprous house,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">It is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:46" href="/Leviticus.14.46"><i>Lev</i>. 14:46</a> that anybody who enters a locked-down leprous house becomes impure immediately; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:47" href="/Leviticus.14.47">v. 14:47</a> states that if somebody eats in the house even his garments become impure. This is interpreted rabbinically to mean that garments become impure if the wearer stays in the house the time needed for eating half a loaf of bread (Mishnah <i>Nega`im</i> 13:9).</i> and half of this half bring impurity to a body<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">If anybody eats the volume of a quarter loaf of impure food, he may not eat sanctified food unless he purify himself in a <i>miqweh</i>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i>. Rebbi Meïr says, on a weekday, when he has nothing to eat, he eats little bread. On the Sabbath, when he has something to eat, he eats a lot of bread<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meïr states the rules for very poor people who have only a little bread for weekday meals and save all their money for a Sabbath meal.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, on a weekday, when he has nothing to eat, he eats a lot of bread. On the Sabbath, when he has something to eat, he eats little bread<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah states his rule similarly for people who have no additions to the bread on weekdays and therefore eat only bread, but on the Sabbath they have meat and vegetables and therefore eat little bread.</i>.
It was stated: Their words<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa and R. Simeon. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 82b" href="/Eruvin.82b">Babli 82b</a>.</i> are close one to another. Come and see: One makes a loaf [the volume of] twelve eggs<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The Yerushalmi defines the volume of a <i>qab</i> as that of 24 eggs; this makes the volume of a standard egg about 89cm<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup>. The text of the Mishnah implies that a loaf of R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa is half a <i>qab</i> and that of R. Simeon one third of a <i>qab</i>. These measures are far apart.<br>Since a <i>qab</i> is 16 <i>quartarii</i>, this makes the <i>quartarius</i>, the standard volume (<i>reviˋit</i>) of a ritual cup, equal to the volume of 1½ eggs. This is not found in the Babli but is accepted by the Medieval authorities (Alfasi <i>Pesaḥim</i> §763). In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 83a" href="/Eruvin.83a">Babli (83a</a>) a <i>modius</i> is estimated as volume of 217 eggs. Based on the data collected by F. Hultsch, <i>Griechische</i> <i>und römische Metrologie</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>, Berlin 1882, the <i>modius</i> in question is either the Egyptian provincial of 11.8 1 or the Syrian large <i>modius</i> of 17.5 l. The Yerushalmi <i>quartarius</i> is the Roman measure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 17:11" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.17.11">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 17:11</a>) of 133.2 cm<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup>, whereas 1½ Babli eggs, which according to Rav Nissim (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 82b" href="/Eruvin.82b"><i>ad</i> 82b</a>) define the Gaonic <i>quartarius</i>, are either 82 or 121 cm<sup>3</sup>.</i>, the other makes a loaf of eight, and you are saying so? Rav Huna said, deduct a third for expenses<sup>41</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.2.1">Note 5</a>, this makes the difference between the loaf sizes only 1/9 <i>qab</i>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 8:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.8.5.4"><i>Peah</i> 8:6, Notes 77–78</a>.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun gave to the bakers according to the statement of Rav Huna, but only for these measures<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">For larger quantities, the price to be paid to miller and baker may be less and can be freely negotiated without being unfair to the baker.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If people of a courtyard and those on a balcony forgot and did not make an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In a two-story, multi-party building where the stairs are on the outside and a balcony is built along the entire length of the house by which one has access to the different apartments on the upper floor. If the dwellers on the upper floor made an <i>eruv</i> among themselves but not with the other dwellers in the same courtyard, they may carry on the common balcony but not in the courtyard.</i>, anything higher than ten hand-breadths belongs to the balcony, less than this to the courtyard. The enclosure of a cistern or a rock<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Shabbat 11:2" href="/Mishnah_Shabbat.11.2">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 11:2, Note 8</a>.</i> ten hand-breadths high belong to the balcony, less than this to the courtyard. When has this been said? If it is close. But if it is far away even if it is ten hand-breadths high it belongs to the courtyard. What is close? Any which is not four hand-breadths distant.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>. Is it not of the lower ones<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Why should the top of a rock standing in the courtyard belong to the balcony’s domain while being in the courtyard which is the ground floor’s domain?</i>? Rebbi Yudan said, this implies that if Ruben’s ruin stands in Simeon’s domain and the latter uses it, it is as if it were his<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">For the laws of the Sabbath, not property rights are essential but right of undisturbed use and easy accessibility. It is presumed that Ruben does not live in the courtyard and does not use the space on the Sabbath.</i>.
The following is obvious. If both of them are by a door<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph has a parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 83b" href="/Eruvin.83b">Babli, 83b</a>. Between two courtyards where the inhabitants did not make a common <i>eruv</i> there is space easily accessible by doors from each side.</i>, both of them are forbidden the house (in which are) [between]<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The text reads בּוֹ “in it”; one should read בֵּין “in between”.</i> two courtyards. For one of them by throwing and for one by lowering<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">There are no doors, only solid walls. One side has access only by throwing things over the wall; the other side has easy access to the top of its wall and may lower things into the intermediate space without exertion.</i>. Samuel says, lowering is permitted since it is ordinary but throwing is forbidden since it is not ordinary. Rav said, it did not do anything<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Both sides are equally forbidden.</i>. Our Mishnah disagrees with Rav: “anything higher than ten hand-breadths belongs to the balcony, less than this to the courtyard.” The Mishnah refers to the case that neither one uses by throwing nor by lowering; within three hand-breadths of the upper one which is more than ten hand-breadths from the lower one<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the Mishnah there is ease of use for the dwellers on the balcony but difficulty for the occupants of the courtyard. But if the top of the hill is more than 3 hand-breadths from the balcony the entire hill belongs to the courtyard even if access is easier from the top than from the bottom.</i>. Where do Rav and Samuel disagree?
