If it is equal<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In the dispute between Samuel and Rav there is only difficult access; Rav follows the reasoning given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.3.2">Note 46</a>, Samuel considers difficult access as easy if it is of the kind frequently used on weekdays.</i>.
If there was a ruin there, Rebbi Joḥanan says, one gives the ruin to its owner<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the owner’s access is more difficult than that of the other side.</i>. That is in case he uses the ruin through his door. If he uses the ruin through a window? Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, even in this case one gives the ruin to its owner<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the owner’s access is more difficult than that of the other side.</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar (ben Rebbi Simeon)<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parenthesis has to be deleted with the quote by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 84b" href="/Eruvin.84b">Rashba (<i>Eruvin</i> <i>ad</i> 84b, col. 528</a>). The tradent is R. Eleazar ben Pedat, one generation younger than Rav Hoshaia and three than R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon.<br><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 85a" href="/Eruvin.85a">Babli 85a</a> in the name of Rav.</i> in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: If there were three; each one uses a ruin through his door and the middle [space] is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Parallel to the situation described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.3.3">Note 52</a> there are two ruins, one adjacent to each courtyard, and an empty space in between. Since each courtyard made an <i>eruv</i> by itself, the ruins belong to the corresponding courtyards.</i>. When is this? If the middle [space] belonged to both of them. But if the middle [space] belonged to a person from the market, one uses one [property] and the other one uses one [property] and a second one<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">If the middle space belongs to an absent landowner who does not use it, the space is allocated to the person who actually uses it during the week; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.3.2">Note 46</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 11:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.11.2.3"><i>Šabbat</i> 11, Notes 59–63</a>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, what is standing and the hollow combine together to four, but only if what is standing is more than the hollow<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">There is no hill but a cistern built above ground. If the cistern is covered it belongs to the courtyard since the dwellers on the balcony have to descend to the ground to remove the cover. But if there is a hole through which a pail can be lowered to draw water it belongs to the balcony if the other conditions are satisfied.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira asked, only if what is standing on each side is more? Rebbi Yose said, it is obvious for Rebbi Zeˋira that what is standing on one side only does not combine; it is obvious for him that one side must be more. He only questions even the second standing part.
<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The same text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:6:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.6.6">Chapter 1, Note 224</a>.</i> Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: the Mishnah is from three to four [hand-breadths]. Less than three even more than ten [cubits] is permitted, four even less than ten [cubits] is prohibited. Where do we hold? From three to four<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">If the hill is within three hand-breadths of the balcony, it belongs to the balcony even if it is by ten hand-breadths higher than the balcony. If it is farther than 4 hand-breadths, it belongs to the courtyard irrespective of height since it can not be accessed directly from the balcony. The conditions of the Mishnah only apply if the distance from the balcony is between 3 and 4 hand-breadths.</i>.
If there is no cover; but if there is a cover it belongs to those on the ground floor. Rebbi []<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The name of an Amora is missing.</i> said, is there is no hole there. But if there is a hole one uses it through the hole<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">There is no hill but a cistern built above ground. If the cistern is covered it belongs to the courtyard since the dwellers on the balcony have to descend to the ground to remove the cover. But if there is a hole through which a pail can be lowered to draw water it belongs to the balcony if the other conditions are satisfied.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one deposits his <i>eruv</i> in a porter’s lodge<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">A small room for the guard at the entrance to the courtyard. This and the following are not places built as dwellings.</i>, a porch<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἐξέδρα, a covered walkway without walls.</i>, or a balcony, it is no <i>eruv</i> and any person who might dwell there does not forbid him. In a storage room for straw, in a cowshed, in a storage room for wood, or a general storage area, it is an <i>eruv</i> and any person who might dwell there does forbid him<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since these buildings are enclosed structures they may occasionally be rented out as apartments; therefore they are acceptable places for depositing an <i>eruv</i> of courtyards. People occupying these premises are required to contribute to the <i>eruv</i> which otherwise would be incomplete and invalid.</i>. Rebbi Jehusah says, if the householder retains rights of use he does not forbid him<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">If the owner reserved for himself the right to occasionally use the building which he rented out as a dwelling, it remains his space and is included in his contribution to the <i>eruv</i>; the renter is not obligated to contribute.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> New paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>. Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Read: “Rav”.</i> Jehudah in the name of Rav: This you only say referring to a porter’s lodge of a single family dwelling. But for the porter’s lodge of the many it is an <i>eruv</i> and he who dwells there forbids for you<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The moment that the porter’s lodge has to be occupied at all times it is to be assumed that the porter lives there; this turns the porter’s lodge into an apartment which has to participate in the <i>eruv</i>.</i>.
The House of Rebbi Yannai are saying, even a peg to hang his shoes on<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph discusses the statement of Rebbi Jehudah that the dweller in a rented apartment can be disregarded in matters of <i>eruv</i> if the landlord has the right to enter at all times. It is enough that a peg be reserved for the landlord’s shoes.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Ḥinena said, even a metal plate<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">With R. Ḥananel <i>ad</i> 85b read עֲשָׁשִׁית.</i>, even a table. Rav said, but only what may be moved on the Sabbath. Rebbi Abba said, it happened that a person had a chicken coop inside another person’s house, where he could enter without asking for permission. The case came before Rav who said, since he is obligated to give them water it is as if it could be moved on the Sabbath. Rebbi Jacob in the name of Samuel: The students of Rebbi Joḥanan ascended to Akhbara and relied on the statement of Rebbi Yannai. Ḥizqiah does not say so but: Rebbi Ḥiyya, Rebbi Assi, and Rebbi Ammi<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Babylonian spelling for the Babylonians R. Ḥiyya bar Abba, R. Yasa, and R. Immi, students of R. Joḥanan.</i> ascended to Akhbara and heard from the House of Rebbi Yannai that practice follows Rebbi Jehudah.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody leaves his house to stay in an other town for the Sabbath, whether he be Gentile of Jewish, he forbids<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The Jew unless he contributes to the <i>eruv</i>; the Gentile unless permission was bought from him.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah says, a Gentile forbids but a Jew does not forbid since it is not Jewish to come on the Sabbath.<br>Rebbi Simeon says, even if he left his house and went to keep the Sabbath at his daughter’s in the same town he does not forbid since already he does not think of it any more.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.5.1">Mishnaiot 5–6</a>.</i>. This is Rebbi Meïr’s, since Rebbi Meïr says, a locked house forbids<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Any house in the courtyard must contribute (or at least receive its share by a transfer of ownership) even if it is temporarily unoccupied.</i>. Could he not come by an <i>eruv</i>, or transgress and come<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">This contradicts R. Yose’s statement, that a Jew will not come to his house if he started the Sabbath at another place.</i>? Mar Uqban in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi (Simeon) [Meïr]<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe wrote “Simeon” (as stated in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 86a" href="/Eruvin.86a">Babli, 86a</a>); the corrector changed it to “Meïr”. The correction makes sense; if practice followed R. Simeon it would not have to be mentioned since it already was stated that practice about <i>eruvin</i> in general follows the most lenient opinion (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:10:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.10.4">Chapter 7, Note 110</a>). S. Liebermann conjectures that the first sentence of the next paragraph still belongs to this Mishnah: “Mar Uqban in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Simeon; the words of the rabbis disagree” (i. e., practice follows R. Meïr.)</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> From a cistern between two courtyards one may not draw water on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Unless the two courtyards made a common <i>eruv</i>.</i> unless one made for it a partition ten hand-breadths high, either below or inside its rim<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Either in the water or at least inside the rim of the cistern even if it does not touch the water.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, the House of Shammai say below but the House of Hillel say above<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">It even may be high above the rim of the cistern as long as its projection would split the water into two parts.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, a partition should not be more than the wall in between them<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The walls are enough of a separation to let each party draw water on its side without additional partition.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.6.1">Mishnah 7</a>.</i>. The words of the rabbis disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe wrote “Simeon” (as stated in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 86a" href="/Eruvin.86a">Babli, 86a</a>); the corrector changed it to “Meïr”. The correction makes sense; if practice followed R. Simeon it would not have to be mentioned since it already was stated that practice about <i>eruvin</i> in general follows the most lenient opinion (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:10:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.10.4">Chapter 7, Note 110</a>). S. Liebermann conjectures that the first sentence of the next paragraph still belongs to this Mishnah: “Mar Uqban in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Simeon; the words of the rabbis disagree” (i. e., practice follows R. Meïr.)</i>, as Rebbi Jacob<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Rashba (<i>ad</i> 86b, col. 533) reads: R. Jacob bar Idi. Instead of “to lower a pail” he reads: “the full size of a pail.”</i> said in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Only if the partition is lowered into the water to lower a pail<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since water is always moving, it is impossible to know which molecules will be where at a given time. The answer is that this is irrelevant; at the time the pail is lowered it will draw water only from the side of the cistern allocated to its part. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 86b" href="/Eruvin.86b">Babli 86b</a>.</i>. Do not two different domains use the same domain? They supposed to say that no pail moves more than four hand-breadths. Rebbi Tevelai in the name of Rav: The rules of a ruin do not apply to water<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The restrictions imposed in the earlier Mishnaiot in this Chapter do not apply to water. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 86b" href="/Eruvin.86b">Babli 86b</a>.</i>. If there was a ceiling, one considers it as if it went down and locked<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">If part of the roof of the houses of one courtyard extends above the cistern it defines the domain allotted to this courtyard.</i>. If there was a cross-beam<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Greek, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.26">Chapter 1, Note 41</a>.</i>, one considers it as if it went down and locked.
The argument of Rebbi Jehudah is inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.5.4.1">Mishnah <i>Beṣah</i> 5:4</a>. If a woman borrows baking ingredients on a holiday from another with a different <i>eruv</i> <i>teḥumin</i>, the resulting cake may be moved only in the domain common to both women. R. Jehudah instructs to disregard this rule concerning water. Why here does he require the existence of a wall? No answer is given. In the quote by R. Ḥananel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 87a" href="/Eruvin.87a"><i>ad</i> 87a</a>), this paragraph is missing and the statement of R. Huna refers directly to that of R. Tevelai.</i> he said, “Rebbi Jehudah does not apply this to water since it has no consistency.”
Rebbi Huna said, but the partition must extend into the hollow of the cistern<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Even the House of Hillel require that a hanging partition has to extend into the hollow of the cistern.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> From a water canal<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Ten hand-breadths deep and four wide. In general this is a separate <i>karmelit</i> and not part of the courtyard.</i> which crosses a courtyard one does not draw on the Sabbath unless one made a partition ten hand-breadths high at its entrance and exit. Rebbi Jehudah said, it happened that from the water canal of Abel one was drawing water<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Without separate partitions for every courtyard the canal is crossing.</i> on the instruction of Elders on the Sabbath. They told him, because it did not have the measure<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Not 10 hand-breadths deep and 4 wide.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.7.1">Mishnah 8</a>.</i>. The Mishnah if it is deep ten [hand-breadths] and wide four [hand-breadths]<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Ten hand-breadths deep and four wide. In general this is a separate <i>karmelit</i> and not part of the courtyard.</i> and open on both sides. If it is open on one side one puts a beam or a lathe on that side<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">As S. Liebermann points out, this cannot describe the situation of the Mishnah that a water canal runs through a private domain since beam and lathe are appropriate only for alleys. Therefore one has to assume that the canal at Abel was running in the alleys and the problem is to let the dwellers in an alley draw water on the Sabbath.</i>. If it is open on both sides one puts a beam or a lathe on one side and makes the shape of a door at the other. Do not two different domains use the same domain<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">If the canal is wide and deep enough to constitute a <i>karmelit</i> domain in itself it would be impossible to make a common <i>eruv</i> for both sides of the street. This in itself should prevent anybody from drawing water on the Sabbath.</i>? Explain it if there were houses on one side<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The objection is well taken. One has to assume that there were houses only on one side; the other side was bounded either by a solid wall or by uninhabited areas.</i>.
The following is obvious: If it is ten deep but not four wide one may move and fill<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">From a canal running through an alley. Any ditch which is not 4 hand-breadths wide at the surface does not interfere with <i>eruvin</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.3.1">Mishnah 7:3</a>).</i>. If it was four wide<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">This is implied by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.3.1">Mishnah 7:3</a>.</i> but not ten deep it is obvious that one may move. May one fill? Rebbi Ḥinena said, it is permitted. Rebbi Mana said, it is forbidden. Our Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Mana: “They told him, because it did not have the measure.” It was neither ten deep nor four wide<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 87a" href="/Eruvin.87a">Babli 87a</a>.</i>. But if it had been ten deep but not four wide<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">It is obvious that one has to read: if it would <i>not</i> have been ten deep but four wide.</i> it would have been forbidden. Even if it is four wide but not ten deep it is permitted. The case happened as it happened<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">No logical derivations are possible for rules derived from actual happenings since there the conditions are not subject to adjustment.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> From a balcony above the sea one may not draw water on the Sabbath unless one made a partition ten hand-breadths high either above or below<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The house is on a lakeshore, the balcony is built over the lake, and there is a hole in the balcony through which a pail may be lowered to draw water. While obviously water is always moving and it cannot be asserted that water drops found under the balcony on the Sabbath were there at sundown, since the restriction is purely rabbinic it is enough that under the balcony one make a symbolic wall whose extension would enclose the water.</i>. Similarly, two balconies one on top of the other<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Over the same water, with the holes in the balconies one directly on top of the other.</i>, if they made for the upper one but not for the lower one both are forbidden until they make an <i>eruv</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.8.1">Mishnah 9</a>.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira, Rav Jehudah in the name of Rav: Only if the partition is lowered into the water to lower a pail<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since water is always moving, it is impossible to know which molecules will be where at a given time. The answer is that this is irrelevant; at the time the pail is lowered it will draw water only from the side of the cistern allocated to its part. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 86b" href="/Eruvin.86b">Babli 86b</a>.</i>. Do not two different domains use the same domain<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second case of the Mishnah, that two balconies are built over the sea, one on top of the other (S. Liebermann). Since the Mishnah states that they did not make a common <i>eruv</i>, how can the upper floor draw water through the domain of the lower balcony?</i>? Rav Huna said, only if the partition be within three hand-breadths of the balcony<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The upper balcony can draw water only if it is wider than the lower one and the pail is lowered outside the lower balcony, more than 3 hand-breadths removed from it. Then the water is drawn in a domain unique to the upper balcony.</i>.
It was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">A different version is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 97b" href="/Eruvin.97b">Babli, 97b</a>. One should not explain the Yerushalmi on the basis of the different Babli version.</i> Rebbi Ḥanania ben Aqabia says, if a balcony protrudes over the sea, one opens the floor covering, lowers down, and fills. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Yasa: Only if the hole is four hand-breadths long, and only if it is four hand-breadths wide, and if the walls are ten hand-breadths high<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">If the enclosure of the balcony is 10 hand-breadths high it may serve as the required partition for the House of Hillel; then a hole up to 4-by-4 hand-breadths large can be made in the floor of the balcony to draw water.</i>. There<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia.</i> they are saying, even if the partitions are not ten hand-breadths high it is made like a funnel<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The walls of the balcony together with the floor form an oversized funnel which is made to connect with the water below. This is enough of a distinction not to require walls ten cubits high. (The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 16a" href="/Eruvin.16a">Babli 16a</a>/b which requires the balcony to be a hammock is difficult to understand.)</i>. Rebbi Idi said before Rebbi Ḥiyya: One is permitted to fill but forbidden to pour out<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">With a different interpretation, as Babylonian rule, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 88a" href="/Eruvin.88a">Babli 88a</a>.</i>. He said to him, when we will ordain Sages, we shall ordain you with them<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">This must be meant ironically since R. Idi already was ordained. The question is, is it ironic since the statement is obvious or is it a criticism because the statement is false?</i>. Rebbi Mana said, he shouted<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic مجهر “shouting”. In a parallel expression <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 8:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.8.6.2"><i>Yebamot</i> 8:6 (Note 230</a>), R. Mana uses מְגָחֵךְ “made fun of”. This may be the intended word here also.</i> at him. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, did he not say to him that one is permitted to fill, since he fills inside his partition, but forbidden to pour out, for he pours outside of his partition<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">It certainly is rabbinically forbidden to pour water from a private domain directly into a <i>karmelit</i>, such as the sea. As noted in the sequel, the sea intended is a lake.</i>?
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, Rebbi Ḥanania ben Aqabia said it only for Lake Genezareth since mountains surround it<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 87b" href="/Eruvin.87b">Babli 87b</a>, but there the reason given is that the shore of the lake is everywhere private domain with no public access to the water except harbors which also are private installations for the rules of the Sabbath. The statement here, that a valley surrounded by mountains is never public domain, is not found elsewhere.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar asked Rebbi Joḥanan, if one threw from a circular road<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">This word appears in many forms is the Yerushalmi: <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 9:1:31" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.9.1.31"><i>Berakhot</i> 9:2 (Note 81</a>) פווסרוס ,פופסדס, <i>Terumot</i> 8:10 ,אפיפסרוס אפיפסרון (but this may be another word), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 7:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.7.2.2"><i>Šeqalim</i> 7:2</a> פיוסרוס. S. Liebermann identifies the word as πρόσοδος, ἡ, “access road”. This assumes that Roman roads in the East where built in the style of the great straight roads in Italy which passed by the walled cities, which had to be accessed by smaller roads branching off from the main road. But these access roads are not less frequented nor more difficult to use than the main road. E. Guggenheimer (<i>Berakhot loc. cit</i>.) reads περίοδος, ἡ, “way around, circuit, circular path”.</i> into public domain or from public domain into it<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The question of R. Eleazar is whether a circuitous road, of difficult use but public property and open for use by the public, nevertheless should be considered private property for the rules of the Sabbath.</i>? He said to him, in your opinion a public domain does not exist. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, there is no public domain unless it be open from one end of the world to the other. The argument of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish seems inverted<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">His first statement seems to imply that somewhere in the world there exists public domain.</i> since he said, there is no public domain in this world, only in the future as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 40:4" href="/Isaiah.40.4"><i>Is</i>. 40:4</a>.</i>, <i>every valley will be lifted</i>. A Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Taharot</i> 6:6.</i> disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: “What is a private domain? The paths of Bet-Gilgul<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">In the definition of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 22b" href="/Eruvin.22b">Babli (22b</a>), any road on which the carrier of a load cannot run; according to Rashi because of an incline, according to Maimonides because of obstacles in the road.</i>. Private domain regarding the Sabbat<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Not really private domain but <i>karmelit</i>.</i> but public domain for impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">A public domain for the rules of impurity, where cases of doubt may be disregarded, is any place accessible to the public, even if it is private property.</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel text is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.10.2"><i>Sukkah</i> 1:9</a>. Since the reference to the rules of <i>Sukkah</i> is formulated there differently from here, the texts in their second parts are different versions, rather than straight copies.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Yose said it only for Tabernacles<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.10.1">Mishnah <i>Sukkah</i> 1:10</a>. The essence of a tabernacle is a temporary thatched roof, but it also needs at least three walls. According to the majority opinion the walls must start no more than 3 hand-breadths from the ground; R. Yose admits walls 10 hand-breadths wide hanging from the top.</i>, but in the matter of the Sabbath even Rebbi Yose agrees. The word of Rebbi Ḥanina implies that it is even for the Sabbath, as Rebbi Ḥanina said, a ruler came to Sepphoris and they suspended quilts<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Sheets sewn together from rectangular pieces of cloth.</i>; Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose permitted carrying under them following his father’s argument. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac: Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina comes like Rebbi Ḥanania and both disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan’s argument, as Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously one has to read: R. Yose ben Ḥanina.</i> said, Rebbi Jehudah, and Rebbi Yose, and Ḥanania bar Aqabia all three said the same. Rebbi Jehudah of the open bridges<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:1:2-5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.1.2-5.3">Mishnah 9:5</a>, where R. Jehudah defines the space under a bridge as private domain if the underpinning of the bridge structure extends 10 hand-breadths below the roadway.</i>, Rebbi Yose that of tabernacles, Rebbi Ḥanania ben Aqabia as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 3:3:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.3.3.7"><i>Šabbat</i> 3, Notes 101–103</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 87b" href="/Eruvin.87b">Babli 87b</a>.</i>: Rebbi Ḥanania permitted three things, he permitted a balcony, and seaweed<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Reading עצה with the text in all parallel sources.</i>, and bringing bathtowels<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 22:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.22.5.1"><i>Šabbat</i> 22:5, Note 21</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">There exists a complete text of this difficult passage in the commentary of R. Ḥananel of Kairawan to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 88a" href="/Eruvin.88a">Babli, 88a</a>. While this text obviously was written from memory and is influenced by Babylonian spelling, and the reproduction of the text in the Wilna Babli may give rise to some questions, it is a testimony predating the Leiden ms. by more than 250 years and shows that the emendations proposed by the standard commentaries have to be rejected summarily.</i> Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The version of R. Ḥananel, “R. Abba and R. Yose”, Amoraim of the last generation, is preferable to the Leiden text which quotes the third generation Amora R. Abbahu in the name of the fourth generation Tanna R. Yose (ben Ḥalafta), unless one wants to read “R. Yasa” in place of “R. Yose.”</i>: This<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of the Mishnah that if two balconies one above the other and both above the sea without <i>eruv</i> may not draw water.</i> you are saying if they are using the hole. But if they are using the outside it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">He seems to disagree with Rav Huna (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.8.2">Note 92</a>) that for the laws of the Sabbath the balcony extends 3 hand-breadths beyond its actual width but allows the upper balcony to draw water through an appropriate hole if it extends in any way further than the lower balcony.</i>. It was stated thus: If three balconies are one above the other<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Without common <i>eruv</i>.</i> it is forbidden to pass from the upper one to the lower one passing by the middle one, but one may pass from the courtyard to the roof without worry<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">In a multi-story house with a high atrium and several occupants without an <i>eruv</i> one may not lower anything from the top to the bottom through the airspace belonging to third parties. But outside the house from roof to courtyard there is no problem even if the house has more than one floor and no <i>eruv</i> was made inside.</i>. Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac says, this<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of the Mishnah (Note 120).</i> you mean from the inside but from the outside it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Even with an <i>eruv</i>. If one refers the statement of R. Samuel bar Rav Isaac to R. Abbahu’s statement, one would have to read “permitted”. Since R. Ḥananel’s testimony makes emendation impossible, the statement has to refer even to a single balcony.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira said, both inside and outside are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of two balconies, inside use is forbidden without an <i>eruv</i> and outside in all cases,</i>. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Zeˋira<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 8:4" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).8.4">Tosephta 8:4</a>.</i>: “One lowers a pot with meat from a ledge which is ten [hand-breadths] high and four wide<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">This defines a domain by itself but since it is not inhabitable it does not require an <i>eruv</i>. Since the ledge is outside the house, it contradicts both R. Samuel bar Rav Isaac and R. Zeˋira.</i>. If a window of four hand-breadths was in between<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">A window 4-by-4 wide and 4 deep is a domain by itself; the dish cannot be lowered through such a window.</i> it is forbidden since one does not pass from one domain to another through a [third] domain.” What does Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac do with this? He explains it (if captive) [if equal]<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.9.1">Mishnah 10</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a courtyard be less that four [by four] cubits one may not pour water in it on the Sabbath unless one make for it a sump containing two <i>seah</i> below the hole<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Since the space is so small one has to assume that water poured into the courtyard will run off into the public domain. Then pouring the water may look as if one were pouring from a private into the public domain, which would look like a Sabbath violation. Therefore one requires that there be a sink absorbing 2 <i>se’ah</i> (about 25 l) of the run-off. If later there is an overflow from this sink it is not directly the result of pouring out water and therefore one may pour out water in unlimited amounts.</i>, whether inside or outside; only that outside one has to cover but inside one does not have to cover<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This makes the sink an extension of the courtyard; it no longer is counted as public domain.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, one may pour water on the Sabbath into a sewer which is covered for four cubits in the public domain, but the Sages are saying, even from a roof or a courtyard 100 cubits [wide] one may not pour into the sewer<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since it looks like pouring into the public domain.</i> but one may pour onto the roof and it flows down into the sewer. Courtyard and porch combine for four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">If the sum of the areas is at least 16 square cubits nothing has to be excavated.</i>.<br>Two rows of apartments<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δίαιτα, ἡ. The two rows of houses share a common courtyard but no <i>eruv</i>.</i>, one opposite the other, one side made a sump, the other did not make a sump. Those who made a sump are permitted, those who did not make a sump are forbidden.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.9.1">Mishnah 10</a>.</i>. They estimated (the place of)<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Probably the word מקרם should be deleted.</i> what a person uses is two <i>se’ah</i> and four cubits absorb two <i>se’ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">A person on one day uses no more than 2 <i>se’ah</i> of water and an unpaved area of 16 square cubits can absorb 2 <i>se’ah</i> per day. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 88" href="/Eruvin.88">Babli 88a/b</a>.</i>. If the sump sprung a hole<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">And then any additional water which is poured into the yard will immediately cause water to flow into the public domain.</i>, there are Tannaïm who state that one does not have to plug; there are Tannaïm who state that one has to plug. If the sump was full, there are Tannaïm who state that one has to empty, there are Tannaïm who state that one does not have to. Rebbi Mana said, it is one Tanna. He who says that one has to plug forbids to pour out<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">He reads לשפוך as “to pour into the yard”. If it is necessary to plug the hole so that no water may flow from it into the public domain, it will be forbidden to pour anything into the yard for the rest of the day.</i>, he who says that one does not have to plug permits to pour out. Rehhi Ḥiyya bar Marius said to Rebbi Mana: So said your father Rebbi Jonah, thinking about it<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Reading בָּהּ for בר “outside”.</i>: If it is sloping<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Greek καταφερής, -ές.</i> inside, even more than ten is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously this has to read “forbidden”. If the yard is on an incline, any water spilled there will immediately flow into the public domain. But if the outside is sloping into the courtyard, the water will remain in the yard.</i>; outside even less than ten is permitted. What we are dealing with if it is level.
The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “Courtyard and porch combine for four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">If the sum of the areas is at least 16 square cubits nothing has to be excavated.</i>.” He explains it if they are level. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: The terrace, the roof, and the courtyard combine for four cubits. Still it is if they are level. You should know that it is so since it was stated: The house, the upper floor, the courtyard, and the porch do not combine. Is that not because they are not level<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Since the disagreement between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish is not indicated, it is difficult to assess the meaning of the discussion. An educated guess (by <i>Qorban haEdah</i>) is that R. Joḥanan holds that domains can be added only if they are on equal heights; the questioner assumed that the porch was higher than the courtyard. In the <i>baraita</i> quoted as opposing R. Simeon ben Laqish it is clear that courtyard and rooftop cannot be of equal height. It is conjectured that he holds that the list of the Mishnah, yard and porch, is exhaustive; this would exclude terrace and rooftop. The answer is that he accepts yard and everything on the same level, porch and everything on the same level. In this interpretation the <i>baraita</i> does not allow one to add the surface areas of courtyard and rooftop.</i>?
Rebbi Jeremiah said, Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob both said the same. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob as we have stated: “Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, on the Sabbath one may pour water into a sewer which is covered for four cubits in the public domain, but the Sages are saying, even from a roof or a courtyard 100 cubits [wide] one may not pour into the sewer.” Rebbi Meïr as it was stated: In large cities one may pour water into flows even though they are perforated, the words of Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">If a river is diverted into many small currents which run in streets and through courtyards (as can be seen, e. g., in Freiburg i. B., Germany), one may pour water into them on the Sabbath since it will not be noticed.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">The main discussion about prohibitions added because people may get a bad impression, which also includes the text here, is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.1.9"><i>Kilaim</i> 9:2 (Notes 34–40</a>, <i>Eruvin</i> problem Note 39). Rav’s opinion is accepted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 64b" href="/Shabbat.64b">Babli (<i>Šabbat</i> 64b</a>, 164b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 9a" href="/Beitzah.9a"><i>Beṣah</i> 9a</a>, ˋ<i>Avodah zarah</i> 12a) but consistently rejected in the Yerushalmi.</i> And it was stated thus, if it was a drainpipe it is permitted; in the rainy season it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 88b" href="/Eruvin.88b">Babli 88b</a>. The Babli holds that during the rainy season there never was any rabbinic prohibition because everything is being wet anyhow.</i>; streaming spouts are forbidden and Bar Qappara stated, if it occurred at a hidden place it is permitted. This disagrees with Rav and he cannot explain it, since Rav said that everything forbidden because of a bad impression is forbidden even in the most private room.

Chapter 9



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> All rooftops of a town form one domain on condition that no roof be ten [hand-breadths] higher or ten lower, the words of Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">All vessels which were on any rooftop at nightfall Friday evening may be transported from one roof to any other without an <i>eruv</i> and even if the inhabitants of the different houses made <i>eruvin</i> for themselves. But if the level of roofs differs by at least 10 hand-breadths, each level constitutes a domain by itself and one may not carry from one to the other without an appropriate <i>eruv</i>.</i>, but the Sages say that each roof is a domain by itself. Rebbi Simeon says, not only rooftops but also courtyards and corrals<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.3.1">Chapter 2, Note 6</a>.</i> are single domains for vessels which started the Sabbath in them but not for vessels which started the Sabbath in the house<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Without an <i>eruv</i> one may carry any vessel of the courtyard from one place to any other in the yard; only moving vessels from a house to the yard or vice versa is forbidden.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “All rooftops of a town form one domain,” etc. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rav and Samuel disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since rooftops are not built as dwellings, their status is comparable to that of corrals, subject to the same restrictions; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 5:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.5.8.4">Chapter 5, Note 167</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 89a" href="/Eruvin.89a">Babli 89a</a>.</i>. Samuel said, up to two bet <i>se’ah</i>. Rav said, one carries there even a <i>kor</i>, even two <i>kor</i>. They wanted to say, where do Rebbi Meïr and the rabbis disagree? If they made an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If the houses belonged to different owners and they made an <i>eruv</i> for the whole walled town, in the majority opinion the <i>eruv</i> does not extend to the rooftops which are not intended as dwellings.</i>. But if they did not have to make an <i>eruv</i>, he explains it if all [houses] were the property of one person<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since in a corral one may carry only if it is not more than 2 <i>bet se’ah</i>, the same applies to rooftops. The rabbis may hold that roofs of different houses are different domains even if all belong to one and the same person.</i>. Our Mishnah if all of them were climbing up either on a Tyrian or an Egyptian ladder<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">An Egyptian ladder is small and movable; a Tyrian is large and fixed.</i>. But if all were climbing up using an Egyptian ladder except for one using a Tyrian, the Tyrian becomes a door<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Using a Tyrian ladder really is using a staircase; using an Egyptian is inconvenient exercising. Since the owner of the Tyrian ladder has easy access, he is the legitimate user of all rooftops, to the exclusion of the others who may walk there but not carry (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.3.2">Chapter 8, Note 46</a>).</i> and the remaining ones are as if climbing up and climbing down.
May one carry in all of it<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This question is asked for the rabbis who oppose R. Meïr. Since each rooftop is a separate domain it is a question whether it has the status of a backyard of a single house where one may carry without restriction. A backyard, or a multiple dwelling courtyard, is bounded by walls but the borders between flat roofs of contiguous houses may not be easily seen. Does one say that the walls between houses may ideally be extended over the roofs?</i>? Samuel said, he carries in all of it. Rav said, he only carries within four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 90a" href="/Eruvin.90a">Babli, 90a</a>, interchanges the names.</i>. Our Mishnah disagrees with Rav: “If a large roof is adjacent to a small one, the large one is permitted but the small forbidden.” One understands that of the large one four cubits are permitted. But of the small one is forbidden four cubits? Is anything forbidden in four cubits? Do not Rav and Samuel say that one allows him four cubits in front of his door<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Not only may one carry distances less than 4 cubits in the public domain, one even may carry in a multi-family courtyard without <i>eruv</i> within 4 cubits of one’s door. There is absolutely no situation where one is not permitted to carry up to a distance of 4 cubits.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said in Samuel’s name: If there was a door open to the roof<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of S. Liebermann, if the house was built on a slope and the roof opened on a courtyard, the large roof forms one domain with the courtyard since on both sides where it is joined to the small one there is railing which counts as a wall, whereas the smaller roof is forbidden without an <i>eruv</i> since no wall indicates that it is separated from the large roof.</i>, even then the large one is permitted since it is not completely open, and the small one is forbidden because it is completely open.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 7:14" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).7.14">Tosephta 7:14</a>.</i> Rebbi Meïr says, do you not agree with me that vessels which started the Sabbath in a courtyard may be moved in the courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This is stated as commonly accepted rule in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:19" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.19">Tosephta 5:19</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:23" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.23">23</a>.</i>? What is the difference between a roof and a courtyard? They told him, no. If you say about a courtyard, which has no dwellers under it, what can you say about a roof, which has dwellers under it? He asked them, what if the courtyard was higher than the roof<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The house was built into a hillside and the courtyard is the extrension of the roof.</i>? They told him, no. If you say about a courtyard where nobody knows his place, what can you say about a roof where everybody knows his place? He asked them, what if the courtyard was split by pebbles<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">With the Tosephta read פסיפס Greek ψήφος, ὁ.</i>? Up to this point there was an answer<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Mishnah <i>Makhširin</i> 8. One would have expected the name of a Tanna stating the objections of R. Meïr’s students.</i>.” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, from here on they were answering him in person; they told him, no. If you say about a courtyard whose dividers rise about it, what can you say about a roof whose dividers do not rise about it? He was telling them, in fact I am equating depth and height.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:24" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.24">Tosephta 5:24</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 91a" href="/Eruvin.91a">Babli 91a</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah said, in times of persecution<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The Hadrianic persecutions when study of Torah was forbidden.</i> we were taking the scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">A Torah scroll.</i> and climbing from courtyard to roof, from a roof to another roof, and sitting down and reading. They told him, times of persecution are no proof<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since the restrictions are rabbinic, not biblical, they may be overridden by biblical obligations; this does not diminish their validity.</i>.” “<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 5:24" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).5.24">Tosephta 5:24</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 91a" href="/Eruvin.91a">Babli 91a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 147b" href="/Shabbat.147b"><i>Šabbat</i> 147b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 72a" href="/Menachot.72a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 72a</a>.</i> Rebbi said, when we were studying with Rebbi Simeon (in a valley) [in Tekoa]<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is the scribe’s; the text in brackets is the corrector’s following the consensus of the Babli texts. It is difficult to locate R. Simeon in Judea.</i>, we were taking oil and a bath towel<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>linteum</i>, -<i>i, n</i>..</i> and going from a courtyard to a roof, and from the roof to a corral, and from one corral to another corral, until we came to the corral adjacent to the spring, and descending and immersing ourselves in it.”
You may say that there are four disagreements. “All rooftops of a town form one domain.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meïr’s opinion in the Mishnah.</i>” Rebbi Jehudah says, courtyard and rooftops form one domain. “Rebbi Simeon says, courtyards, rooftops, and corrals are single domains for vessels which started the Sabbath in them but not for vessels which started the Sabbath in the house”<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The fourth opinion is that of the rabbis for whom each rooftop is a separate domain.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, if they did not make an <i>eruv</i>. But if they made an <i>eruv</i> the courtyard becomes like the houses<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah and R. Simeon cannot refer to the cases where each courtyard made an <i>eruv</i> for itself since they permit carrying from one to the other by way of the roofs. Since with an <i>eruv</i> one may bring vessels from the houses into the courtyard, they also would allow bringing vessels from one house to another in another courtyard via the roof. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 91a" href="/Eruvin.91a">Babli 91a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira said, it is the same whether they made an <i>eruv</i> or they did not make an <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since vessels from the house are explicitly excluded by R. Simeon, the situation considered by R. Joḥanan cannot occur.</i>. Our Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Zeˋira: “not for vessels which started the Sabbath in the house.” If they did not make an <i>eruv</i>, is this necessary? It is forbidden to remove from the roof, not so much to the roof<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since the vessels cannot be removed from house to courtyard, for RR. Jehudah and Simeon certainly they cannot be brought to any roof.</i>? Rebbi Abun bar Cahana said before Rebbi La: When each one made an <i>eruv</i> by itself; even then not for the vessels which started the Sabbath in the house<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Even with an <i>eruv</i> and it is possible to bring vessels into the courtyard and onto the roof, they cannot be moved to any other roof nor to another courtyard. R. Zeˋira is not contradicted by the Mishnah.</i>.
Paragraph. <sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">A slightly garbled restatement of the first paragraph in this Chapter. The spelling תרויהו is Babylonian Aramaic in place of Yerushalmi תריהון.</i> The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun: It follows that Samuel and Rav both disagree with the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan, as we have stated: “All rooftops of a town form one domain.” Samuel said, up to two bet <i>se’ah</i>. Rav said, one carries there even a <i>kor</i>, even two <i>kor</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a large roof is adjacent to a small one<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the Sages in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>. The situation is supposed to be the same as described in the second part of the Mishnah for courtyards: the larger extends beyond the smaller roof on both sides; if a person fromf the smaller house uses his roof to go to the larger roof, the smaller roof plays the role of entrance gate to the larger one. Therefore the dweller in the smaller house may use his own roof only if he made an <i>eruv</i> with the larger house.</i>, the large one is permitted but the small forbidden. If a large courtyard is open to a small one, the large one is permitted but the small forbidden since it acts as a door to the large one.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a large roof is adjacent to a small one<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the Sages in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>. The situation is supposed to be the same as described in the second part of the Mishnah for courtyards: the larger extends beyond the smaller roof on both sides; if a person fromf the smaller house uses his roof to go to the larger roof, the smaller roof plays the role of entrance gate to the larger one. Therefore the dweller in the smaller house may use his own roof only if he made an <i>eruv</i> with the larger house.</i>, the large one is permitted but the small forbidden.” Our Mishnah [refers to] one roof only, but on another roof it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Following the Sages in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>, even if the two roofs belong to the same owner, it only is permitted to carry from the large onto the small but not from the small onto the large (Chapter 2, Notes 48,91).</i>. If it is not level, but if it is level it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah may even be read as following R. Meïr, in case there is a step from the large roof down to the small.</i>. Our Mishnah disagrees with Samuel: “If a large courtyard is open to a small one, the large one is permitted but the small forbidden.” Should one not be able to move there up to the place of the partitions<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Why is a person living in the smaller house not permitted to use his roof if he keeps his distance from the large roof?</i>? The colleagues said before Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Aḥiyya: What Samuel said, on this Sabbath itself<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">It is not quite clear which of Samuel’s statements the discussion refers to. It seems that one reads in the Mishnah the expression נפרצה as “was torn down” not as a prior state but that it was torn down on the Sabbath and Samuel holds (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:8:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.8.9">Chapter 6, Notes 158ff</a>.) that what was permitted at the start of the Sabbath remains so for the entire Sabbath.</i>, what the Mishnah deals with the next Sabbath. Where do we hold? If they made one <i>eruv</i> together, they should be permitted both on this and on the next Sabbath. If they did not make any <i>eruv</i>, they should be forbidden both on this and on the next Sabbath. The case happened as it happened<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.7.3">Chapter 8, Note 89</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Cahana said before Rebbi La: When each of them made separate <i>eruvin</i>, nevertheless the large one is permitted since it is breached along its entire length and the small one is forbidden since it was not breached along its entire length<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">It seems clear that one has to switch the places of “breached” and “not breached.”</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a courtyard is open to the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">There is no entrance gate and anyone entering is not trespassing. For the opponents of R. Eliezer, the status of the yard is no different from shoulders of a public road (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 1:1:24" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.1.1.24"><i>Šabbat</i> 1, Note 73</a>); transporting from the yard to either the public road or a private house is only rabbinically forbidden.</i>, anybody who brings from it into a private domain or from a private domain into it is liable, the words of Rebbi Eliezer. But the Sages say from it into the public domain and from the public domain into it one is not liable since it is like <i>karmelit</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i>. Rebbi Zeriqa, Rebbi Jacob bar Abun in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: Rebbi Eleazar said this only for the place of partitions if the main parts had been removed on both sides<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In this opinion, R. Eleazar holds that a courtyard used as a passageway remains <i>karmelit</i> except for the two entrances where at the place of the missing walls a strip 4 hand-breadths wide each is added to the public domain.</i>, and only four [handbreadths]. Rebbi Zeˋira and Rebbi Illa both are saying, it is forbidden not at the place of partitions, but at the place of partitions even the rabbis agree<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In their opinion R. Eleazar declares the entire courtyard as public domain. The opinion attributed to R. Eleazar in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.3.2">Note 47</a> is attributed here to the rabbis who in addition permit the dwellers around the courtyard to make an <i>eruv</i>.</i>. Rebbi Zeriqan said, many times I explained [the Mishnah] with Rebbi Jacob bar Abun and never heard this from him<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">A similar expression of R. Zeriqan is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 3:1:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.3.1.13"><i>Šabbat</i> 3, after Note 57</a>.</i>. They said to him, does it not happen that one person hears something which another does not hear? Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: if the main partitions had been removed on both sides, and only if it is level<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The courtyard open on both sides becomes public domain only if it is level with the roads on both sides.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a courtyard became open on both sides<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Before the Sabbath it was a private domain. On the Sabbath it became open and now can be used as a shortcut by the public.</i>, or a house became open on both sides<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Before the Sabbath it was a private domain. On the Sabbath it became open and now can be used as a shortcut by the public.</i>, or an alley whose beam or lathes were removed, they are permitted the same Sabbath but forbidden in the future, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Yose says, if they are permitted the same Sabbath they are permitted in the future, and if they are forbidden in the future they are forbidden this Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore it is forbidden immediately on the first Sabbath.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>. Why do I need “on both sides”<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Before the Sabbath it was a private domain. On the Sabbath it became open and now can be used as a shortcut by the public.</i>? Even on one side! Does this not follow Rav, as Rav said, a courtyard becomes permitted by two planks<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Rav’s statement is recorded only in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 12a" href="/Eruvin.12a">Babli, 12a</a>: Rav Sheshet said in the name of Rav Jeremiah bar Abba who said in the name of Rav: The Sages agree with Rebbi Eliezer about planks of a courtyard (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.2.1">Mishnah 1:2</a>).</i>? Rebbi Samuel in the name of Rebbi Zeˋira: It is a disagreement. He who says there by one plank<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbis opposing Rebbi, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 12a" href="/Eruvin.12a">Babli 12a</a>.</i>, here also by one plank; he who says there by two planks, here also by two planks. If it became breached in the middle it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">If walls are standing at both sides of the breach, the breach is a door. Even if the breach is wider than 10 cubits, if the standing walls are at least 4 hand-breadths wide they act as planks.</i>; Samuel said it is forbidden. Rebbi Zeˋira said, if that of Samuel is not what he learned, it is difficult<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">If Samuel’s statement is an old Babylonian tradition, it has to be accepted as rabbinic decree. If it is presented as a new ruling, it seems to be illogical.</i>. If one entire side was breached? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, that is [the case of] the porch; if it were a porch, would it not be permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Since a porch has no walls, the rules of the courtyard cannot be more restrictive than those of a porch. This is R. Zeˋira’s objection to Samuel’s statement.</i>?
Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> where in all other references the name of the Tanna is Eleazar.</i> said this only for a courtyard and an alley; but is a house like a bridge<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">A house, even if it is a public building open to all and a passage through it may serve as a shortcut, is never treated as public domain.</i>?
Rav and Rebbi Joḥanan say, it is forbidden both on this Sabbath and the coming Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>, practice is decided following R. Yose; since it is forbidden in the future it must be forbidden now.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">A shortened version of a text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 3:4:2-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.3.4.2-12"><i>Ḥagigah</i> 3:4</a> (79c 1.22).</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, <i>costos</i>, and hollowed squash, and alley, and convert, and vulgar, are restrictive.
<i>Costos</i> as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oktzin 3:5" href="/Mishnah_Oktzin.3.5">Mishnah <i>Uqeṣin</i> 3:5</a>. תנן is Babylonian Aramaic. <i>Costos</i>,<i>-i, f</i>., Greek κόστος, an Oriental aromatic plant.</i>, “<i>costos</i>, and cardamon, and important spices<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Money dedicated as Second Tithe which may be spent only on pure food or drink in Jerusalem.</i> may be bought with tithe money<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah is stated as an argument, not as a statement, to permit in practice to accept each argument even though this results in two mutually contradictory restrictions.</i> but do not become impure by impurity of foodstuff, the words of Rebbi Aqiba. Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri said, if they may be bought with tithe money they become impure by impurity of foodstuff, and if they do not become impure by impurity of foodstuff they may not be bought with tithe money.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, restrictive<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 5:3" href="/Mishnah_Parah.5.3">Mishnah <i>Parah</i> 5:3</a>. “Sanctify” means to put some of the ashes of the Red Cow into the water to use it to purify from the impurity of the dead. This water has to be taken from flowing water (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:17" href="/Numbers.19.17"><i>Num</i>. 19:17</a>). Since the squash, used as a pot, will absorb of this water, immediately after it has been immersed in flowing water it might be used in the ceremony, but later the water retained in its walls will invalidate new water drawn by the hollowed squash. In the Mishnah, R. Joshua argues that if at the start the squash was acceptable it always should be acceptable, if later it is not acceptable neither should it be at the start (since the point in time when it becomes unacceptable is not well defined.)</i>: they become impure by impurity of foodstuff and may not be bought with tithe money.
Hollowed squash as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">This is here, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>, proof that the <i>Ḥagigah</i> text is original.</i>: “A hollowed squash which one immersed is water suitable for sanctification, etc.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, restrictive, one may not sanctify either at the beginning or at the end.
Alley, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">As wine prepared by a Non-Jew, cf. <i>Avodah zarah</i>, Chapters 2–5.</i>, “or an alley whose beam was removed, etc.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, restrictive, it is forbidden both on this Sabbath and the coming Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>, practice is decided following R. Yose; since it is forbidden in the future it must be forbidden now.</i>.
Proselyte, as it was stated: If a proselyte converted while he had wines, and said, it is clear to me that no libations were made from it, if he made it by himself it is pure for him but impure for others<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">A fellow is a person observing all rules of purity in the absence of a Temple, cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Demay</i>, pp. 349–350.</i>, by others it is impure both for him and for others. Rebbi Aqiba said, if they are pure for him they should be pure for others; if impure for others they should be impure for him. Rebbi Joḥanan said, restrictive, impure for him and for others. Vulgar, as it was stated: If a vulgar person who was inducted as a fellow<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:1:2-5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.1.2-5.3">Mishnah 5</a>.</i> has food made in purity and he said, it is clear to me that it was prepared in purity, if he made it by himself it is pure for him but impure for others, by others it is impure both for him and for others. Rebbi Aqiba said, if they are pure for him they should be pure for others; if impure for others they should be impure for him. Rebbi Joḥanan said, restrictive, impure for him and for others.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody builds an upper floor connecting two houses<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The houses are on two different sides of the public road; the construction creates a bridge between the sides. Since the outer walls of this bridge are at least 10 hand-breadths high, in R. Jehudah’s opinion they constitute acceptable partitions which turn the space between them into a private domain. In the opinion of the anonymous majority, no part of a public road may be made private.</i>, and similarly open bridges<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A bridge open to the public as part of a public road. It is assumed that the railings at the sides of the bridge are 10 hand-breadths high and can act as partitions between the space under the bridge and the outside.</i>, one carries under them, the words of Rebbi Jehudah, but the Sages forbid it<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since the formulation is “prohibited” but not “he is liable”, the argument of R. Jehudah is accepted in principle but rejected rabbinically.</i>. In addition, Rebbi Jehudah said that one may make an <i>eruv</i> for open alleys but the Sages forbid it<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If an alley is not a dead end it is a thoroughfare even if it cannot be used for vehicular traffic.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:1:2-5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.1.2-5.3">Mishnah 5</a>.</i>. Rav Huna said, no public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">For the rules of the Sabbath, not in other respects. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 98a" href="/Shabbat.98a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 98a</a>.</i> is roofed. Rebbi Simeon bar Carsana said, did you not learn from the desert, and was the desert not roofed<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">As the Babli <i>loc. cit</i>. explains, all rules of the Sabbath are derived from the Tabernacle in the desert, and the space under the carts used by the Levites to transport the disassembled Tabernacle was roofed public domain.</i>? Our Mishnah does not say so, but “and similarly open bridges, one carries under them the words of Rebbi Jehudah, but the Sages forbid it.” It only says “but the Sages forbid it;” therefore there is no liability of a purification offering<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since “forbidden”, in contrast to “liable”, in talmudic terminology means “rabbinically, not biblically, forbidden.”</i>.
Does it need a partition<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. Jehudah, is the space under a bridge automatically private domain? The roadway represents a roof; does one say that the borders of the roadway define imaginary walls (as stated in Chapter 1, Notes 40,42), or does the definition of private domain imply the existence of actual walls (which seems to be implied by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 16:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.16.2.4"><i>Šabbat</i> 16, Note 84</a>)?</i>? Rebbi Abba said, it needs a partition. Rebbi Yose said, it does not need a partition. The colleagues said before Rebbi Yose, does Rebbi Abba not say it correctly? As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.6.1">Mishnah 8:7</a>.</i>, “Rebbi Jehudah says, a partition should not be more than the wall between them.” He said to them, there<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the Mishnah here, where the roadway is a roof.</i>, where there is a roof, it does not need a partition. But here, where there is no roof, it needs a partition, as Rav Jehudah said, if it is open to an agricultural area<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">He notes that R. Jehudah considers the area under the bridge as automatic private domain only if that domain is bordered by <i>karmelit</i>; if it were bordered by public domain in the biblical sense, even R. Jehudah would require actual partitions (standing or hanging.)</i>, but if it is open to the public domain it is forbidden if there are no partitions, but if there is a partition it is permitted.

Chapter 10



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who finds <i>tefillin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Phylacteries, to be worn on weekdays, one on the arm and one on the head. Since they contain passages of Torah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:1-10" href="/Exodus.13.1-10"><i>Ex</i>. 13:1–10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 11-16" href="/Exodus.11-16">11–16</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:4-9" href="/Deuteronomy.6.4-9"><i>Deut</i>. 6:4–9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:13-21" href="/Deuteronomy.11.13-21">11:13–21</a>), they are holy and may not be left unprotected lying in the open on the road. Since the rule that phylacteries are <i>muqṣeh</i> and not to be worn on the Sabbath is rabbinic, in case of need the rabbinic restriction is waved; the <i>tefillin</i> may be worn as clothing; bringing them to a safe place in this way is a meritorious act, not a Sabbath desecration.</i> brings them in pair by pair; Rabban Gamliel says, two by two<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Even though on weekdays one never wears <i>tefillin</i> this way, since it is possible to wear two pairs simultaneously it is preferable to reduce the number of trips, i. e., the number of violations of rabbinic Sabbath restrictions.</i>. When has this been said? For used ones, but for new ones<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">They look like <i>tefillin</i> but in fact might be amulets for which no rules are waived.</i> he is not liable. If he found them in heaps or bundles<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Heaps are lightly tied together, bundles are tied pair by pair.</i> he stays there until it becomes dark and then brings them, but in times of danger<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">When the exercise of Jewish rites is prosecuted by the government.</i> he covers them and goes away.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> ““He who finds <i>tefillin,” etc</i>. “He who finds <i>tefillin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Phylacteries, to be worn on weekdays, one on the arm and one on the head. Since they contain passages of Torah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:1-10" href="/Exodus.13.1-10"><i>Ex</i>. 13:1–10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 11-16" href="/Exodus.11-16">11–16</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:4-9" href="/Deuteronomy.6.4-9"><i>Deut</i>. 6:4–9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:13-21" href="/Deuteronomy.11.13-21">11:13–21</a>), they are holy and may not be left unprotected lying in the open on the road. Since the rule that phylacteries are <i>muqṣeh</i> and not to be worn on the Sabbath is rabbinic, in case of need the rabbinic restriction is waved; the <i>tefillin</i> may be worn as clothing; bringing them to a safe place in this way is a meritorious act, not a Sabbath desecration.</i> brings them in pair by pair;” as clothing, one on his head and one on his arm. “Rabban Gamliel says, two by two<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Even though on weekdays one never wears <i>tefillin</i> this way, since it is possible to wear two pairs simultaneously it is preferable to reduce the number of trips, i. e., the number of violations of rabbinic Sabbath restrictions.</i>,” two on his head and two on his arm.
<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Most of this and the next paragraph are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:3:8-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.3.8-9"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:3, Notes 104–115</a>. A text closer to the formulation here is in <i>Pesiqta Rabbati</i> 22 (ed. Ish-Shalom p. 112b, Notes 47–54).</i> Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Pesiqta rabbati</i>: R. Joḥanan.</i>: He who puts on <i>tefillin</i> in the night transgresses a positive commandment.
